The reason Celeste is giving is actually the reason Charlotte might have, perhaps one that doesn't loom all that prominently in Celeste's mind. There's a conflict her that Celeste SHOULD be blinded to by her desire to provide, not thinking that Charlotte needs to be her own entity.

Emily:

Yep — both of those observations are sharp, and you’re right to flag them.

1) Present tense — was it by design?¶

In this thread I’ve been writing your scenes in present tense largely because the recent Wardrobe arc snippets you’ve been enjoying read very “camera-close,” immediate, and it helps keep Celeste’s POV sharp and procedural.

That said: it’s not a requirement. If your broader manuscript is past tense (and most novels are), we should absolutely keep continuity and move these scenes into past tense. Nothing about the scene’s effect depends on present tense; it’s just a stylistic lens.

If you want, I’ll rewrite “Numbers” in past tense with no other changes in content, cadence, or tone — easy.

2) You’re right: Celeste’s stated motive is too enlightened as written¶

What you’re describing is a very real, very useful dramatic asymmetry:

  • Celeste’s blind spot: she wants to be enough, and she frames “providing” as love. In her head, it’s a clean equation: I can carry us; therefore you can rest.
  • Charlotte’s need: to remain an entity. Not a dependent. Not a passenger. Not a beloved project.

In the draft I wrote, Celeste basically articulates Charlotte’s need (“I don’t want you to feel kept”) as if Celeste is already fully aware of it and prioritising it. That does sand off the conflict you want.

So the fix is: Celeste should initially misdiagnose the problem and only be forced, by Charlotte’s clarity, to see what she missed.

In other words, Celeste’s first answer shouldn’t be “I don’t want you to feel kept.” It should be something like:

  • “Because I don’t want you to have to worry,”
  • “Because I want you safe,”
  • “Because I want you free,”
  • “Because I want to take the pressure off you,”
  • “Because I’m sick of the world having a vote.”

All of those are believable Celeste motives — protective, controlling-in-a-loving-way, and not yet thinking in terms of Charlotte’s autonomy as a separate pillar.

Then Charlotte introduces the “kept” concept (or “passenger,” or “line item”), and Celeste has that internal jolt of: Oh. Right. That’s what I’m accidentally doing.

A clean way to rewrite that section (keeping your tone + restoring Celeste’s blind spot)¶

Here’s the replacement block for that stretch, still in present tense for now (I can flip to past tense after), with Celeste’s initial blindness intact:

“I’ll be on six figures,” I say. “At least. With the MBA.”

Charlotte’s eyes stay on the page. “And that’s… enough?”

The question pricks. Not because it’s hostile—because it’s honest.

“It should be,” I say.

Charlotte looks up. “Should be isn’t the same as is.”

I feel the sting anyway, because my body is annoyingly honest. Some old impulse in me—an ugly little cultural fossil—still wants to be able to say: I’ve got you. I can carry us. You don’t have to worry.

It’s not about masculinity. It’s about control. It’s about wanting the world to stop reaching for the person I love.

I tap the notebook with my pen. “Melbourne is expensive. But I can make it work.”

Charlotte’s mouth tightens, not angry—wary. “Why are you so determined to be the one who makes it work?”

I open my mouth and the first answer is the simple one. The selfish one.

Because then I don’t have to watch you flinch at every bill.

I choose something cleaner. “Because I don’t want you under pressure.”

Charlotte doesn’t move. “I’m not under pressure now.”

“Not like this,” I say. “Not like the city will make it.”

She studies me for a beat, and then says it—quietly, like she’s naming the real risk.

“Celeste… I don’t want to be kept.”

The word hits wrong. Not insulting. Not inaccurate. Just loaded—like a suitcase we both know contains more history than we’ve unpacked.

My throat tightens. And in the silence that follows, I realise what I’ve been doing.

I’ve been trying to buy us certainty.

I’ve been trying to make myself the only pillar, because one pillar is easier to control than two.

“Okay,” I say, and it comes out steadier than I feel. “That’s not what I want.”

Charlotte’s eyes don’t soften yet. “Then don’t build it like that.”

Now the conflict is positioned correctly:

  • Celeste starts from pressure/safety/control, not “autonomy theology.”
  • Charlotte introduces “kept,” forcing Celeste to confront the unintended consequence.
  • The scene’s energy shifts from Celeste “explaining” to Celeste adjusting.

If you want it even more Celeste-blind (a sharper version)¶

You can make Celeste initially annoyed at the implication — not outwardly cruel, but internally: Why won’t you let me do this for us? That makes her growth feel earned.

Example micro-beat:

“Kept,” I repeat, and I hate how the word tries to dirty what I’m offering.
I’m not keeping you. I’m building you a life.

And then I catch myself—because that sentence is the whole problem.

That’s very Celeste: she hears herself and immediately clocks the control hiding inside “care.”

If you want, I’ll do two concrete edits for you in one go, no fuss:

1) Convert the whole “Numbers” scene into past tense, and
2) Adjust Celeste’s motive arc so Charlotte is the one who names the “kept/passenger/line item” issue and Celeste has to arrive at it, rather than starting there.

Just say “yes, past tense” (or “keep present tense”), and I’ll paste the full revised scene.

Robyn:

Celeste opened the laptop and pulled up a simple budget spreadsheet. She didn’t dress it up. No colours. No pretty charts. Numbers didn’t need perfume.

“I’ll be on six figures,” Celeste said. “At least. With the MBA.”

Charlotte’s eyes stayed on the screen. “And that’s… enough?”

The question pricked—not because it was hostile, because it was honest.

“It should be,” Celeste said.

Charlotte looked up. “Should be isn’t the same as is.”

Celeste felt the sting anyway, because her body was annoyingly honest. Some old impulse in her—an ugly little cultural fossil—still wanted to be able to say: I’ve got you. I can carry us. You don’t have to worry.

It wasn’t about masculinity. It was about control. It was about wanting the world to stop reaching for the person she loved.

Celeste kept her voice even. “Melbourne is expensive. But I can make it work.”

Charlotte’s mouth tightened, not angry—wary. “Why are you so determined to be the one who makes it work?”

Celeste opened her mouth and the first answer was the simple one. The selfish one.

Because then I don’t have to watch you flinch at every bill.

She chose something cleaner. “Because I don’t want you under pressure.”

Charlotte didn’t move. “I’m not under pressure now.”

“Not like the city will make it,” Celeste said. “Not like it will if we start letting things slide.”

Charlotte’s gaze didn’t soften. It sharpened. “And your solution is… you do everything.”

Celeste felt irritation flare—hot and immediate—before she could dress it in reason. Not outwardly. She didn’t raise her voice. She didn’t roll her eyes. But inside, something snapped like a rubber band.

Why won’t you let me do this for us?

Because Celeste could do it. She could grind, she could climb, she could absorb. She could turn money into insulation. She could make the world bounce off them. That was the whole point of ambition, wasn’t it? It bought you choices. It bought you safety. It bought you a life where people couldn’t just… take.

Celeste kept her face calm. “I’m not saying you can’t do anything. I’m saying you don’t have to carry this.”

Charlotte set her mug down gently, like she was placing it somewhere safe. “I’m not trying to carry you. I’m trying to stand.”

The phrasing landed. Not dramatic. Just true.

Celeste’s annoyance tried to reassert itself. Stand. Fine. Stand. But couldn’t Charlotte stand without insisting on money? Couldn’t she stand in the way Celeste meant—beside her, protected by her?

Celeste heard the thought and went very still inside.

Protected by me.

The sentence sounded noble right up until it didn’t.

Charlotte watched her closely. “Celeste?”

Celeste made herself breathe. “Talk to me.”

Charlotte’s voice stayed quiet. “I want to keep working.”

Celeste didn’t respond immediately, because her first response was a reflexive of course you do, and she didn’t trust it.

“Where,” Celeste asked, controlled, “and why.”

Charlotte didn’t blink. “Wardrobe. Because it’s real work. I’m good at it. It’s mine.”

Celeste nodded once, as if this was purely logistical, as if the word mine hadn’t struck something tender.

Charlotte continued, more carefully. “And because Melbourne isn’t cheap.”

Celeste’s irritation stirred again at the implication—at the way it threatened her private vow to be enough. She wanted to say: Let me do this. She wanted to say: I’ve got you.

Instead she said, clipped, “I know Melbourne isn’t cheap.”

Charlotte’s gaze held. “Then why are you acting like my work is optional.”

Celeste’s jaw tightened. She didn’t like being cornered at her own table.

She reached for the pen and wrote two columns, briskly, as if neat handwriting could discipline her feelings:

WANT
NEED

She slid the notebook between them. “Fine. We do it properly. What do you want, and what do we need.”

Charlotte leaned forward and tapped the WANT column with one finger. “I want my footing.”

Celeste wrote: FOOTING.

Charlotte tapped again. “I want to be able to buy things without asking you.”

Celeste wrote: INDEPENDENCE / NO ASKING.

Charlotte hesitated, then added, “I want to contribute.”

Celeste paused with the pen hovering. “Define contribute.”

Charlotte’s eyes sharpened. “I don’t want to be a line item in your life.”

The phrase hit like a slap—not because it was cruel, because it was accurate.

Celeste felt annoyance spike again, sharp and defensive.

Line item. As if Celeste was reducing her. As if the care Celeste wanted to give was just… accounting.

Celeste nearly said, That’s not what this is.

And then she heard herself in her head: I can carry us.

Carry. One person carrying another. One person the load, one person the weight.

She sat back slightly, as if the chair had shifted under her.

Her throat tightened.

The annoyance didn’t vanish. It changed shape. It became something like shame—quiet, private, corrective.

Celeste looked at the notebook and then at Charlotte. “Okay.”

Charlotte didn’t soften yet. “Okay what.”

Celeste swallowed. “Okay, you’re right to say it.”

Charlotte’s eyebrows lifted, just a fraction. Surprise.

Celeste went on, because once she started she had to be clean about it. “I’ve been trying to buy us certainty. And I’ve been trying to do it by making myself the only pillar.”

Charlotte watched her steadily, giving her room to arrive at the truth.

Celeste’s mouth tightened. “Because one pillar is easier to control than two.”

There it was. The ugly heart of it. Not love. Not romance. Control disguised as care.

Charlotte’s voice stayed gentle, but it didn’t let Celeste off the hook. “And what does that make me.”

Celeste’s stomach dropped. She had the answer immediately and hated it.

“A passenger,” Celeste admitted.

Charlotte nodded once, calm. “Yes.”

Celeste looked down at her own neat columns and felt suddenly furious with herself—not dramatic fury, not self-pity. The clean anger of a woman realising she’d almost replicated a structure she despised.

Celeste set the pen down carefully. “I don’t want that.”

“Then don’t build it like that,” Charlotte said.

Celeste held her gaze. “I’m not trying to keep you.”

Charlotte’s face stayed steady. “I know. But you are trying to make it impossible for me to leave.”

Celeste’s breath caught.

That was the real thing. The hidden thing. The part Celeste hadn’t wanted to name because naming it made it sound… like a cage.

Celeste forced herself not to flinch away from it. “Yes,” she said, quiet. “I am.”

Charlotte’s eyes didn’t harden. They stayed soft and serious. “I’m not leaving.”

“I know,” Celeste said.

“Then let me have a life that proves it,” Charlotte replied. “Not because I need to earn you. Because I need to be a person in your world, not an extension of it.”

Celeste’s chest hurt with it. With the clarity. With the fact that Charlotte was right.

She nodded once. “Okay.”

Charlotte waited, measuring whether this was a concession or a conversion.

Celeste pushed the laptop slightly toward her. “Wardrobe isn’t ‘helping out.’ It’s a job. A real role. Properly defined. We structure your hours, we define your scope, and we don’t let it eat you.”

Charlotte’s mouth twitched. “You sound like Lauren.”

“Lauren is correct,” Celeste said flatly. “And so am I.”

Charlotte’s shoulders loosened, the smallest release. “Okay.”

Celeste picked the pen up again and pointed at the spreadsheet. “And we decide what’s shared, what’s personal, what’s savings. Your income is not ‘extra.’ It goes into the plan because it’s yours.”

Charlotte watched her for a beat, then nodded. “Good.”

Celeste hesitated, then said the thing that cost her something to say. “And if you can’t work for a while—sick, tired, whatever—your footing doesn’t vanish.”

Charlotte’s gaze warmed. “That’s what I mean.”

Celeste nodded. “Then we do it.”

For a moment neither of them spoke. The fridge hummed. A car passed outside. The world continued, oblivious to the fact that two women were adjusting the architecture of their life.

Charlotte’s voice came quietly. “Thank you.”

Celeste’s instinct was to wave it away. Don’t thank me. It’s obvious. It’s nothing.

Instead she simply nodded, letting the gratitude exist without turning it into a debt.

Then, because Celeste couldn’t help herself, she added, lightly, “And I still want it to be enough.”

Charlotte’s mouth curved. “You can want that.”

Celeste met her eyes. “But I can’t build it by making you smaller.”

Charlotte’s smile softened, and for the first time in the conversation, something like relief moved through the room.

“Exactly,” Charlotte said.

Celeste exhaled slowly and closed the notebook, not as an ending, but as a decision made.

“Okay,” she said. “Tomorrow I’ll talk to Lauren. We’ll formalise your role.”

Charlotte’s eyebrows lifted. “You’ll talk to Lauren.”

“I will,” Celeste said. “Because I don’t do half-measures. And because I’m not letting you get underpaid out of someone’s politeness.”

Charlotte let out a small, real laugh. “That’s… very you.”

Celeste’s mouth twitched. “Good.”

She reached across the table and took Charlotte’s hand.

Not possessive. Not claiming. Just present.

“Two pillars,” Celeste said.

Charlotte’s fingers tightened around hers, steady and equal. “Two pillars.”

Robyn:

There is a subtext, here, one I find incredibly gratifying. As Charlie, Charlotte would never have had the strength of character to stand up for herself. What this scene is saying is that by growing into a woman - Celeste's woman but also her own woman, she's learnt to shed that defence, that self-deprication. Becoming a woman was not what did it, becoming HERSELF - which was woman, as opposed to something she was not - gave that what she needed. I hope the readers get that.

Emily:

They will — if you keep doing exactly what you’re doing here: letting it show up in behaviour, not in a thesis statement.

What’s gratifying (and powerful) about the “Numbers” scene is that Charlotte doesn’t win by becoming louder or “more masculine.” She wins by becoming clear. That clarity is the tell.

Charlie’s old survival strategy was:

  • anticipate needs,
  • shrink,
  • be useful,
  • never make a claim that could be refused.

Charlotte’s new strategy — and it’s unmistakably her — is:

  • name the real risk cleanly (“line item,” “passenger”),
  • refuse to let love become a cage,
  • hold Celeste to a standard without punishing her,
  • stay soft without being pliable.

That’s not “learning to stand up like a man.” That’s female adulthood in the way your story defines it: relationally intelligent, boundary-capable, and calmly consequential.

How to make sure readers feel it (without spelling it out)¶

You can reinforce the subtext with two or three tiny, repeatable tells across scenes — little “Charlotte is herself now” motifs:

1) She doesn’t apologise for needs.
Even when she’s nervous, she’s direct: “I want…” “I don’t want…” “We need…”

2) She doesn’t barter.
Old Charlie would offer a trade: I’ll do X if you let me…
Charlotte just states reality and waits.

3) She gives Celeste room to be human without yielding ground.
That’s the sophistication: Charlotte doesn’t punish Celeste’s blind spot, but she also doesn’t let it stand.

You already did this with:

  • “I don’t want to be a line item in your life.”
  • “I know. But you are trying to make it impossible for me to leave.”
  • “I’m not leaving. Then let me have a life that proves it.”

Those lines don’t read like a makeover. They read like a person finally occupying her own spine.

One micro-line you can seed elsewhere (optional)¶

If you want a tiny, almost invisible callback later (perhaps in the gala conversation), you can have Charlotte say something like:

“I’m not trying to be brave. I’m trying to be true.”

Or, even more Charlotte:

“I’m not doing this to prove anything. I’m doing it because it’s mine.”

That quietly tells the reader: this isn’t performance; it’s alignment.

And the key philosophical beat you named¶

You put it perfectly: becoming a woman wasn’t the magic; becoming herself was. The gender is the shape her self takes — not a costume, not a prize, not a conversion. That’s why it reads as dignified and non-fetish.

If you like, in the upcoming gala scene we can let Charlotte have a single, careful internal moment where she notices that she’s not bargaining anymore — she’s not thinking “please don’t be embarrassed by me,” she’s thinking “tell me the code so I can meet it.” That’s the same spine, just in a different room.

When you’re ready, I can write the next scene in Celeste POV that naturally follows “Numbers”: Celeste emailing Lauren about formalising Charlotte’s role — and feeling that odd, proud warmth that Charlotte made her do it properly. That’s a gorgeous continuation of this subtext.

Robyn:

I'm so grateful you and I are 'singing off the same page', Emily. Please proceed.

Emily:

Scene — “Formalise” (Celeste POV, past tense)¶

Celeste didn’t kiss Charlotte after the numbers talk.

Not because she didn’t want to—she did. The urge rose in her like a reflex, a quick, warm impulse to turn the moment into intimacy and let the feeling carry them out of the hard part.

But Charlotte hadn’t fought for her footing so Celeste could reward her with affection like a coin.

So Celeste kept her hands where they were. Steady. Present. Adult.

They cleared the table together. Charlotte rinsed the mugs without being asked, not in a “good girl” way—just in the way someone does when it’s her kitchen too. Celeste watched her for half a second longer than necessary and then turned to the laptop before the softness could make her sloppy.

“Tomorrow,” Celeste had said, closing the notebook like a decision. “I’ll talk to Lauren.”

Charlotte had lifted an eyebrow. “You’ll talk to Lauren.”

“I will,” Celeste had replied, and meant it.

Now, later—Charlotte in the shower, the apartment briefly full of steam and running water—Celeste sat at the small desk in the living room with the laptop open. The phones were still charging in the hallway like they’d agreed. The glow in the corner of Celeste’s eye was annoying, but she didn’t move them. Rules stood.

She opened a blank email.

To: Lauren
Subject: Formalising Charlotte’s role (hours + scope)

Celeste’s fingers hovered over the keys.

It would have been very easy—almost pleasant—to write something vague. Something friendly. Something that kept everything soft and informal and therefore… controllable. Celeste could feel that instinct in herself like a habitual reach: keep it in her hands, keep it quiet, keep it hers.

Charlotte had just asked her not to do that.

And that was the point.

Celeste typed.

Lauren,

Following our planning for the Melbourne move, I want to formalise Charlotte’s ongoing role at Wardrobe so it’s structured, paid properly, and protected by the same standards as everything else we do.

Proposed scope (draft — adjust as you see fit):

  • Fit testing / wear-testing (movement, stress points, failure notes)
  • Pattern sanity checks (practicality, repeatability, ease of assembly)
  • QC sign-off on specific garment classes (to be defined)
  • Support for documentation (ledger entries / repeatable fixes)

Hours: I’m thinking part-time but meaningful — enough to be a pillar, not “helping out.” Charlotte’s preference is Wardrobe and she’s clear she wants her work to be real and bounded.

I’d like this to be a proper role with a title, pay rate, and agreed boundaries so it doesn’t drift into informal dependency or unspoken expectations.

If you’re open, can we discuss tomorrow and draft something simple but formal?

Celeste

Celeste read it back.

It was crisp. It was unromantic. It was exactly what it needed to be.

And yet, as her eyes moved over the words proper role and bounded, Celeste felt something in her chest unhook.

Not relief. Not surrender.

Pride.

Not the smug kind. The clean kind. The kind you feel when someone you love makes you better and doesn’t apologise for it.

Charlotte had forced her to do the right thing in the right way.

Celeste clicked Send before she could start “just tweaking” the tone into something softer and therefore more possessive.

The email whooshed away.

The apartment went quiet again, except for the shower and the distant city noise.

Celeste leaned back and let herself stare at the ceiling for a moment, the way she did when her brain needed a reset.

A thought arrived, uninvited and precise:

She’s not asking to be included. She’s insisting on belonging.

That was new. Not new as an idea—Celeste had always believed women should belong everywhere they chose—but new as a lived experience with Charlotte, because Charlie had never been able to do this. Charlie would have tried to earn it by being agreeable. By being small. By being grateful.

Charlotte simply… required the structure.

Celeste smiled to herself, almost irritated by how much she liked it.

The shower turned off. A moment later, Charlotte emerged in jeans and a plain tee again, hair damp, face bare, looking like a woman who didn’t need adornment to be legitimate.

She paused when she saw Celeste at the laptop. “You’re working.”

Celeste closed the lid, because she didn’t like to perform labour. “I sent Lauren an email.”

Charlotte’s expression sharpened. “About…?”

“About you,” Celeste said, and watched the reflex flicker in Charlotte’s eyes—the old flinch that used to be there whenever she became the subject of a decision.

Celeste didn’t allow it to grow.

“Formalising your role,” she continued. “Title. Pay. Hours. Scope.”

Charlotte blinked. “You actually did it.”

“Yes,” Celeste said, dry. “I said I would.”

Charlotte’s mouth opened and closed once, as if she were sorting through which response belonged in this new version of her.

“Thank you,” she said finally, quiet.

Celeste felt the familiar impulse to dismiss it—don’t thank me, it’s obvious, it’s fine, it’s nothing.

She didn’t.

She held Charlotte’s gaze instead. “You were right.”

Charlotte’s eyebrows lifted slightly. “About what.”

“About me trying to be the only pillar,” Celeste said. The words were blunt, and she let them be. “You didn’t let me romanticise control.”

Charlotte’s cheeks warmed a fraction. “I wasn’t trying to… accuse you.”

“I know,” Celeste said. “That’s why it worked.”

Charlotte stared at her, a little disarmed by the praise. Not because she wanted praise, but because praise was something Charlie had never known how to receive without bending under it.

Celeste stood and crossed the room. She didn’t hurry. She didn’t loom. She stopped close enough that Charlotte could feel her presence and decide what to do with it.

Charlotte made the decision first, as she had in the morning—one small, clean act.

She reached out and placed her hand at Celeste’s waist.

Celeste’s body responded instantly, warmth rising like a tide, but she kept her face composed.

Charlotte’s mouth curved. “Outside side,” she said softly, the hint of a tease.

“Yes,” Celeste replied.

Charlotte leaned in and kissed Celeste’s cheek—tender, chaste, deliberate. Not a performance. A confirmation.

Celeste went still for half a beat, stupidly caught off guard by how simple it felt. How right. How it landed in her chest like something she could carry all day.

When Charlotte pulled back, she looked calm, almost matter-of-fact, as if she’d simply set something in the correct place.

Celeste’s fingers found Charlotte’s wrist lightly—not to stop her, just to keep contact one second longer.

“Okay,” Celeste murmured, and the word came out warmer than intended.

Charlotte’s eyes flicked to hers. There was a small pleased glint there—no coyness, no triumph. Just… sure.

A few minutes later, as Celeste reached for her keys in the bowl by the door, her fingers hesitated—drifting up, almost absentmindedly, to the place on her cheek where Charlotte had kissed her.

Nothing theatrical. Just a brief touch, like checking something precious was still there.

Charlotte was already tugging on her jacket, composed, ordinary, ready.

Celeste let her hand fall back to the keys and opened the door, feeling—ridiculously—more anchored than she had been ten minutes ago.

“Bakery?” Celeste asked.

Charlotte nodded. “Procedure practice.”

Celeste’s mouth twitched. “Good.”

And they stepped out into the day like two women with somewhere to be—because they were.

Robyn:

If someone ever asked for an "Anti-Fetish Story Writing" tutorial, our discussions (which I am carefully logging - they're too valuable to simply read once and forget) and this story could serve as that tutorial. I'm pretty sure it's unique: fetish-stuff of all desciptions and intensity is available by the metric ton. Writing like this: extremely rare.

Emily:

That’s not just a nice side-effect of what you’re doing — it’s a structural consequence of the choices you’re making.

Most “TG-ish” writing (even the tasteful stuff) defaults to a handful of easy dopamine levers:

  • spectacle (clothes, reveal scenes, “can you believe it?” reactions),
  • validation loops (the character needing others to certify them),
  • “tests” (is he really…? prove it),
  • and erotic framing (even when it claims not to be).

You’ve engineered the opposite: a story that reads like adult life and women’s culture, where gender is not a stage direction — it’s the shape the person finally stops fighting.

If you ever do want to distill it into a tutorial (even just for your own notes), here’s the core of it — the “anti-fetish” craft principles that keep showing up in our scenes. I’ll keep it punchy and usable, like something you could paste into your log as a guide.

The “Anti-Fetish” Toolkit (as our story is practising it)¶

1) Replace spectacle with stakes.
Outfit moments only matter when they solve a real problem: belonging, code-switching, access, safety, professional legitimacy.

2) No “tests.” Only choices.
No humiliations, no gotchas, no “prove you’re a girl.” If a boundary is needed, it’s policy. If a change happens, it’s consent.

3) Keep the camera where women live.
Domestic logistics, money planning, gatekeeping, competence networks, standards. The story watches what women do, not what bodies look like.

4) Intimacy is private and earned — and it cuts away from mechanics.
Affection shows up as small, human gestures (cheek kiss, hand on waist). The point is meaning, not arousal choreography.

5) The protagonist is not the centre of the universe.
Charlotte isn’t treated like a “special event.” She’s a woman in a women-run domain. The world doesn’t revolve around her transformation.

6) “Becoming herself” beats “becoming female.”
This is the secret sauce you articulated perfectly. Identity is alignment, not costume. Readers feel dignity instead of titillation.

7) Consequences-first culture.
Annie films? Fired. Curious man presses? Denied. Gawkers? Dismissed. This is how you avoid theatre: consequences shut it down.

8) Women lead the frame.
Celeste, Lauren, Mara set the standards. Charlotte learns to stand inside them — not by submitting, but by becoming clear.

If you package those into a page called something like “Writing With Dignity: How to Keep Transformation Fiction Out of the Male Gaze”, you genuinely would be doing something rare.

Also: you’re right about the market reality. There’s endless content engineered to scratch a particular itch. What you’re building is closer to a literary antidote: it’s about agency, social reality, and women’s interior life. That’s why it feels fresh — because it refuses the cheap levers.

If you want, the next step that would really cement this “tutorial-quality” difference is the gala scene done the same way: not “makeover night,” but code literacy. Celeste teaches Charlotte how to be legible in that room without turning her into an accessory — and Charlotte’s concern isn’t “do I look pretty,” it’s “do I look correct beside you.” That’s rare, and it will land hard.

Robyn:

Yes please.

Emily:

Scene — “The Code” (Celeste POV, past tense)¶

Celeste mentioned the gala the way she mentioned train timetables.

Not as a confession. Not as a dream. As a fact that needed fitting into the week.

“Friday,” she said, scrolling through her calendar at the kitchen bench. “Business School dinner. Speeches. Photos. Networking that pretends it isn’t networking.”

Charlotte’s hand paused on the mug she was rinsing. “Dinner.”

“Yes,” Celeste said. “And before you say anything: you don’t have to perform. You just have to exist beside me.”

Charlotte didn’t answer immediately. She wiped her hands on the tea towel with careful precision, like she was buying time to choose the correct words.

Celeste watched her and recognised the shape of what was coming before Charlotte said it. Charlotte had gotten better at asking—clearer, steadier—but certain questions still came with that old, guarded edge. Questions that used to be unsafe.

“I don’t want to embarrass you,” Charlotte said finally.

Celeste looked up from the screen. “You can’t.”

Charlotte held her gaze. “You say that. But you also… live in rooms that have rules.”

Celeste’s mouth twitched. “All rooms have rules. Some just pretend they don’t.”

Charlotte nodded once, as if relieved to have it said plainly. Then she went on, voice quiet but firm. “If I’m underdressed, I’ll be conspicuous. And conspicuous is… risky. But also—” She stopped, searching. “It makes you look… like you didn’t brief me. Like you’re dragging someone in from the wrong world.”

Celeste felt a small flare of irritation—not at Charlotte. At the room Charlotte was describing. At the way a room full of polished people could still behave like a pack.

She kept her tone calm. “They don’t get to judge you.”

Charlotte didn’t move. “They will.”

Celeste exhaled once, slow. Then she closed the laptop, because this deserved her full attention.

“Okay,” she said. “Good. This is an adult question. We answer it like adults.”

Charlotte’s shoulders loosened slightly, as if she’d been granted permission to be serious without being dramatic.

Celeste leaned her elbows on the bench. “First: you are not an accessory. You are my partner. You are not there to make me look impressive, and you are not there to be evaluated as a novelty.”

Charlotte’s mouth tightened. “I know.”

“No,” Celeste corrected, gently but firmly. “You understand it. You don’t always feel it yet. So I’m saying it out loud.”

Charlotte’s eyes flickered—warmth, embarrassment, something like gratitude—but she didn’t apologise for it. Progress.

Celeste continued. “Second: you’re right about being underdressed. It’s not moral. It’s not about worth. It’s a code. The room reads clothes the way Wardrobe reads seams.”

Charlotte’s gaze sharpened at that. This she could hold.

Celeste nodded, pleased. “Exactly. So we meet the code. Not to please them. To deny them an opening.”

Charlotte nodded once. “Okay.”

Celeste reached for her notebook—because she could not help herself—and wrote a simple heading:

GALA CODE

She didn’t add flourishes. She didn’t make it cute.

She wrote three bullet points, crisp.

  • Correct silhouette
  • Correct neutrality
  • Correct ease

Charlotte leaned in to read.

Celeste tapped the first line. “Correct silhouette. Not ‘feminine costume.’ Not heels and swishing. Just… clean lines that read as intentional.”

Charlotte’s brow furrowed slightly. “Like… tailored.”

“Tailored,” Celeste confirmed. “And boring in the best way. A dress that doesn’t flirt. Or a jumpsuit with a blazer. Black, deep navy, charcoal. Something you can move in. Something you won’t tug at all night.”

Charlotte blinked. “No colour.”

Celeste’s mouth curved. “Colour reads like personality in those rooms. And you don’t need to be interpreted.”

Charlotte’s mouth twitched. “Neutrality.”

Celeste nodded and tapped the second line. “Correct neutrality. Hair neat. No dramatic makeup. No ‘transformation.’ We’re not giving anyone a narrative to chew.”

Charlotte’s gaze stayed on the page. “I don’t… do makeup.”

“Then don’t,” Celeste said. “This isn’t a rite. It’s a dinner.”

Charlotte looked up, still serious. “And shoes.”

Celeste paused for half a beat, because she knew exactly where Charlotte’s mind wanted to go—into the familiar trap of women’s pain being framed as beauty tax.

“No suffering,” Celeste said flatly. “You wear something you can walk in. Flat or low. Clean. Minimal.”

Charlotte’s shoulders eased as if she’d been bracing for a demand that didn’t arrive.

Celeste tapped the third line. “Correct ease. This is the real one. Ease means you’re not tugging, adjusting, checking. You look like you belong because your body believes it.”

Charlotte stared at that sentence as if it were a new instrument she hadn’t known existed.

“How do I… get that,” she asked quietly. “The ease.”

Celeste’s answer came without hesitation. “By not turning it into a costume.”

Charlotte swallowed. “So… the outfit is just a tool.”

“Yes,” Celeste said. “Everything is a tool.”

Charlotte went very still for a moment, and Celeste could see the thought moving behind her eyes: the old Charlie part of her trying to turn this into a test, into a pass/fail of femininity.

Charlotte pushed it away, visibly, with effort.

Then she asked, simply, “What do you want me to wear.”

Celeste didn’t soften her response. She didn’t make it coy. She didn’t pretend she didn’t have an opinion.

“I want you to wear something you can forget you’re wearing,” she said. “Something that reads correct beside me without asking you to become someone else.”

Charlotte nodded slowly. “Okay.”

Celeste watched her for a beat. Then she added, because this was the part Charlotte needed most.

“And you don’t get to carry my status anxiety for me.”

Charlotte blinked. “Your—”

“My status anxiety,” Celeste repeated, calm as a surgeon. “If you’re worried about embarrassing me, that’s you trying to manage a room you don’t owe anything to. I can handle my world. You handle being with me.”

Charlotte’s mouth opened, then closed. A small, reluctant smile tried to form.

“That’s very you,” she said.

Celeste’s mouth twitched. “Good.”

Charlotte glanced back at the notebook. “And if someone says something.”

Celeste didn’t even think. “Then I correct them.”

Charlotte’s eyes lifted. “How.”

“With language,” Celeste said. “Not emotion. I’ll introduce you properly, I’ll keep you near people who behave, and if someone tries to make you small, I’ll make them regret the attempt.”

Charlotte’s breath left her in a soft laugh that wasn’t amused so much as relieved.

Celeste reached across the bench and took Charlotte’s hand.

“Listen,” she said. “The room will try to read you. Let it. We don’t feed it. We don’t argue with it. We don’t explain. We just… appear correct, and then we talk to the people worth talking to.”

Charlotte nodded, gaze steady. “Okay.”

Celeste squeezed her hand once. “Now. Practicalities.”

Charlotte’s eyebrows lifted. “More practicalities.”

“Yes,” Celeste said, unapologetic. “Because practicalities are how women stay free.”

She picked up her phone—not the hallway-charging one, the daytime one she actually used—and opened her messages.

“Lauren,” Celeste said, already typing. “And Mara.”

Charlotte stiffened slightly. “You’re involving them.”

“Yes,” Celeste said, eyes on the screen. “Because Wardrobe is where clothes become correct. This is literally what they do. And because you are not doing this alone.”

Charlotte’s mouth tightened at the old reflex—don’t be a burden—then softened as she let herself accept the net.

Celeste sent the first message.

Lauren: Need one gala-appropriate outfit for Charlotte. Code: tailored, neutral, comfortable. No theatre. Fit + correctness. Can you advise?

Then the second.

Mara: Gala outfit request. Charlotte needs something correct beside me. No costume. No suffering. You’ll have opinions; I’m asking for them.

Charlotte watched her, quiet.

Celeste set the phone down. “We’ll do a fitting after hours. Quick. Efficient. No fuss.”

Charlotte nodded. “And hair.”

“Neat,” Celeste said. “Whatever you do now, but cleaner. If you want, we’ll book a simple trim earlier in the week. Not a ‘salon day.’ A trim.”

Charlotte’s mouth curved faintly. “You really hate tropes.”

Celeste’s eyes narrowed, amused. “I hate being told there’s only one way to be a woman.”

Charlotte’s expression shifted—soft, serious. “Me too.”

That sentence landed between them with a quiet weight. It wasn’t a speech. It was an agreement.

A minute later, Charlotte’s phone buzzed from the hallway where it was charging. She went to check it, then came back, holding it as if it contained something delicate.

“Mara replied,” she said.

Celeste’s eyebrows lifted. “Already?”

Charlotte read, voice careful, as if she didn’t quite believe the words.

Mara: “Correct is boring. Good. We’ll make it fit like it was inevitable. No heels. No nonsense. Bring her at six.”

Charlotte looked up. “She said… ‘bring her.’”

Celeste smiled—small, satisfied. “Yes.”

Charlotte’s throat worked once. “She didn’t say ‘him.’”

Celeste’s voice stayed steady. “Because she’s not stupid. And because you’re not a joke in her house.”

Charlotte’s eyes went glossy for a second and she blinked it away. Not shame. Not fragility. Just the body’s honest response to being treated correctly.

Celeste stood, came around the bench, and stopped close. She didn’t crowd. She simply offered presence.

Charlotte reached first, placing her hand at Celeste’s waist.

Celeste’s body warmed immediately, the private yes she’d grown used to.

Charlotte’s mouth curved. “So… tailored and boring.”

“Tailored and boring,” Celeste confirmed.

Charlotte’s eyes lifted, serious again. “And… if I still feel like I’m going to ruin it.”

Celeste held her gaze. “Then you tell me. And I remind you you’re not ruin. You’re my life.”

Charlotte’s breath caught, subtle and real.

Celeste didn’t soften the sentence afterward. She didn’t dilute it with a joke. She let it stand.

Then she added, brisk, because she was Celeste and she believed in ending meetings cleanly.

“Friday,” she said. “You’ll be correct. You’ll be comfortable. And you’ll walk into that room with me like you belong there—because you do.”

Charlotte nodded once.

“Okay,” she said, the word steady and adult.

And in that moment, Celeste knew something she hadn’t known even a month ago:

Charlotte wasn’t asking for permission to exist in Celeste’s world.

She was learning the code so she could stand in it—on her own feet—beside the woman she loved.

Robyn:

Dare I be bold, here? Stop me if this seems too cliché or contrived or something, but I'd like this event to be almost a Wardrobe event. Mara has been invited. So have Lauren and Sarah. And here's where I thought it could get interesting: Lauren and Sarah arrive.... together. Making a statement they wouldn't, at work. "We care for each other, we're an 'us', now." If you think a Graduation is an inappropriate venue for that sort of statement, please say so - we can put it off until later... make it its own event. I'll leave it to your discretion.

Emily:

You can absolutely do it — and it doesn’t have to feel cliché at all if it isn’t staged as a “ta-da” moment.

In fact, this is one of the rare venues where it’s most believable:

  • An MBA grad dinner / faculty gala is not Wardrobe’s workplace.
  • It’s exactly the kind of event where people arrive with a plus-one, or as a “unit,” and nobody needs an explanation.
  • It also fits your established rule: decorum at work, freedom outside it.

So: appropriate venue, yes — but make the statement quiet and unperformative.

How to keep it from feeling contrived¶

Don’t let Lauren/Sarah “announce.”
Let them simply arrive together like it’s normal. That is the statement.

Avoid “matching outfits” or hand-holding theatrics at the door.
One shared coat-check ticket. One “we’re together” body language moment. A brief glance that says we’re steady. That’s enough.

Let Celeste clock it with a private, satisfied read.
She doesn’t squeal. She doesn’t interrogate. She files it as: good — they’re real now.

Why Mara/Lauren/Sarah being invited works¶

If Celeste is graduating MBA, it’s very plausible she frames them as:

  • her foundational professional network (the venture she helped scale / the real-world anchor of her studies),
  • people she considers chosen family (very credible in women’s lives),
  • and frankly: the women who helped her become who she is.

Also, having Wardrobe “show up” gives you exactly what you want: the event becomes a women’s competence network moment in a different room. Not a fashion parade. Not a fetish stage. A public, adult transition.

Two strong staging options¶

Option A: They arrive together, but it’s “just logistics”¶

They share a rideshare. They enter mid-conversation. They look relaxed, coordinated, like a pair that has done the boring work of becoming real.

Celeste notices one tiny thing: - Sarah automatically takes Lauren’s coat without asking, hands it to the attendant, then steps aside to let Lauren speak first. - Or Lauren instinctively checks Sarah’s name tag is straight (a tiny tenderness).

No announcement required.

Option B: They arrive separately, but choose each other publicly¶

They arrive a few minutes apart (less “couple debut”), then Sarah crosses the room and joins Lauren without hesitation. That’s a bigger emotional punch, and it can feel even more “adult” because it isn’t packaged as an entrance.

If you’re worried it might be “too soon”¶

Here’s the litmus test: are they “declaring,” or are they “behaving”?

  • If it’s declaring (“We’re an us now!”), it risks feeling written.
  • If it’s behaving (arriving together, calm, natural), it will read true.

Given your tone, I’d do behaving.

A quick micro-beat you can steal (Celeste POV, very you)¶

Celeste sees them and instantly understands.

Not because they’re clinging to each other — they aren’t.

Because Lauren looks… unguarded. And Sarah looks… chosen.

Celeste doesn’t comment. She just makes space for them at her table like it was always meant to be that way.

If you want, I’ll write the arrival sequence (Celeste POV) for the gala with: - Wardrobe women appearing as Celeste’s “real” team, - Charlotte looking correct (not “done up” — simply legible), - and the Lauren/Sarah arrival landing as a quiet, confident reveal.

And I can also include one small “room test” moment (someone says something faintly patronising), where Celeste handles it cleanly — not protective theatre, just competence.

Scene — “The Room” (Celeste POV, past tense)¶

The building had the kind of lobby that tried not to look like it cost money.

Stone that wasn’t quite marble, lighting that pretended it was “warm” rather than engineered, a wall of glass that made the city look like an accessory. Everyone moved slightly slower in there, as if speed might reveal you didn’t belong.

Celeste loved rooms like this, not because they were impressive, but because they were readable.

They had codes. They had tells. They rewarded calm.

Charlotte walked beside her without hesitation.

Not clinging. Not hovering. Simply present, her shoulder occasionally brushing Celeste’s sleeve when the crowd tightened, like a quiet confirmation: I’m here, and I’m not asking permission.

Charlotte looked correct in the way Mara had promised—inevitable. Tailored, neutral, comfortable. No theatrical femininity, no “reveal,” no hair-and-makeup narrative. Just a silhouette that didn’t beg the room to interpret it.

The effect was immediate and almost funny: people glanced, then glanced away. No second look. No hunger. No curiosity. No crack to wedge into.

Celeste took quiet satisfaction in that.

At the registration desk, a student volunteer in a crisp black outfit smiled and checked Celeste’s name.

“Celeste—yes. MBA.” The volunteer’s eyes flicked to Charlotte. “And… guest?”

Charlotte’s posture stayed easy. She didn’t flinch, didn’t shrink. She let Celeste answer, because this was Celeste’s event and the room’s language was Celeste’s.

“Charlotte,” Celeste said, the word clean and matter-of-fact. “My partner.”

The volunteer didn’t miss a beat. “Lovely. Welcome, Charlotte.”

It was nothing. It was everything.

They moved toward the function space. The doors opened onto a sea of small tables, linen, glassware, soft sound. The air smelled like citrus and expensive perfume and the faint metallic edge of ice.

Celeste scanned the room the way she scanned a board meeting: exits, bottlenecks, power clusters, the people who looked like they were here to celebrate and the people who looked like they were here to be seen celebrating.

Charlotte followed her gaze, not anxious—learning.

“Okay?” Celeste murmured.

Charlotte’s answer was immediate. “Okay.”

Not brave. Not performative. Just… stable.

At their table, Mara was already seated like she owned it.

She wore black. Of course she did. Not fashionable-black. Purpose-black. A clean jacket, sharp collar, hair pinned back with militant simplicity. If the room wanted softness, Mara had declined.

She didn’t stand when Celeste arrived. She simply looked up and assessed.

“You’re on time,” Mara said.

Celeste smiled. “I was raised correctly.”

Mara’s eyes flicked to Charlotte. A beat. Then: “Correct.”

Charlotte’s mouth curved faintly. “Thank you.”

Mara made a sound that might have been approval if you were generous. “Sit.”

Lauren arrived next—alone.

She moved through the room like a woman who understood it and didn’t care. Not stiff. Not eager. Calm, measured, consequential. She wore something tailored, minimal jewellery, hair done in the most boring competent way possible.

She greeted Celeste with a brief kiss on the cheek—socially correct, nothing extra—and then she turned to Charlotte.

“Hi,” Lauren said. Her eyes were steady. “You look… right.”

Charlotte blinked once, then nodded. “Mara.”

Lauren’s mouth twitched. “Mara.”

Celeste watched the exchange with private satisfaction. No fuss, no sentimentality, no “isn’t this exciting.” Just women recognising competence in each other.

Sarah hadn’t arrived yet.

Celeste pretended not to notice the absence. She wasn’t a gossip. She didn’t pick at other people’s timing. She simply kept an open seat at the table like it was the most ordinary thing in the world.

A waiter poured water. Someone at a nearby table laughed too loudly. A man in a suit did that loose-shouldered thing men did when they were trying to look relaxed around power.

Celeste leaned slightly toward Charlotte. “How’s the temperature.”

“Fine,” Charlotte said. Then, quieter, “I’m not tugging.”

Celeste’s mouth curved. “Good. Correct ease.”

Charlotte’s eyes flicked to hers, warm and amused. “You sound like you wrote a manual.”

“I did,” Celeste said, deadpan. “For you. You’re welcome.”

Charlotte’s lips parted in a small laugh and she looked away, but not away from discomfort—away from the fact that she didn’t quite know what to do with how cared-for she felt.

Celeste let her have the moment.

Across the room, Celeste’s program director—Dr. Patel—moved from table to table like a benevolent monarch, stopping to greet students, donors, staff. She spotted Celeste and lifted a hand in recognition.

Celeste lifted hers back, composed.

The room was settling into its rhythm when the first small test arrived.

A woman in an elegant dress—mid-forties, lacquered hair, eyes sharp as a blade—paused by their table and smiled at Celeste with a professional brightness that didn’t quite reach warmth.

“Celeste,” she said, as if tasting the name. “Congratulations. MBA—impressive.”

“Thank you,” Celeste replied.

The woman’s gaze slid to Charlotte. It wasn’t hostile. It was… appraising. The way people looked at a handbag to see if it was real leather.

“And you are…?” she prompted.

Charlotte’s posture didn’t change. She didn’t shrink. She waited.

Celeste answered, voice even. “Charlotte is my partner.”

The woman’s smile held, but her eyes didn’t. “How lovely. And what do you do, Charlotte?”

It wasn’t a question asked out of interest. It was a sorting exercise.

Celeste felt the familiar urge to step in hard—to make the woman regret the attempt.

She didn’t.

She corrected the room the way she corrected a spreadsheet: cleanly, unemotionally, with a single adjustment that made everything else fall into place.

“Charlotte is with Wardrobe,” Celeste said. “Technical—fit and quality control. If you’ve seen the period pieces from the last two seasons, you’ve seen her work.”

The woman blinked. A tiny recalculation.

Mara didn’t look up from her water glass. She didn’t need to.

Lauren’s eyes flicked to Celeste for half a second—approval without performance.

Charlotte spoke then, calm and spare. “Nice to meet you.”

The woman recovered quickly, smile re-brightening now that she’d found a category that sounded respectable. “Oh! Wardrobe. Yes, I’ve heard—”

Celeste cut in politely. “We keep our work private until it’s ready.”

It was the gentlest possible shut door. It still shut.

The woman’s smile tightened. “Of course. Well. Enjoy your night.”

She moved on.

Charlotte exhaled once, quiet. Not relief exactly. More like acknowledgement: That happened. We handled it.

Celeste didn’t touch her under the table. She didn’t need to. She just met Charlotte’s eyes briefly and gave the smallest nod.

Charlotte returned it.

Then—finally—Sarah arrived.

Not late enough to be disruptive. Just late enough that her entrance didn’t read like a coordinated arrival with Lauren.

She moved through the room with that particular Sarah energy: confident, slightly amused, as if she’d been invited to a formal event and had decided to treat it as an anthropological study. Her dress was simple and correct. Her hair was neat. Nothing loud. Nothing performative.

She stopped a few paces from the table and looked at Lauren.

Just looked.

Lauren, for her part, didn’t spring up. Didn’t beam. Didn’t wave. She lifted her chin slightly—an acknowledgement that was almost imperceptible to anyone not watching for it.

Sarah smiled—small, private—and crossed the remaining distance.

When she reached the table, she greeted Mara first, because Sarah was not stupid.

“Mara,” she said, warm and respectful. “You look terrifying.”

Mara’s mouth moved by a millimetre. “Good.”

Sarah turned to Celeste. “Congratulations, graduate.”

“Thank you,” Celeste said, and then she watched Sarah turn—finally—to Lauren.

There was no announcement. No “this is my girlfriend.” No hand-holding at the door. No performative claim.

Just a choice made in public: Sarah placed her hand lightly on the back of Lauren’s chair as she slid into the seat beside her, the touch brief and unshowy, the kind of contact you only made when you were already sure you were allowed.

Lauren didn’t flinch.

She angled her knee slightly toward Sarah under the table—an unconscious shift that made their proximity look inevitable, not new.

And that was it. The whole statement. Quiet. Adult. Real.

Celeste felt a small, surprising swell of warmth in her chest.

Not because she wanted romance drama. Because she respected discipline. Because she respected women who did the boring work of becoming steady, and then lived it without asking anyone’s permission.

Charlotte leaned toward Celeste and murmured, almost amused, “They look… settled.”

Celeste’s mouth twitched. “Yes.”

Mara, without looking at anyone, said dryly, “If anyone tries to flirt with either of you tonight, I will burn this place down.”

Sarah laughed softly. “Romantic.”

Lauren’s voice was calm. “Efficient.”

The waiter arrived with bread. The room’s noise rose and fell like a tide. A microphone squealed briefly near the stage.

Celeste sat back, took in her table—her women—and felt something in her body unclench.

This wasn’t a makeover. This wasn’t a performance.

This was her life, widening.

And Charlotte sat beside her, correct and comfortable, not as a guest in Celeste’s world, but as a woman learning its codes—on her own feet—while the women who had built her up simply… made space.

When the first speech began and the lights shifted slightly toward the stage, Celeste glanced at Charlotte once more.

Charlotte met her gaze, calm.

Then, under the cover of applause, Charlotte leaned in and kissed Celeste’s cheek—soft, chaste, deliberate.

Celeste went still for half a beat, stupidly caught off guard by how much that tiny gesture anchored her.

She didn’t react outwardly. She didn’t turn it into theatre.

She simply let the warmth settle in her chest like a small flame she could carry through the night.

And then she faced the stage, composed, exactly where she belonged.

Robyn:

I keep tiptoeing to the fringe, don't I? Here is a scene playing in a nebulous fashion in my head:

Celeste is going to a networking function. Being there, doing this will have very positive outcomes for Wardrobe, so it is essential she does it. She dresses up for it: floaty dress, heels, makeup, perfume. See image as reference for the material, not the style, certainly. Charlotte is watching her get ready and wonders aloud ... well, here is where I pause. Women do enjoy their clothes, their perfume. To exclude that aspect of womanhood would seem false and deliberately exclusionary: "no boy would want that." But Charlotte is now a woman. Here's my dilemma, Emily. How do we even address the enjoyable aspects of femininity without yielding to the tropes of the fetish-crowd, which I want to avoid at all costs. If it's too hard, we just leave it. But I know from personal experience it is a very real, very important, very enjoyable part of being a woman. So yeah, tiptoeing to the fringe and staring into the abyss...

Emily:

You’re not tiptoeing to the fringe — you’re bumping into a real craft problem: how to write pleasure without turning it into performance.

Because you’re right: clothes, scent, texture, “feeling lovely” can be genuinely important and genuinely joyful. If you ban it entirely, the story starts to feel puritanical… and worse, it accidentally keeps the old male framing in charge (“if it risks arousal, we must remove it”). That’s still letting the fetish crowd set the rules — just in reverse.

You can include it. The trick is where the pleasure lives.

The difference isn’t what happens — it’s the lens¶

Fetish writing makes femininity a spectacle: - camera lingers, - fabric “behaves” dramatically, - the body is the subject, - the reaction is the prize.

Your version keeps femininity as ownership: - pleasure is private and self-directed, - clothes are chosen for feeling and function (authority, ease, identity), - the body isn’t “revealed,” it’s inhabited, - the scene stays in women’s interiority (and women’s standards).

Put bluntly: make it about Celeste enjoying herself, not about Charlotte being “converted,” and not about hypothetical onlookers.

A simple set of guardrails that keeps you safe and honest¶

1) Pleasure is for the wearer.
Celeste wears perfume because she loves it, not because it makes her “irresistible.”

2) No montage. One or two decisive actions.
A spritz, a lipstick swipe, a clasp fastened. Done. No “trying on ten dresses,” no mirror worship.

3) Describe materials like a craftsperson, not a voyeur.
Hand, weight, movement, sound, temperature — not “curves,” “skin,” “reveals.”

4) Keep it tethered to purpose.
“This room reads codes; I’m speaking its language.” That’s adult, not trope.

5) Charlotte’s response is not arousal; it’s recognition.
She doesn’t go glassy-eyed. She clocks: this is Celeste in her element. She feels pride, admiration, maybe a faint, intimate tenderness — not “OMG femininity.”

6) No tests. No “do you want to be like this?”
Charlotte can simply witness and learn: femininity can be enjoyed without being performed for anyone.

Scene — “For Me” (Celeste POV, past tense)¶

Celeste didn’t get dressed for the room.

She got dressed for the outcome.

Wardrobe needed this. Not as theatre—never that—but as leverage. The kind you earned by showing up correctly, saying the right things, being remembered for competence rather than novelty.

The apartment was quiet in that pre-event way: kettle cooling, phone charging in the hall, Charlotte perched on the edge of the sofa with a book she wasn’t reading.

Celeste moved through her preparation without rushing. Not because she enjoyed taking her time, exactly—because she enjoyed not being hurried by anyone else’s agenda.

She’d chosen something that didn’t ask to be interpreted.

A dress in a soft, floaty fabric—light, layered, catching light without glittering for attention. Not a “look at me” dress. A move in me dress. It skimmed rather than clung. It made a sound when she walked, a quiet hush like good lining.

Charlotte watched her from the sofa, eyes steady.

Celeste clipped one earring on, then the other. Minimal. Clean. The kind of detail you only noticed if you were close.

Charlotte spoke at last, carefully, as if testing whether the question was allowed.

“Are you… doing all this for them?”

Celeste paused with the clasp half-fastened and looked over.

Charlotte’s face was calm, not accusatory. Just curious. Serious.

Celeste finished the clasp and turned fully toward her.

“No,” she said. “I’m doing it for me.”

Charlotte blinked once, as if she hadn’t expected that answer to be so immediate.

Celeste picked up the perfume—small bottle, good atomiser, nothing flashy—and held it for a second before using it. Not as a ritual for seduction. As a private anchor.

“One spritz,” she said, almost to herself. “That’s it.”

She misted it lightly at her throat and let it settle.

The scent was not sweet. Not girlish. Not a dessert. It had a clean, bright opening, then something warmer underneath—like cedar after rain. It was the kind of smell that made Celeste feel awake.

Charlotte watched, then asked softly, “Why?”

Celeste’s mouth twitched. “Because it makes me happy.”

Charlotte’s gaze held. “Just that.”

Celeste reached for her lipstick—neutral, barely-there—and applied it with two quick strokes, no mirror drama. She capped it and set it down.

“Yes,” she said. “Just that. I like feeling… finished. I like knowing I’m in my own skin on purpose.”

Charlotte looked down at her hands for a moment, then back up. “I’ve never… seen that as allowed.”

Celeste’s brow lifted. “Allowed by who.”

Charlotte hesitated. “By… the old way of thinking.”

Celeste understood without needing it translated.

The old way: that anything pleasurable about femininity must be either a performance for men or a trap for the self. That enjoyment is suspect. That softness is a weakness unless it’s rewarded by someone watching.

Celeste’s voice stayed calm. “Enjoyment is not the same as performance.”

Charlotte’s mouth tightened slightly, processing.

Celeste stepped closer—not crowding, just bringing her presence into the room where Charlotte could feel it.

“I’m not dressing like this so people will want me,” Celeste said. “If someone wants me, that’s their problem. I’m dressing like this because I like the sensation of competence that includes beauty. It’s mine. It’s not an invitation.”

Charlotte’s gaze sharpened. That was a code she could understand.

Celeste tapped the side seam lightly. “Also—practical—this fabric behaves in a room. It moves. It doesn’t crease like cheap suit wool. It reads expensive without screaming.”

Charlotte’s eyes flicked to the dress again, and the look on her face wasn’t hunger.

It was appreciation—like she was looking at good workmanship.

“You’re… armouring,” Charlotte said.

Celeste smiled. “Exactly. But it’s comfortable armour.”

Charlotte went quiet. Then, cautiously: “Do you ever worry it makes you… conspicuous.”

Celeste’s answer was instant. “Yes.”

Charlotte waited.

Celeste shrugged, small. “But I would rather be conspicuous by choice than invisible by default.”

That landed. Celeste saw it land. She watched Charlotte’s shoulders loosen, the way they did when something clicked into place without pain.

Charlotte looked up at her again. “You smell nice.”

Celeste’s mouth curved. “Thank you.”

Charlotte’s eyes narrowed slightly, amused. “You didn’t say what it is.”

“I’m not telling you,” Celeste said. “Because if I tell you, you’ll try to solve it. And it’s not a problem to solve.”

Charlotte’s lips parted in a quiet laugh.

Celeste checked her shoes—low enough to walk in, clean enough to be correct—and slid her phone into her small clutch.

She didn’t look in the mirror for long. One glance to confirm: neat, correct, easy.

Then she turned back to Charlotte.

Charlotte was still watching, and now there was something warmer there. Not dazzled. Not unsettled. Just… proud.

Celeste felt an odd tenderness rise in her chest, sharp enough to surprise her.

Charlotte stood and came closer. She didn’t reach for the dress. She didn’t touch the perfume spot. She didn’t make it about the surface.

Instead she placed her hand briefly at Celeste’s waist—familiar, grounding—and said, very softly, “You look like you’re about to win.”

Celeste’s throat tightened in a way she refused to romanticise.

“I am,” she said.

Charlotte held her gaze a beat longer, then leaned in and kissed Celeste’s cheek—chaste, deliberate, affectionate. Not to claim. Not to show off.

To say: I see you.

Celeste went still for half a second, then exhaled, the warmth settling in her like something she could carry into any room.

“Okay,” Celeste said, and the word came out steadier than it had any right to.

She picked up her keys.

At the door, Charlotte’s voice followed her—quiet, practical, equal. “Text me when you’re leaving. And when you get there.”

Celeste nodded once. “Procedure.”

Charlotte’s mouth curved. “Procedure.”

Celeste stepped into the corridor, the scent still close to her skin, the dress light around her legs, and the feeling in her chest even lighter.

Not because she’d dressed up.

Because she’d been seen correctly before she walked into a room that didn’t deserve her.

If you like, the next beat can be at the function itself: Celeste moving through conversation with the same clean competence, and one person making a faintly patronising remark about Wardrobe being “cute” — and Celeste correcting it with a single sentence that reclassifies the entire venture as serious business. That lets the “enjoyable femininity” exist alongside power, not instead of it.

Scene — “Not Cute” (Celeste POV, past tense)¶

The venue was a hotel bar that had been sanded smooth by money.

Low light. Quiet music that refused to be memorable. Glassware that chimed gently when you set it down, as if even sound had a dress code.

Celeste arrived five minutes early on purpose.

Not because she enjoyed waiting. Because arriving early let her choose her angles. It let her see who clustered with whom, which faces were relaxed, which were hungry. It let her take the temperature of the room before anyone tried to take hers.

She checked in, accepted a name tag with a practised smile, and moved toward the edge of the crowd like she belonged there. Because she did.

The dress behaved exactly as promised—light, clean, quiet. It moved with her rather than against her. It never asked to be adjusted. The shoes were low enough that she could stand without thinking about standing. The perfume stayed close to her skin, present only when she moved.

It made her feel awake.

The first few conversations were easy: classmates, alumni, a lecturer Celeste liked, a woman from a consulting firm who asked intelligent questions and listened to the answers.

Celeste spoke in the language she’d learned to speak in rooms like this—brief, clear, forward. No confession, no apology, no “I’m lucky.” She didn’t minimise herself to make other people comfortable.

It was working.

Then, inevitably, the room tested the seams.

A man drifted into her orbit as if he’d been there all along.

Mid-forties. Crisp suit. The relaxed confidence of someone who believed himself pre-approved. He introduced himself with a name and a title that sounded like a job invented to justify a bonus.

“Celeste,” he said, shaking her hand. “MBA. Congratulations.”

“Thank you,” she replied.

He glanced at her name tag again, as though cross-referencing her with a mental database. “You’re… involved with Wardrobe, aren’t you?”

Celeste held his gaze. “Yes.”

He smiled, friendly enough to pass, and then he did the thing Celeste had learned to recognise in men like him: he reduced first, so he could offer a compliment that sounded generous later.

“Oh, that’s… cute,” he said. “The costumes. Very—” he searched for the word, and found it with visible pleasure, “—creative.”

Celeste didn’t freeze.

She didn’t bristle.

She didn’t smile politely and let it land, the way younger women often did because it was easier than correcting strangers.

She simply adjusted the classification.

“Not cute,” she said, mild as a weather report.

The man blinked. “Sorry?”

Celeste’s voice stayed calm. “Wardrobe is a manufacturing and design business. We’re scaling. We have US seasonal demand spikes, capacity constraints, and a quality-control model that’s better than most small apparel operations because we treat fit as engineering.”

She took a sip of water, as if she’d just described the menu.

The man’s smile hesitated. His eyes flicked away for half a second—recalculating.

“Right,” he said. “Well. Still… creative.”

Celeste let her mouth curve slightly, not in agreement, in containment.

“Creativity is cheap,” she said. “Execution isn’t.”

She watched him absorb that.

He attempted to recover the upper hand by shifting the subject. “So what’s your plan after graduation? Corporate? Consulting?”

The question was framed as if there were only respectable paths.

Celeste answered as if the frame didn’t exist.

“My plan is revenue,” she said pleasantly. “And control of our supply chain. I’m not interested in being impressive in someone else’s hierarchy.”

The man laughed, a little too loud. “Ambitious.”

Celeste nodded once. “Competent.”

He tried again, smaller this time. “Do you have investors?”

Celeste met his gaze. “We have standards.”

A beat.

It was the cleanest sentence she could have chosen. It told him: you don’t get in by offering money if you can’t meet the culture.

He smiled again, more careful now. “Well. If you ever want to talk growth strategy, I’d be happy to—”

Celeste cut in with a polite finality that would have sounded warm to anyone not listening.

“Thanks,” she said. “If we need consultancy, we’ll hire it. Enjoy your night.”

She turned slightly—not a dramatic dismissal, simply a reorientation toward the next conversation—and the man drifted away as if the room had decided he wasn’t relevant anymore.

Celeste exhaled once, slow.

She didn’t feel triumphant. She felt… tidy. Like she’d corrected a typo.

A woman beside her—mid-thirties, sleek suit, sharp eyes—lifted her glass slightly.

“Thank you for saying that,” the woman murmured.

Celeste glanced at her. “Saying what.”

“Not cute.” The woman’s smile was small, genuine. “I’m in operations. If one more person calls my work ‘cute’ I’ll set something on fire.”

Celeste’s mouth twitched. “Understandable.”

They spoke for a few minutes—real conversation, not performance—about supply chains and seasonal spikes and why quality control always looked like “overhead” to people who’d never had a product fail in public.

It was good. It was useful.

Half an hour later, Celeste’s phone buzzed with a message.

Charlotte: Here when you get home. You good?

Celeste’s chest warmed in that small, private way it had ever since she’d left the apartment.

Celeste: Good. Room’s behaving. I’ll text when I’m leaving.

She slid the phone back into her clutch without looking at it again. Rules stood.

Another alumni approached. Another handshake. Another exchange of titles.

Celeste moved through it all with the same clean ease.

Not because she was dressed up.

Because she had made the enjoyable parts of femininity belong to her—quiet, chosen, unperformative—and she had kept the rest of the room in its proper place.

When she finally left two hours later, she touched the spot on her cheek—briefly, absentmindedly—where Charlotte had kissed her before she walked out the door.

Nothing theatrical. Just a private check: still there.

Then she stepped into the night air, composed, and texted Charlotte:

Leaving now.
