Emily:

Scene — “After” (Lauren POV, past tense)¶

The room was dim in the way bedrooms were dim when you didn’t want to break what had just happened.

Not darkness—just mercy. A soft lamp on the dresser. The curtains pulled. The faint, distant hiss of cars on wet asphalt. The air still carried that clean warmth of bodies that had finally stopped bracing.

Lauren lay on her back, sheet pulled up to her waist, one arm under her head. Sarah was curled beside her, shoulder to Lauren’s chest, the crown of her hair tickling Lauren’s collarbone whenever she breathed.

Lauren’s body felt loose. Unarmoured.

Which was why her face refused to cooperate.

Sarah shifted, sensing it the way she sensed everything. She lifted her head slightly and looked up at Lauren, eyes sharp even in low light.

“Hey,” Sarah murmured. “What’s wrong.”

“Nothing,” Lauren said automatically.

Sarah didn’t accept it. She never did. It was one of the reasons Lauren had let her in; it was also one of the reasons it terrified her.

Sarah propped herself on her elbow, studying Lauren’s expression like it was a garment seam that didn’t quite sit flat.

“Did I—” Sarah began, then stopped, careful. “Did I do something.”

Lauren exhaled through her nose, almost a laugh. “No.”

Sarah waited.

Lauren stared at the ceiling for a moment, letting herself feel the weight of saying the truth out loud. It wasn’t dramatic truth. It wasn’t romantic truth. It was the kind that lived in the body and made your throat tighten because it had been avoided for years.

“I’m just thinking,” Lauren said quietly.

Sarah’s brows knit. “About what.”

Lauren turned her head slightly, just enough to see Sarah properly. Sarah’s face was open. Not demanding. Just present.

Lauren swallowed.

“My so—” she started, and the word snagged. Caught between old life and new life.

She heard herself. She heard what it meant. She felt the implication roll through the room like a slow wave: I almost said ‘son.’

Sarah didn’t move. She didn’t pounce. She didn’t correct. She didn’t make it about language or ideology.

She simply watched Lauren with a steadiness that made Lauren’s chest ache.

Lauren cleared her throat once. “My daughter,” she finished, and her voice held, even if her eyes didn’t. “I’m thinking about my daughter.”

Sarah’s gaze softened—not pity, not sentimentality. Understanding. The kind that didn’t require explanation.

“Okay,” Sarah said, quiet. “Tell me.”

Lauren’s mouth tightened. She hadn’t expected the invitation to be so simple.

“She’s at that age,” Lauren said. “Where everything is becoming… loud. Schools, friends, social media. People watching her, weighing her, judging her.” She stared at the ceiling again, the words coming out in an unbroken line now that they’d started. “And I keep thinking I’m supposed to help her, but half the time I’m just… trying to keep her safe from things I can’t even see.”

Sarah listened like it mattered. Like Lauren wasn’t being indulgent by speaking.

Lauren let her hand rest on Sarah’s shoulder, fingertips idly tracing the warm line of her skin. Not possessive. Anchoring herself.

“And then tonight happened,” Lauren said, and heard the dryness in her own tone. “And I should feel… happy.”

Sarah’s mouth curved faintly. “You are happy.”

Lauren looked down at her. Sarah’s eyes were calm. Sure.

Lauren’s throat tightened again, irritation at herself flaring up because she hated being readable.

“I am,” Lauren admitted. “And that’s the problem.”

Sarah’s eyebrows lifted slightly. “Because?”

Lauren’s jaw flexed once. “Because happiness makes me imagine things.”

Sarah stayed still, letting the sentence breathe.

Lauren stared at the wall. “Like… what it would look like if my daughter grew up with more women around her who were steady. Who didn’t bargain with men for space. Who didn’t shrink.” Her mouth pulled into something that wasn’t quite a smile. “And then I think—God, Lauren, don’t be ridiculous. You’ve known Sarah for five minutes.”

Sarah’s laugh was soft and immediate. “Rude. It’s been longer than five minutes.”

Lauren’s mouth twitched despite herself.

Sarah shifted closer, pressing her cheek against Lauren’s chest again, as if to say: I’m still here. You didn’t talk me out of the room.

Then Sarah spoke carefully, choosing each word like it had weight.

“I’m not her mother,” Sarah said. “I’m not trying to be. I’m not trying to insert myself into your life like a solution.” She lifted her head again and met Lauren’s gaze. “But I can be… another woman who cares about you. And if she’s part of you, then I can care about her too.”

Lauren felt the sentence land with almost physical force.

Not because it was dramatic. Because it was clean. No claim. No conquest. No “I’ll be her new parent.” Just presence, offered without conditions.

Lauren’s eyes stung.

“Don’t,” she said, rougher than intended.

Sarah’s brows drew together. “Don’t what.”

“Don’t be… lovely,” Lauren said, and the attempt at humour failed halfway through.

Sarah’s face softened. “Too late.”

Lauren let out a breath that sounded like it had been trapped under her ribs for years. She turned her head and pressed her lips to Sarah’s hair, a brief kiss—pure instinct.

Sarah went still for a beat, then relaxed again.

Lauren swallowed. “When I almost said it,” she murmured, “you didn’t… make a thing of it.”

Sarah’s eyes stayed on hers. “Because it wasn’t about me.”

Lauren’s mouth tightened. “Most people would have—”

“Most people,” Sarah said gently, “don’t know how to stay with someone when something tender appears. They want to label it. Fix it. Win it.”

Lauren stared at her.

Sarah’s voice stayed quiet. “I’m not here to win you, Lauren.”

Lauren’s chest tightened in a way that felt like both relief and fear.

Sarah continued, steady. “I’m here because I like you. And because you deserve to be held without being managed.”

Lauren’s eyes went glossy. She hated it. She also didn’t stop it.

For a moment, the room was just breath and quiet and the soft weight of the sheet.

Then Lauren reached up and cupped Sarah’s jaw, thumb resting lightly near the corner of her mouth.

“You’re dangerous,” Lauren said, voice low.

Sarah’s mouth curved. “Yes. But not like that.”

Lauren huffed a small laugh, and it broke something open.

She leaned down and kissed Sarah—slow, deliberate, unhurried. Not hungry. Not performative. A kiss that said: I’m here. I’m not running. I’m letting this be real.

Sarah kissed her back with the same steadiness, the same care.

When they parted, Sarah stayed close, forehead resting lightly against Lauren’s.

“Do you want to talk more,” Sarah whispered, “or do you want to sleep.”

Lauren closed her eyes for a beat, feeling the warmth of Sarah’s breath, the safety of the question—two options, both acceptable, neither a trap.

“I want…” Lauren began, and felt herself smile in the dark at the absurdity of how hard it was to ask for what she wanted.

Sarah waited, patient.

“I want you to stay,” Lauren said simply.

Sarah’s smile was small and certain. “I’m staying.”

She kissed Lauren again—brief, tender—and settled back against her.

And as the room quieted, Lauren realised something with a clarity that startled her:

This wasn’t an escape.

This was what it felt like when someone made space for your whole life—the complicated parts, the mother parts, the hopeful parts—without asking you to shrink it down into something easier to love.

Lauren’s hand found Sarah’s in the dark.

Sarah’s fingers laced through hers, steady.

Outside, the city kept moving.

Inside, Lauren let herself believe—just for tonight—that the future didn’t have to be carried alone.

Robyn:

Before we go back to Celeste and Charlotte, I'd like to call in with the Wardrobe team, who are all at Celeste's place. Mara has decided that the work schedule was at a point where the department could afford to shut up shop for one day and help Charlotte and Celeste move to their new place in Melbourne. At first, Celeste doesn't want it but Sarah and Mara convince her it's just to help pack up, not the actual move (removalists will do that job). So, everyone is milling about in Celeste's old place and Charlotte's room is being packed up. And Sarah has some quips about Charlotte's fashion sense (as Sarah does). Celeste leaves Charlotte to fend for herself. Charlotte wisely asks Sarah: "So, what look SHOULD I go for?" Again, adjust as needed... if the language moves dangerously close to tropes.

Emily:

Scene — “One Day” (Celeste POV, past tense)¶

Mara announced it like a policy.

“We’re shutting for one day,” she said, standing in Celeste’s kitchen with her coat still on and her expression already bored of dissent. “Packing support. Not the move. Removalists do the lifting. We do the thinking.”

Celeste stared at her as if Mara had suggested they set fire to the ledger.

“We can’t shut,” Celeste said.

Mara didn’t blink. “We can.”

Lauren, seated at the table with her phone in hand, didn’t look up. “We’re ahead on the Christmas run. We’ve got buffer. If we don’t, it’s because we’re addicted to pressure, and that’s not a virtue.”

Celeste opened her mouth.

Sarah beat her to it, breezing in behind them with a tote bag and that air of cheerful menace she brought to any plan that involved other people’s discomfort.

“It’s one day,” Sarah said brightly. “Not a sabbatical. Not a spiritual retreat. Just women helping women do something boring. You’ll survive.”

Celeste’s jaw tightened. She hated it.

Not the help. The implication that she needed it. She’d built her life around being the one who didn’t.

“I can pack,” Celeste said.

Mara’s eyes narrowed. “You can. But you won’t. You’ll keep working until midnight and then panic-pack badly.”

Celeste scowled. “That’s not—”

“That’s exactly what you’ll do,” Lauren said, finally looking up, calm as a judge. “And then you’ll resent everyone for watching you do it.”

Sarah smiled as if she’d been offered dessert. “See? We’re doing this for your personality, love.”

Celeste looked at Charlotte.

Charlotte was leaning against the doorway with her arms folded, quietly amused in a way that made Celeste feel both adored and betrayed.

Charlotte lifted her eyebrows as if to say: They’re right.

Celeste exhaled, slow.

“Fine,” she said. “But it’s packing. Not moving. No one lifts anything that requires a spine.”

Mara nodded once. “Correct.”

Sarah clapped her hands together. “Right. Domestic invasion begins.”

The apartment filled the way it always did when women arrived with purpose: not loud, not chaotic—efficient.

Boxes appeared. Tape. Markers. Someone put music on low. Lauren claimed the kitchen like she’d always lived there and started organising a packing station with the same calm competence she used in Wardrobe.

Mara went straight to the linen cupboard and began folding with grim intensity, as if creasing fabric correctly was a moral position.

Sarah wandered in circles, making commentary like a sport, occasionally useful in the way people like Sarah were useful: she could lift morale without lifting a sofa.

Charlotte hovered at the edge of the corridor that led to her room, watching her own life become cartons.

It should have felt exposing.

Instead, it felt… strangely clean. Like a house being turned out before a new season.

Celeste found herself bristling anyway.

The place was intimate. It contained their routines. Their small agreements. Their softness.

She didn’t like it being handled.

“Celeste,” Lauren said from the kitchen, without looking up. “Go do something else.”

Celeste’s mouth tightened. “I’m fine.”

Lauren’s gaze lifted briefly—one of those looks that ended arguments without raising a voice. “You’ll hover, and then you’ll control, and then you’ll apologise, and we’ll all lose time. Go. Trust your team.”

Celeste’s instinct was to resist on principle.

Then she heard Charlotte’s earlier words in her head: Two pillars.

Celeste exhaled once and walked away before she could turn surrender into a debate.

She went to her own room and started sorting books into a box with more force than necessary.

Behind her, the apartment kept moving.

In the living room, Sarah had found Charlotte’s packing pile and was now holding up a pair of jeans with a look of theatrical despair.

“Oh, sweetheart,” Sarah said, voice loud enough to carry down the hall. “Do you own anything that isn’t… emotionally beige?”

Charlotte, sitting cross-legged on her bed with a box open beside her, looked up calmly. “Yes.”

Sarah held up another pair of jeans. “Where.”

Charlotte’s mouth twitched. “Those are black.”

Sarah gasped. “Black! Well. Sorry. Vogue.”

Mara walked past the doorway with a stack of folded towels and didn’t even glance in. “Don’t distract.”

Sarah ignored her. “You know what it is? Your wardrobe has the energy of a very polite fence.”

Charlotte blinked. “A fence.”

“Yes,” Sarah said. “Not hostile. Not inviting. Just… there. A boundary made of denim.”

Charlotte’s gaze flicked to the jeans in Sarah’s hands and then back to Sarah’s face. She didn’t look offended. She looked mildly curious.

“I like denim,” Charlotte said.

Sarah put a hand to her chest. “I’m not attacking denim. I’m attacking your refusal to have fun.”

Charlotte’s expression stayed even. “I have fun.”

Sarah’s eyes narrowed. “When.”

Charlotte thought about it, honestly. “When I’m not being observed.”

Sarah made a soft noise—half laughter, half understanding—because she knew exactly what Charlotte meant.

She sat on the edge of the bed, jeans still draped over her arm like a prop.

“Okay,” Sarah said, tone shifting. Less teasing. More real. “That’s fair.”

Charlotte’s shoulders loosened a fraction.

Sarah’s voice softened. “But here’s the thing. You can have ease without being invisible. You can have comfort without looking like you’re apologising for existing.”

Charlotte stared at her.

Sarah tipped her head. “That one landed.”

Charlotte hesitated. Then, because Charlotte had learned not to barter or flinch, she asked the clean question.

“So,” Charlotte said, quiet and steady, “what look should I go for?”

The room went still enough that Sarah blinked.

Because that wasn’t Charlie asking.

That was Charlotte.

Not pleading. Not eager to please. Simply… wanting a code.

Sarah’s eyes softened.

“Good,” Sarah said. “That’s a grown woman question.”

Charlotte waited.

Sarah shifted closer, elbows on her knees, suddenly serious in a way that made her feel older than her jokes.

“First,” Sarah said, “you don’t pick a look for other people. You pick it for you. But you can pick a strategy.”

Charlotte nodded once. “Strategy.”

Sarah held up a finger. “You already have one: neutral, functional, safe. It’s served you.”

Charlotte’s gaze held. She wasn’t defensive. She was listening.

Sarah held up a second finger. “But if you want to look like you belong in the city, you can move from ‘neutral’ to ‘intentional.’ That’s the shift.”

Charlotte frowned slightly. “Intentional how.”

Sarah gestured at Charlotte’s stack of clothes. “Same palette if you like it. Same comfort. But choose shapes that read like a decision. Not just ‘this was clean.’”

Charlotte looked down as if seeing her clothes as language for the first time.

Sarah continued, practical now. “Good jeans. Proper ones. A few tops that aren’t just… T-shirts that have given up.”

Charlotte’s mouth twitched. “My T-shirts are fine.”

“They are,” Sarah conceded. “But fine is not the same as intentional.”

Charlotte nodded slowly.

Sarah leaned back, warming to it. “Add one or two things that you like purely because you like them. A jacket with structure. A scarf that isn’t for warmth but for… you. Shoes that are comfortable but don’t look like you’re about to mow a lawn.”

Charlotte’s eyebrows lifted. “I don’t mow lawns.”

Sarah’s grin returned. “Exactly.”

Charlotte was quiet for a moment, then asked, cautiously, “And how do I… start.”

Sarah’s answer was immediate. “Small. One piece at a time. No makeover. No drama. We’re not inventing a new you. We’re just letting you be seen a little more.”

Charlotte’s throat worked once. She nodded.

“Okay,” she said.

Sarah’s voice softened again. “And if it feels wrong, you stop. This isn’t a project. It’s a permission.”

Charlotte looked at her, steady. “Thank you.”

Sarah blinked, then waved it away with a flick of her hand. “Don’t thank me. Thank Celeste. She’s the one who built the room you could ask that in.”

Charlotte’s gaze shifted toward the hallway, where Celeste was packing books with unnecessary force.

Then Charlotte looked back at Sarah and said, quietly, firmly, as if correcting the record.

“I built it too.”

Sarah’s smile turned sharp and delighted. “Yes, you did.”

In the kitchen, Lauren sealed a box and scribbled KITCHEN / GLASS on the side.

Mara passed through like a silent standard.

Celeste appeared in the doorway, arms full of books, and paused when she heard the tail end of Sarah’s conversation.

Charlotte met her eyes.

There was no apology in Charlotte’s face. No request for permission.

Just that calm, adult steadiness that had started to make Celeste’s chest hurt in the best way.

Celeste didn’t speak. She simply nodded once—approval, recognition, a silent good.

Then she carried the books to the stack of boxes and let her team keep doing what they were doing:

Helping, without turning it into theatre.

Building, without making it a debt.

And—quietly—making room for Charlotte to choose what she wanted to look like, not as a performance, but as a life.

Robyn:

Is this moving time a good venue for Lauren to work through her conflict with Charlotte being, Charlotte? I would propose she moves from the kitchen to the bathroom and as she packs up the medicine cabinet she sees the anti-androgens and estradiol gel... and freezes. And tears up? Or stops cold: can't go on? Wants to destroy the medications then realises she has a problem, not Charlotte, a real one.

Emily:

Yes — moving day is a fantastically believable venue for that conflict, because it’s one of the few times Lauren would be in close proximity to the infrastructure of Charlotte’s life (bathroom, drawers, the quiet evidence people don’t “announce”). It also suits Lauren’s character: she’s a process woman; she doesn’t get blindsided by feelings in a candlelit conversation. She gets blindsided by a label on a box.

Two cautions to keep it from drifting into trope or contrivance:

1) Consent and privacy (so it doesn’t feel like a “caught” moment)¶

If Lauren discovers the meds by rummaging through Charlotte’s private cabinet without invitation, it risks feeling like a violation that the story then asks the reader to forgive.

Easy fixes: - Charlotte asks Lauren to pack the bathroom (“Can you do the cabinet? Just put the medical stuff in its own box.”)
- or the meds are already sitting out in a small basket/tray to be packed (Charlotte’s competence), and Lauren sees them without “snooping.”
- or Celeste says, briskly, “Leave the meds. Those come with us,” and Lauren catches sight as she’s obeying.

That preserves the women-led standards: no gotcha, no shame.

2) The “destroy them” impulse is great… if it stays internal and becomes a mirror¶

It’s a powerful beat if it reads as: I had an ugly, controlling flash—and I’m not that person.
If Lauren actually reaches for them, it can become melodrama and makes her harder to respect. Better: the impulse appears like a stab, she goes still, then she chooses correctly.

A very Lauren way to show growth is: she doesn’t “accept” with a speech; she labels a box.

MEDICATION — KEEP WITH YOU (NOT REMOVALISTS)

That’s her love language: procedure.

Draft scene (Lauren POV, past tense)¶

Lauren had been in the kitchen long enough to forget that this apartment wasn’t hers.

Not in the sentimental way. In the practical way: she’d made a packing station, established a tape dispenser, started a pile for “don’t let removalists touch,” and her body had relaxed into competence.

Then she ran out of boxes.

“Bathroom,” Lauren called, mostly to herself, and went down the hall with an empty carton tucked against her hip.

The bathroom was tidy in that lived-in way Celeste and Charlotte had—nothing staged, nothing chaotic. Toothbrushes aligned. A hand towel that had been replaced because someone cared. A faint, clean scent that wasn’t trying to seduce anybody.

Lauren opened the medicine cabinet because that was where small items hid, and small items were always what got left behind.

She reached for the toothpaste.

Her hand stopped.

On the middle shelf, pushed back with deliberate neatness, were two pharmacy items: one box, one pump bottle. Labels. A name.

Charlotte’s name.

Lauren’s stomach dropped with the sudden, stupid force of it — not as a fact she didn’t know, but as a fact made physical. Evidence you could hold.

Her eyes flicked over the words she didn’t want to read and read them anyway.

Anti-androgens.

Estradiol gel.

For a moment she couldn’t move. Couldn’t even pretend she was moving.

Her throat tightened and she felt heat behind her eyes — the humiliating kind of emotion that came with maternal reflex, with grief that didn’t have a proper place to go.

A thought rose up, sharp and primitive.

Get rid of it.

Flush it. Throw it. Make it not real.

The impulse disgusted her so quickly she almost flinched from herself.

Lauren stared at the shelf and realised, with a cold clarity, that the problem wasn’t Charlotte.

The problem was Lauren’s own ancient wiring: if something changed, undo it. If something threatened the old story, destroy the evidence.

It wasn’t love. It was control wearing love’s clothes.

Lauren exhaled once, slow, as if she were steadying her hands at the cutting table.

This is not your cupboard. This is not your body. This is not your decision.

She closed the cabinet gently, the way you closed a door when you’d heard something private on the other side.

For a few seconds she just stood there, palms on the vanity, looking at her own face in the mirror like she might find the correct version of herself if she stared hard enough.

There was no correct version. There was only the choice, right now.

Lauren opened the cabinet again, more carefully this time, and lifted the items as if they were fragile, not because they were, but because the moment was.

She put them into the carton and immediately stopped.

No. Wrong box. Wrong chain of custody.

Lauren set the carton down, took a fresh one, and wrote in thick marker with the decisive, unromantic clarity she used at work:

MEDICATION — KEEP WITH YOU
NOT REMOVALISTS
NOT STRAYS

Then she placed the items inside, cushioned by a folded hand towel, and sealed the box.

The tape made its familiar ripping sound — ordinary, grounding.

She carried it out of the bathroom as if it weighed nothing, and yet it felt like carrying a truth through a house full of women who didn’t need her permission to live.

In the hallway, she paused.

Charlotte was in her room, folding a stack of plain tops into a suitcase with quiet concentration. Sarah was perched on the bed, commenting on everything like a sport. Celeste was down the hall, boxing books with unnecessary force.

Lauren walked straight to Charlotte and held the box out.

“Here,” she said, voice even.

Charlotte looked up, eyes sharp for half a second—assessing what Lauren knew, what she’d seen, whether this was about to become a problem.

Lauren didn’t give it oxygen.

She tapped the label once. “This stays with you. Not with the removalists.”

Charlotte’s gaze held hers.

Lauren kept her face calm, because she refused to make Charlotte manage her feelings.

After a beat, Charlotte nodded. “Thank you.”

Lauren’s throat tightened again, but she didn’t let it turn into a confession.

“Of course,” she said simply, and walked away before she could do something stupid like apologise for being human.

In the kitchen, she picked up a roll of tape and went back to work.

Her hands were steady.

Her eyes weren’t, quite.

But she was still standing.

And that, Lauren realised, mattered more than whether she felt pure about it in the moment.

If you want to deepen it one notch (without turning it into a speech), the next micro-beat could be Lauren stepping outside for thirty seconds, breathing hard, then coming back in and being more gentle with Charlotte for the rest of the day — not coddling, just… less armoured. That’s how you show the internal shift without preaching.

Want me to write that “thirty seconds on the balcony” beat, or do you prefer it stays entirely in action (box label + delivery) like above?

Robyn:

I would like to see that "30 seconds on the balcony" beat, please. That sounds genuine. And loving.

Emily:

Add-on beat — “Thirty Seconds” (Lauren POV, past tense)¶

Lauren took the roll of tape from the kitchen counter and carried it to the balcony as if she’d simply remembered something she needed outside.

No announcement. No drama. Just a small extraction from the noise.

The air hit her face—cooler than the apartment, faintly damp, with that city smell of concrete and eucalyptus and traffic that never fully slept.

She shut the sliding door behind her.

For a moment she stood with both hands on the railing, tape still in one fist, and stared down at the street as if she might find a simpler life down there. One where she didn’t have to keep revising the story in her head.

Her chest felt tight, too tight, as if her ribs had forgotten they could move.

Lauren breathed in once—shallow. Then again—deeper, forced. She hated that her body was doing this. Hated that she could run a business, negotiate consequences, handle a crowd, and still be ambushed by a label on a box.

She pressed her tongue against the back of her teeth and let herself name it, privately, because denial only made it louder.

I’m scared.

Not of Charlotte.

Of what it meant for her daughter. Of what it meant about the world. Of how cruel people could be when they scented difference. Of how easily love could become a target.

And underneath all of that, another fear she didn’t like admitting:

What if I don’t know how to be the mother she needs anymore.

The thought landed like a weight.

Lauren closed her eyes for a second and let her forehead rest against the cool metal of the railing.

Then—because she was Lauren—she forced the feeling into a shape she could use.

She didn’t need purity. She needed correctness.

My job is not to control her life. My job is to keep her safe enough to live it.

Charlotte was not a problem to solve. Charlotte was a person in her daughter’s world. A person who had survived becoming herself. A person who had learned to stand.

Lauren exhaled slowly and felt some of the pressure leave her chest.

Not all of it. Enough.

She looked down at the tape in her hand, absurdly grounding in its ordinariness, and almost laughed at herself. She was standing on a balcony clutching packing tape like it was a lifeline.

She took one more breath—deep, clean—and made a decision she could enact without speeches.

Be gentler. Not softer. Gentler.

Lauren opened the sliding door and stepped back into the apartment.

The noise met her again—boxes, markers, Sarah’s voice ricocheting down the hallway, Mara’s efficient silence. Ordinary women doing ordinary work.

Lauren walked past the kitchen and, as she passed Charlotte’s doorway, she paused.

Charlotte was folding again, quiet, focused, doing that competent thing she did where everything became neat without fanfare. Sarah was still perched on the bed, but her teasing had softened into something more like coaching.

Lauren didn’t interrupt. She didn’t hover.

She simply reached out and put her hand briefly on Charlotte’s shoulder—one light touch, no weight in it, no claim.

Charlotte looked up.

Lauren met her eyes and kept her voice flat, as if this was just another practical instruction.

“If you need anything,” Lauren said, “you ask. We’re here.”

Then she moved on before Charlotte could have to manage her face.

Behind her, she heard the faintest sound—Charlotte’s quiet exhale, the kind that said the touch had landed exactly where it was meant to.

Lauren returned to the kitchen, picked up a marker, and got back to work.

Her hands were steady.

And when Sarah called out a joke that would normally have made Lauren roll her eyes, Lauren found she didn’t have the energy to arm herself against it.

She just let the corner of her mouth lift.

Gentler, she thought.

Not softer.

Just… more human.

Robyn:

Lovely. Thank you. Before we go any further... let's regroup. Sarah and Lauren are slowly gravitating... together. Celeste and Charlotte are doing so, as well. Wardrobe is ticking along nicely. I am trying to think of which woman's life issues we haven't addressed yet. One issue that be uniquely Charlotte's is - as a result of the influencer's video - it comes out that Charlotte is a transwoman. A rather small-minded TERF (trans-exclusionary radical feminist: a person whose views on gender identity are considered hostile to transgender people, or who opposes social and political policies designed to be inclusive of transgender people) runs into Celeste and Charlotte, recognises Celeste and makes assumptions and accusations about Charlotte's ability for "shared experience: can't happen unless you have a uterus" ... something that does happen to transwomen. Do we go there?

Emily:

Yes — you can go there, and it can be one of the most thematically honest “Charlotte-specific” conflicts you’ve got… as long as you write it like your story writes everything else: stakes, standards, and consequence — not debate club.

Because the danger with TERF-confrontation scenes is they often become: - a sermon, - a caricature, - or (worst) a spectacle where the trans character is forced to “perform legitimacy.”

Your story’s strength is the opposite: women don’t litigate their humanity for entertainment. They set a boundary, correct the record only as needed, and move on with dignity intact.

Why it belongs in this story¶

  • The influencer video created a social ripple. So it’s realistic that someone feels entitled to “have a view.”
  • Charlotte’s unique issue isn’t “being trans” as a concept — it’s being treated as a topic in public when she’s just trying to live.
  • Celeste’s unique issue is: she will not allow her partner to be turned into an exhibit, and she’s skilled at clean social correction.

What makes it work (craft rules)¶

1) No uterus debates.
The TERF tries to drag them into “definitions.” Celeste refuses the frame.
The win is not “proving” anything. The win is: this conversation is not admissible.

2) Charlotte gets agency.
She either: - ends it herself, or
- explicitly chooses to let Celeste handle it, as a partnership decision (not “rescued”).

3) Consequences, not catharsis.
A boundary is set; if the person persists, they lose access: socially removed, asked to leave, barred from event, or simply iced out by women with standards.

4) One clean line that reclassifies the behaviour.
Like your “Not cute” moment. The TERF’s comments are treated as harassment, not “a controversial opinion.”

The scene I’d write (Celeste POV): “Not A Conversation”¶

They were leaving the café near campus—nothing glamorous, just coffee and a small bag of pastries—when Celeste heard her name said with the bright certainty of someone who believed recognition was permission.

“Celeste?”

Celeste turned.

A woman stood a few paces away, mid-forties maybe, linen jacket, hair cut to look effortless, eyes too sharp for a friendly encounter. The smile on her face didn’t reach her gaze.

Celeste’s mind filed it immediately: someone who wants proximity more than connection.

“Yes,” Celeste said, neutral.

The woman stepped closer—just enough to signal entitlement, not enough to be openly aggressive. “I thought it was you. From the program. Congratulations, by the way. Very impressive.”

“Thanks,” Celeste replied.

Charlotte stood beside her, calm, carrying the pastry bag, looking unbothered in the way she’d learned to look in public: not inviting, not shrinking, simply present.

The woman’s eyes moved to Charlotte.

Something changed in her expression, subtle as a seam going tight.

“And this is…” she began, drawing the word out.

Celeste didn’t offer anything. She waited.

The woman’s smile sharpened. “Your… partner?”

“Yes,” Celeste said, still calm. “Charlotte.”

The woman’s eyes flicked over Charlotte with that specific kind of appraisal Celeste hated—the one that pretended to be feminist concern while doing the oldest policing in the world.

“I see,” the woman said. “I just hope you understand what you’re doing.”

Charlotte’s posture remained easy, but Celeste felt the tiniest shift beside her—attention sharpening. Not panic. Readiness.

Celeste kept her voice even. “What I’m doing is buying bread.”

The woman’s smile didn’t break. “I mean… the politics of it. The—” She tilted her head, as if offering a lesson. “The shared experience. Women’s spaces. You know. Some things can’t be shared unless you’ve lived them.”

Celeste felt irritation rise—clean, controlled. The same irritation she’d felt at “cute.” The same kind of sorting exercise. Put women into categories. Decide who gets entry.

The woman continued, emboldened by her own sense of righteousness. “You can’t claim sisterhood if you don’t have a uterus. That’s just reality. It’s offensive to women who—”

Charlotte spoke then. Softly.

“Stop.”

Just one word.

It wasn’t a plea. It wasn’t an apology. It wasn’t even anger. It was a boundary placed on the ground like a line of tape.

The woman blinked, caught off guard by Charlotte’s lack of performance.

“I’m not talking to you,” she said, the cruelty slipping out.

Celeste turned her head slightly toward Charlotte—not to check permission, but to acknowledge the fact: we heard that.

Charlotte’s face stayed composed. Her hand tightened once on the pastry bag and then relaxed again, like she’d chosen not to spill the contents of her day into this stranger’s hands.

Celeste turned back to the woman.

“This isn’t a conversation,” Celeste said, mild as a weather report.

The woman scoffed. “Excuse me?”

Celeste’s voice stayed calm. “You’ve approached us in public, attempted to interrogate my relationship, and made biological assumptions about my partner. That’s harassment. Not feminism.”

The woman’s smile turned brittle. “Harassment? Don’t be dramatic. Women have a right to defend their spaces.”

Celeste nodded once, as if she’d just been given a useful piece of data.

“And I have a right to defend mine,” Celeste said.

The woman’s eyes narrowed. “So you’re saying you don’t care about women’s lived reality.”

Celeste didn’t bite.

She didn’t let the woman drag her into definitions.

She didn’t offer Charlotte up as evidence.

Instead she did what she always did: she corrected the frame.

“I care about women,” Celeste said. “Which is why I don’t tolerate women being treated as public property.”

The woman’s mouth opened.

Celeste raised a hand—not threatening, just final. “No. We’re done.”

She turned slightly toward Charlotte. “Ready?”

Charlotte met her eyes. There was no woundedness there. No collapse. Just an adult decision.

“Yes,” Charlotte said.

They began to walk.

Behind them, the woman called, louder now—performing for the sidewalk. “Typical! Men invading women’s spaces and women like you enabling it—”

Celeste stopped.

Not to debate.

To end it properly.

She turned back just enough that her voice would carry, calm and unmistakable.

“If you follow us,” Celeste said, “I will report you. If you contact my program, I will report you. And if you ever speak to my partner like that again, you will learn what consequences look like among women who mean what they say.”

The woman froze, startled—not by aggression, but by the quiet certainty of a woman who was used to enforcing standards.

Celeste didn’t wait for a response.

She turned and walked on.

Charlotte’s shoulder brushed hers once, brief and grounding.

A block later, when the noise of the street had swallowed the encounter, Charlotte spoke.

“Thank you,” she said.

Celeste didn’t look at her immediately. She kept her eyes forward. “For what.”

“For not making me explain myself,” Charlotte replied.

Celeste’s throat tightened, hot with something she refused to sentimentalise.

“I’m not interested in your humanity being up for discussion,” Celeste said. “It’s not a topic.”

Charlotte nodded once. “Good.”

They walked another few steps.

Then Charlotte added, quiet but firm, “Next time… I might end it sooner.”

Celeste glanced at her, and the pride she felt was sharp and clean.

“Good,” Celeste said. “That’s your right.”

Charlotte’s mouth curved faintly. “Also.”

Celeste waited.

“I still want the pastries,” Charlotte said, deadpan.

Celeste let out a small laugh—one breath of normality reclaimed.

“Good,” Celeste replied. “We’re not letting a stranger ruin bread.”

And they kept walking, not because the world was kind, but because their life didn’t require the world’s permission.

If you like this direction, the next beat (quiet, powerful) is the debrief at home:

  • Celeste asks, “Do you want me to handle it next time, or do you want to?”
  • Charlotte answers like an adult: “I want the option. I’ll decide in the moment.”
  • And then maybe Lauren hears about it later and has a real mother reaction: not ideology, not panic — “Who was it, where, and what’s our procedure?”

That’s your story’s signature: women solving it together without turning Charlotte into a debate.

Robyn:

Do I LIKE it? This is how to DO it! This is how to think whilst dealing with it. SOLID! Yes, I like it, Emily. Please continue with the debrief.

Emily:

Scene — “Debrief” (Celeste POV, past tense)¶

They didn’t talk about it immediately.

They carried the pastries home. They put the kettle on. They let the apartment absorb them again—shoes off, keys in the bowl, that familiar easing of shoulders that happened when the front door shut and the world stayed outside.

Celeste moved through the motions with deliberate calm because she didn’t want Charlotte to feel like their home had become a recovery room.

Charlotte set the bag on the counter, unpacked it without fuss, and laid the pastries on a plate as if this was still an ordinary afternoon. As if the sidewalk hadn’t tried to turn her into an argument.

Celeste watched her hands—steady, precise—and felt that hot, clean anger again. Not at Charlotte. Never at Charlotte. At the entitlement of strangers. At the way people thought feminism meant they were allowed to interrogate women in public.

Celeste poured tea and set a mug in front of Charlotte.

Charlotte sat. She didn’t collapse. She didn’t go brittle. She simply sat, shoulders square, as if she were waiting for the part where they did something useful with what had happened.

Celeste sat opposite her.

She didn’t ask, are you okay? Not first. It was too vague. Too passive. Too much like asking Charlotte to do emotional labour to reassure her.

Instead, Celeste asked the question that mattered.

“Do you want a procedure,” she said, “or do you want it handled case by case.”

Charlotte blinked once, then a faint smile touched her mouth. Not amusement. Recognition.

“That’s very you,” she said.

“Answer,” Celeste replied, dry.

Charlotte looked down at her tea for a moment. Then back up, steady.

“I want the option,” she said. “I’ll decide in the moment.”

Celeste nodded. “Good.”

Charlotte’s gaze held. “And you?”

Celeste didn’t pretend. “My first instinct is to end it for you. Fast.”

Charlotte’s face softened, almost imperceptibly. “Because you’re protective.”

Celeste’s mouth tightened. “Because I’m controlling.”

Charlotte’s eyebrows lifted.

Celeste went on, calm, surgical. “Because part of me thinks if I can shut it down quickly enough, I can stop you having to feel it at all.”

Charlotte was quiet. Then she said, gentle and precise, “You can’t stop me feeling it.”

Celeste exhaled once. “I know.”

Charlotte watched her for a beat, then nodded. “But you can stop them getting access.”

“Yes,” Celeste said. “That I can do.”

Charlotte’s fingers curled around her mug. “Today you did it properly.”

Celeste’s jaw tightened. “I’m still angry.”

“I know,” Charlotte said.

“I hate that she spoke to you like you were…” Celeste searched for the word and hated that it existed. “A category.”

Charlotte’s eyes stayed on hers. “She spoke to you like you were a traitor.”

Celeste’s mouth tightened. “Also that.”

A quiet beat.

Charlotte took a sip of tea and put the mug down with care. “I didn’t want to explain myself.”

Celeste nodded, once, sharp. “Good.”

Charlotte’s eyebrows lifted slightly. “Good?”

“Yes,” Celeste said. “Because you don’t owe strangers your interior life. You don’t owe them a biography. You don’t owe them—” She stopped herself before she turned it into a speech.

Charlotte’s gaze warmed. “Say it.”

Celeste held her eyes. “You don’t owe them womanhood.”

Charlotte’s throat worked once. She blinked, slow. Not fragile. Just… receiving.

“Thank you,” she said quietly.

Celeste looked away for a second, because she refused to get sentimental about something that should have been basic decency.

When she looked back, Charlotte had that steady, practical expression again—the one that said: now what.

Celeste understood. This was how Charlotte coped now. Not by shrinking. By structuring.

“Okay,” Celeste said. “If it happens again. What do you want.”

Charlotte didn’t hesitate. “I want to end it myself first.”

Celeste’s eyebrows lifted slightly.

Charlotte went on, calm. “One sentence. ‘Stop.’ If it continues, I walk. If they follow, you handle consequences.”

Celeste nodded slowly. “That’s… excellent.”

Charlotte’s mouth twitched. “I’ve been watching you.”

Celeste’s lips curved, brief and sharp. “Good.”

Charlotte’s gaze flicked to the phone charging in the hallway and then back.

“And if it’s somewhere with staff,” Charlotte added, “we involve them. Not because we need rescue. Because we’re not doing private fights in public.”

Celeste felt something like pride rise again—clean, unromantic, solid.

“Yes,” Celeste said. “Exactly.”

Charlotte hesitated, then added, quieter, “And if I freeze?”

Celeste’s chest tightened.

She kept her voice steady. “Then I handle it. And you don’t apologise for freezing.”

Charlotte nodded, relief loosening her shoulders.

Celeste leaned forward slightly. “And I want one more thing.”

Charlotte lifted her eyebrows. “Of course you do.”

Celeste ignored the tease. “If someone uses ‘feminism’ as a weapon against you, I don’t want you taking it personally as a measure of whether you belong.”

Charlotte’s gaze held hers. “I don’t.”

Celeste didn’t believe her. Not fully. Not yet. People could be brave and still be bruised.

“Charlotte,” Celeste said, quiet and firm. “They’re not the standard.”

Charlotte’s face softened. She nodded once. “You are.”

Celeste’s throat tightened. She hated how much that landed.

She reached across the table and covered Charlotte’s hand with her own—not possessive. Just contact.

“No,” Celeste said. “We are.”

Charlotte’s fingers tightened under hers, steady and equal.

A moment passed. The kettle clicked softly as it cooled. The apartment held them.

Then Charlotte said, deadpan, “Can I eat now.”

Celeste’s mouth twitched. “Yes.”

Charlotte reached for the pastries, tore one cleanly in half, and took a bite with quiet satisfaction.

Celeste watched her, anger still present in her body but no longer sharp enough to cut.

Charlotte swallowed and said, almost casually, “Next time I’ll say it sooner.”

Celeste nodded. “Good.”

Charlotte took another bite and added, “Also… I’m proud of you.”

Celeste blinked. “For what.”

“For not making it a debate,” Charlotte said. “For not making me prove anything. For treating it like what it was.”

Celeste’s jaw tightened again, but this time it was something else—something warmer under the anger.

“Harassment,” Celeste said.

Charlotte nodded. “Yes.”

Celeste let out a breath and finally let herself say the plain truth, without flourish.

“I’m proud of you too,” Celeste said. “For not shrinking.”

Charlotte’s mouth curved faintly, almost shy. “I didn’t.”

Celeste’s eyes stayed on her. “No. You didn’t.”

And that was the real outcome of the encounter: not that a stranger had been corrected, but that Charlotte had come home, made tea, eaten pastry, and chosen a procedure—like a woman who belonged to herself.

Tag-on micro-beat (optional) — Lauren hears¶

If you want to tie the women’s network in without making it melodrama, the next day Lauren can get the debrief as a two-sentence text:

Celeste: Had an incident yesterday. Stranger approached us and got hostile. Shut down. We’re fine.
Lauren: Where. Description. Do you want it logged.

That’s Lauren’s love language: procedure.

If you’d like, I can write that as a short follow-up scene too.

Robyn:

Please do. Mum needs to know. And process... this is one thing she was worried about, so not an experience to keep hidden from her.

Emily:

Scene — “Logged” (Lauren POV, past tense)¶

Lauren was at Wardrobe early, because she always was.

The place smelled faintly of fabric sizing and yesterday’s steam. The worktables were bare in that pre-shift quiet, the calm before hands and pins and decisions.

She was halfway through checking the courier schedule when her phone buzzed.

Celeste’s name.

Lauren’s stomach tightened before she even opened the message. Not panic. Recognition. Celeste didn’t text for nothing.

Celeste: Had an incident yesterday. Stranger approached us and got hostile. Shut down. We’re fine.

Lauren read it twice.

The word incident settled in her body like a stone. The kind you kept in your pocket so you didn’t forget what the world could do.

She typed back immediately.

Lauren: Where. Description. Do you want it logged.

The reply came fast.

Celeste: Near campus, outside the café on Swanston (midday). Woman, 40s, linen jacket, sharp tone. Recognised me, then targeted Charlotte. Tried the “shared experience/uterus” line. We ended it. She didn’t follow.

Lauren’s jaw tightened so hard her teeth ached.

Not because Celeste had handled it badly. Because Lauren had imagined it, for months, and imagining never quite prepared you for the reality of someone thinking they had the right to speak to your child like that in public.

Her child.

Even now, the word still landed with a small shock in her chest, like a lock turning.

Lauren stared at the screen. She could feel her hands wanting to do something—anything—to restore control. To make a call. To make a list. To make the world smaller.

She made herself breathe.

Then she typed, slower.

Lauren: I’m glad you told me. I’m not angry at you. I’m angry at her. Is Charlotte okay.

A pause.

Then:

Celeste: She’s okay. More steady than I was. We did a debrief and set procedure.

Lauren felt a strange, sharp warmth behind her eyes.

Because that was it, wasn’t it.

Charlotte wasn’t just surviving. She was learning how to live.

Lauren set the phone down on the cutting table and stood there for a moment, hands flat on the wood, letting herself feel what she needed to feel without letting it spill into the room.

Fear.

Rage.

And, threaded through it, something like pride that hurt.

Then she did what she always did when the world tried to get in: she made a process.

She pulled a small notebook from the drawer—one she used for “non-work risks,” the kind you didn’t put in the ledger because it wasn’t about garments, it was about people.

On a fresh page she wrote, in her neat, uncompromising hand:

INCIDENT — public confrontation
DATE: (yesterday)
LOCATION: Swanston St café area (midday)
AGGRESSOR: woman, 40s, linen jacket, recognised Celeste
CONTENT: “uterus/shared experience” line; targeted Charlotte
OUTCOME: ended, no follow, no escalation
FOLLOW-UP: procedure agreed (Charlotte first sentence, walk away; escalate to staff/police if followed)

She paused at the last line, pen hovering.

Then she added:

NOTE: Charlotte does not owe explanation. Prioritise exit + calm. No debate.

Lauren stared at the words until they steadied something inside her.

A door opened at the front of Wardrobe. Footsteps. Mara’s keys. The familiar weight of competence entering the building.

Mara appeared in the doorway, coat still on, eyes already scanning the tables.

“You’re early,” Mara observed.

“I’m always early,” Lauren said.

Mara’s gaze narrowed slightly. “You’re quiet.”

Lauren didn’t look up. “We had an incident yesterday. Someone approached Celeste and Charlotte in public. Targeted Charlotte.”

Mara went still in that way she did when her anger became pure focus.

“Who,” Mara said.

Lauren shook her head once. “Unknown. But it’s logged.”

Mara’s eyes flicked to the notebook. Her mouth tightened, approving without softness.

“Good,” Mara said.

Lauren swallowed. “Charlotte’s okay.”

Mara nodded once, like a decision. “We will make sure she stays okay.”

Lauren’s throat tightened. The simple sentence hit harder than any speech.

She picked up her phone again and typed one more message to Celeste—short, because that was how Lauren loved.

Lauren: Thank you for telling me. If it happens again, call me as soon as you’re safe. We’ll handle the rest.

She sent it, then stared at the screen for a beat longer than necessary.

Mara moved past her toward the back, already shifting into work mode, but she paused at the doorway and said, without turning around:

“And Lauren.”

Lauren lifted her eyes.

Mara’s voice was flat, absolute. “You don’t keep this to yourself.”

Lauren felt her chest tighten again—heat, gratitude, anger all braided together.

“I won’t,” she said.

Mara nodded once and disappeared down the hall.

Lauren slid the notebook back into the drawer, shut it carefully, and returned to the courier schedule like a woman who knew exactly what she was doing.

Because she did.

This wasn’t a secret to carry alone.

This was information.

And Lauren, for better or worse, had always known what to do with information: she turned it into procedure, and procedure into safety, and safety into a life her daughter could live without asking anyone’s permission.

Robyn:

At the moment, I am experiencing significant mental conflict. There are themes I'm wondering if we can address but then, another part of my brain says: "Don't. That's a trope." "How about..." "No, don't. That's also a trope." Our story has so sensitively touched and dealt with topics in such a positive, uplifting way that I feel its value to readers touched by these issues... the last thing I want to do is compromise it with anything inappropriate, regardless of how good the intention. Can you see my dilemma?

I'll give you a scenario: the Wardrobe team aree having a well-deserved weekend at the beach. Weather's lovely and warm, girls are in their bathing suits... all except Charlotte. I'm not going to belabour the point on why. In the back of my mind I'm thinking (as solution): "well, there's zero-depth vaginoplasty." That would allow bathing suit wear. But is it even a subject worth venturing into?

Emily:

Yes — I can see it exactly. You’ve built a story whose power comes from refusing the usual levers, so now your craft-brain is (correctly) flinching whenever a new beat smells like it might become “a thing.” That’s not overthinking; that’s you protecting the tone.

And your beach example is a perfect stress-test, because swimwear is one of the most common “trapdoors” into trope-land.

Here’s the good news: you don’t need surgery as a solution to make the scene truthful, meaningful, and Charlotte-specific. In fact, “zero-depth vaginoplasty” as a plot fix risks doing exactly what you fear: turning Charlotte’s body into the story’s problem to solve, and turning a women’s day into an anatomy subplot.

That doesn’t mean you can’t ever mention it. It means: if you do, it has to be for the right reason, at the right scale, and with the right lens.

When it’s worth going there (and when it isn’t)¶

It’s worth going there only if: - the story’s point is Charlotte’s agency about her body, - the decision arises from her desires (comfort, dysphoria relief, sexual selfhood, privacy), not to “unlock bikinis,” - and the scene is written as medical logistics + emotional truth, not transformation spectacle.

It’s not worth going there if: - it functions mainly as a wardrobe-enabler, - it appears as a neat “fix,” - or it pulls focus from women’s life and competence back into “what’s in her pants.”

So for this beach weekend: I’d say don’t solve it with surgery. Not here.

What to do instead (so it’s honest without trope)¶

You can keep the beach scene and make it one of your best “anti-fetish” moments by making it about choice, comfort, and women’s normalcy.

Three options that stay on-theme:

Option A: Charlotte wears what makes sense, and nobody makes it a “moment.”¶

  • She wears a rashie + board shorts, or a swim dress, or a surf suit.
  • It reads as pragmatic, stylish, Australian-beach normal.
  • Sarah gets one quip, then immediately respects the boundary.
  • The focus is: Charlotte participates.

This is the simplest and arguably the most powerful: she doesn’t have to be visually “typical” to belong.

Option B: “I’m not swimming today” — and it’s not a tragedy.¶

Charlotte opts out of swimming, but still fully belongs: - she does sunscreen patrol, - brings the fruit and iced coffee, - reads with her feet in the shallows, - takes photos of the group (not of bodies; of joy).

The beat becomes: women make room for different comfort levels without turning it into pity.

Option C: Charlotte wants to swim; the women solve it like engineers.¶

This is deliciously Wardrobe: - not “tucking talk,” not anatomical focus, - but “what garment design makes this doable?”

Mara: “We can make you a suit that’s correct for you.” Lauren: “Bounded, comfortable, secure.” Sarah: “Sexy is optional; secure is mandatory.” Charlotte: “I want to swim. That’s it.”

They design a custom swim solution (high-waist bottom + skirted layer + rashie; or a one-piece with built-in paneling). The point is women’s competence network — not genital anxiety.

If you still want to seed the surgery topic (without making it a trope)¶

You can plant a single, adult, non-graphic line that says: Charlotte has considered options, including surgery, and it’s not the beach that drives it.

Example, in a quiet conversation with Celeste later:

“I’ve read about options,” Charlotte said. “Some of them would change what I can wear. But I’m not making a medical decision for a swimsuit. I’ll make it when I’m making it for me.”

That tells readers: yes, it exists; no, we’re not fetishising it; no, it’s not a plot coupon.

My recommendation¶

For the beach weekend scene, go with Option C (women solve it like engineers) or Option A (rashie + shorts, no fuss). Both are: - real, - woman-forward, - Charlotte-specific, - and completely resistant to fetish framing.

If you want, I’ll write the beach scene in Celeste POV with: - warm, ordinary summer detail, - Sarah’s one-liner that doesn’t linger, - Mara/Lauren treating Charlotte’s outfit as normal, - and a tiny Charlotte beat of agency (“I’m swimming. I’m not discussing it.”) that lands like a quiet victory.

And I’ll keep it miles away from anatomy talk.

Robyn:

I like Option C because it is once again the team that solve it. And your solutions are so elegant. Thank you. Please proceed.

Emily:

Scene — “Swim” (Celeste POV, past tense)¶

They picked the beach the way women picked anything once they were competent: not for romance, for logistics.

Easy parking. Clean toilets. A café close enough to walk to barefoot if necessary. No heroics.

The weather was the kind that made Melbourne feel briefly forgiving—warm sun, a light breeze, water that looked cold but inviting, sky so clear it made you feel you’d wasted half your life indoors.

Celeste carried the big tote with the sunscreen, the fruit, and the boring supplies that kept a good day from turning stupid.

Mara arrived with a beach umbrella like it was a weapon.

Lauren arrived with a cooler like it was a promise.

Sarah arrived with a grin and a sarcasm reserve that could power a small town.

Charlotte arrived dressed like she always did lately: neutral, practical, correct. A long-sleeve rashie top and board shorts, hair tied back, a tote over one shoulder.

And still—Celeste felt the tiny tightening in her own chest when she saw it.

Not judgement. Not disappointment. Just that brief awareness of how much of womanhood was treated as a costume test, and how often Charlotte had been asked—by strangers, by the world, by old wiring in her own mind—to “pass” in ways that had nothing to do with being herself.

Sarah clocked it instantly, because Sarah clocked everything.

She leaned in toward Celeste as they walked across the sand and murmured, loud enough for Charlotte to hear, “I see we’ve gone with the ‘professional surfer’ aesthetic.”

Charlotte didn’t flinch.

She looked over her shoulder calmly and replied, deadpan, “It’s the only aesthetic I can commit to without lying.”

Sarah laughed—one bright burst—and then, crucially, she let it go. No lingering. No picking. No making Charlotte the day’s entertainment.

They set up near the dunes, where the wind was gentler and the view was wide.

Mara planted the umbrella with one clean shove and said, “Good.”

Lauren unpacked the cooler, already sorting bottles and containers as if she were laying out tools.

Celeste spread the towel and sat down, grateful for the quiet.

Charlotte stood at the edge of the group, looking at the water. Not anxious. Thinking.

Then she turned back toward them and said, with calm directness, “I want to swim.”

No apology. No preamble. No explanation.

Sarah’s eyebrows lifted. “Okay.”

Mara glanced up. “Then you swim.”

Lauren’s gaze sharpened in that mother way—protective without making it a performance. “Do you have what you need.”

Charlotte’s mouth tightened slightly, the old reflex trying to surface—don’t be a problem, don’t be a hassle.

Celeste watched it happen in real time.

And watched Charlotte choose differently.

“I have enough,” Charlotte said. Then, after a beat, “But I don’t have… ease.”

The word landed cleanly.

Not shame. Not drama. A practical request for a better design.

Mara sat back on her towel and looked at Charlotte as if Charlotte had just asked a sensible technical question.

“Come here,” Mara said.

Charlotte walked over and crouched beside her.

Mara pointed at Charlotte’s outfit with two fingers, as if drawing a pattern line in the air. “What you’re wearing is safe. It’s not wrong. But it reads like armour.”

Charlotte nodded once. “Yes.”

Mara’s mouth tightened in what Celeste had learned to recognise as Mara’s version of kindness. “We can make something that reads like choice.”

Sarah plopped down cross-legged and leaned in, suddenly delighted by the prospect of a problem that could be solved with craft.

“Oh, thank God,” Sarah said. “Because I was about to start an intervention for your relationship with board shorts.”

Charlotte’s eyes flicked to hers, faintly amused. “You can intervene after I swim.”

Lauren reached into the cooler, pulled out a bottle of water, and offered it to Charlotte without comment.

Charlotte took it, drank, and steadied herself—not because she was nervous, because it was warm and she was being sensible.

Celeste sat quietly, letting them do what they did best: turn a vulnerability into a plan without turning it into a spectacle.

Mara’s tone remained level. “What do you want it to do.”

Charlotte didn’t hesitate. “Stay where it’s meant to stay.”

Sarah nodded solemnly. “Secure. The sexiest adjective.”

Charlotte’s mouth twitched. “Secure.”

Lauren added, calm and concrete, “And you need to be able to move. Walk out of the water without tugging.”

Charlotte’s gaze flicked to Lauren. “Yes.”

Mara held up one finger. “So: built-in structure. Not visible. Not a story.”

She held up a second. “Coverage that reads normal. Not apologetic.”

Third. “Comfort. If it hurts, it’s wrong.”

Charlotte’s shoulders loosened with each line, like her nervous system understood this language.

Celeste felt something warm rise in her chest. Not romance. Pride. This was their culture: standards, not theatre.

Sarah tilted her head. “What about those swim dresses. The ones with the little skirt. Very ‘I’m here to enjoy myself and also I’m not discussing it.’”

Mara made a small sound that might have been agreement. “A skirted layer works. But not frou-frou. Clean line.”

Lauren nodded. “And the under-layer can be engineered. We do that all day.”

Charlotte looked between them, eyes steady, the corner of her mouth lifting slightly as if she couldn’t quite believe how simple it was when women treated it as normal.

“So,” Charlotte said quietly, “you can… make it.”

Mara’s eyes narrowed. “Of course we can make it.”

Sarah leaned in. “Charlotte, love, we could make you a swimsuit that survives a cyclone and a human rights debate at the same time.”

Charlotte laughed softly, surprised by herself.

Celeste watched the laughter appear and felt her own chest loosen.

Mara gestured at Charlotte’s rashie. “For today, you swim in this. It’s fine. You don’t need permission from fabric.”

Charlotte nodded. “Okay.”

Then Charlotte looked at Celeste—not seeking approval, just checking in like a partner.

Celeste met her gaze and gave a small, clean nod.

Charlotte stood, slipped her board shorts down just enough to secure the waistband properly—no drama, no lingering—and then she walked toward the water with an unhurried steadiness that made Celeste’s throat tighten.

Sarah called after her, cheerful and loud, the way Sarah was when she wanted to make something normal by treating it as normal.

“Don’t drown! I’m not doing CPR on sand. It’s undignified.”

Charlotte lifted one hand in acknowledgement without turning around.

Mara watched her go and then said, almost to herself, “We’ll do a fitting next week.”

Lauren nodded. “After hours. Quiet.”

Sarah grinned. “I’ll bring the brutal honesty.”

Celeste’s mouth twitched. “You always do.”

They sat in the sun and listened to the small roar of the surf.

A moment later, Charlotte waded in. The water took her ankles, her calves, her knees. She didn’t flinch. She didn’t rush. She entered it the way she entered her life now: deliberately.

When she was waist-deep, she turned back once.

Not to ask if she was allowed.

To share the moment.

She lifted her chin slightly, a private little “I’m doing it,” and Celeste felt something tender and fierce bloom in her chest.

Charlotte dove forward and started swimming.

She wasn’t graceful in the polished, feminine way people expected women to be. She was efficient. Stronger than she looked. Calm.

She moved through the water like someone who belonged in it.

Sarah shaded her eyes and watched, then murmured, half to herself, “Look at her. Bloody hell.”

Mara’s voice was flat. “Yes.”

Lauren’s face softened—just for a second—like a mother watching her child do something ordinary that used to feel impossible.

Celeste sat with the warmth on her shoulders, the salt in the air, and the sound of her team behind her, and felt the quiet truth of it:

Nothing about this was a performance.

It was women making room.

It was women making solutions.

And it was Charlotte, out there in the water, not as an exception, not as a project, not as a debate—

just as herself, doing what she wanted, because she wanted it.

When she eventually came back, dripping and breathing hard, Sarah tossed her a towel like it was the most ordinary thing in the world.

Charlotte caught it and smiled—small, real.

“I swam,” she said.

Celeste nodded, voice warm without being sentimental. “Yes, you did.”

Charlotte sat down among them, wrapped in the towel, and Mara—already thinking three steps ahead—picked up her phone and said, “I’m drafting a pattern.”

Sarah leaned in, delighted. “Make it cyclone-proof.”

Lauren’s mouth curved. “Make it hers.”

And Celeste watched Charlotte’s face as those words landed.

Not because of the swimsuit.

Because of the belonging.

You could drop it in as the closing beat, almost tossed off—which is what makes it land:

Sarah flicked sand from her calf, glanced at Charlotte with a grin, and said, “Right. Just so we’re clear—this is not the swimsuit competition of some beauty pageant.”

Charlotte, towel around her shoulders, looked up with that calm steadiness she’d earned. “Good,” she said. “Because I didn’t come here to be judged.”

Emily:

Your instinct is excellent, and the direction is absolutely on-theme: governance-as-love. That’s your story’s signature—women building systems that hold people when feelings get too big.

On the melodrama question: the idea is perfect; the phrasing needs discipline.
“Without you, Mara, there never would have been an ‘us’” is emotionally true, but it risks landing like a banner unfurled at a surprise party.

You can get the same punch with one notch less declarative and one notch more Wardrobe:

  • “You built this. You don’t get to pretend you’re not needed.”
  • “We’re not your employees right now. We’re your structure.”
  • “Stop trying to carry it alone. That’s not the standard you taught us.”
  • “This ‘us’ is not optional, Mara. You made it policy.”

That last one is very you.

How to scale Wardrobe without losing the tone¶

Governance beats that feel like story (not business lecture)¶

1) Capacity ceiling becomes governance, not guilt.
Mara hates “growth for growth’s sake.” Celeste brings models and numbers. Lauren brings risk framing. Sarah brings morale and “culture translation.”

2) New roles appear because the work demands them.
- Celeste: external-facing strategy, finance, contracts, supplier negotiation.
- Lauren: operations lead—rosters, procurement, policy enforcement, incident logging, HR discipline.
- Mara: creative director + standards authority (but increasingly forced to delegate).
- Sarah: client comms / front-of-house / culture guard. She’s the one who says the hard thing with a smile, and it lands.

3) They formalise “standards” into documents.
Not bureaucracy porn—just one or two artifacts that show maturity: - QC checklist v2
- “No filming” policy poster becomes part of onboarding
- Courier protocol
- A “sign-off rule” (nothing ships without two names)

These are narrative gold because they’re love made boring.

Mara’s health issues: what works without melodrama¶

The best Mara-health arc is not “collapse on the floor.” It’s small failures of her usual perfection—which scares her more than pain.

Pick something plausible and unglamorous: - migraines (light sensitivity, nausea, rage at needing dark)
- arrhythmia/palpitations (makes her sit, hate sitting)
- blood pressure issues (headaches, dizziness)
- autoimmune flare / fatigue (hands aching, dropping pins, needing breaks)

You don’t need diagnosis on-page. In fact, it’s stronger if it stays at the level of: Mara’s body is no longer obeying her standards.

Mara’s predictable response (very in-character)¶

  • She tightens her grip.
  • She refuses help.
  • She becomes sharper because vulnerability makes her furious.
  • She tries to “work through it,” which threatens quality.

And that is how the team gets leverage: not “we love you,” but “this will break the work.”

The rally that doesn’t feel sentimental¶

Make them rally the way they do everything: procedure-first.

  • Celeste quietly institutes a “two-signature rule.”
  • Lauren reshuffles rosters and blocks Mara from certain tasks “because risk.”
  • Sarah handles clients to reduce Mara’s contact load.
  • Charlotte (if she’s still in Wardrobe) becomes the “wear-test pillar,” taking pressure off design iteration.

Mara hates it… until she realises they’ve done it correctly. They didn’t take her power; they preserved her legacy.

A scene kernel you can use: “Two Signatures” (Celeste POV or Lauren POV)¶

Set-up: They’ve had a near-miss—an item almost ships with a flaw because Mara pushed through a headache and missed a stress point. Mara catches it last second, furious. Not at herself (outwardly). At everyone else (because that’s safer).

The team response: quiet governance.

Sample beat (Lauren POV, tight and real)¶

Mara’s hand hovered over the ledger, pen poised, jaw set like she could stab the page into compliance.

“This doesn’t leave the building,” Mara said. Her voice was flat, but her eyes were bright in a way Lauren recognised now—too much pain, too much stubbornness.

“I know,” Lauren replied.

Mara’s pen scratched hard across the paper: FAILURE POINT: SIDE SEAM PULL.
She wrote like she was punishing the garment.

Celeste stood in the doorway, phone in hand, calm as a spreadsheet. “I’m adding a sign-off rule.”

Mara didn’t look up. “No.”

Celeste’s voice stayed even. “Two signatures. Every shipment. You and one other.”

Mara’s pen stopped.

“That’s bureaucracy,” Mara said, and the contempt was automatic.

Lauren didn’t raise her voice. “It’s continuity.”

Mara finally looked up, eyes sharp. “You think I’m failing.”

Lauren met her gaze and didn’t blink. “I think you’re human.”

A beat.

Mara’s mouth tightened. “I don’t have time to be human.”

Sarah, leaning against the wall with her arms folded, said lightly, “Well, that’s tragic, because your body didn’t get the memo.”

Mara shot her a look that could have cut glass.

Sarah held it, unafraid. “I’m not being cute. I’m being accurate.”

Celeste stepped closer, still calm. “This isn’t about you being weak. It’s about the business surviving your bad days without lowering standards.”

Mara’s nostrils flared. “My standards don’t bend.”

Lauren tapped the ledger once. “Then let us carry them.”

Silence.

Mara looked from one face to the next. Not moved by sentiment—moved by the fact they were right and they’d framed it properly.

Finally, Mara said, coldly, “Who’s the second signature.”

Lauren didn’t hesitate. “Me.”

Celeste’s voice came right after, crisp. “And me on contracts. Sarah on client-facing exceptions.”

Sarah lifted her hand in a mock oath. “I vow to be terrifyingly polite.”

Mara stared at them, jaw tight, and for a moment Lauren thought she might explode.

Instead Mara looked down at the ledger, wrote one new line with brutal neatness:

SHIP ONLY WITH TWO SIGN-OFFS.

She pushed the book across the table toward Lauren like she was surrendering nothing.

Lauren signed beneath it.

Mara’s voice was low. “Don’t make it a big thing.”

Lauren’s answer was softer than usual, and that was how you’d know it mattered.

“We won’t,” she said. “We’ll just do it right.”

And Sarah—unable to resist, but careful not to ruin it—added, almost gently:

“You built the ‘us,’ Mara. We’re just finally acting like it.”

Mara didn’t respond.

But she didn’t take the rule back, either.

—

That’s the emotional punch without melodrama: Mara’s acceptance is procedural. Which is exactly who she is.

If you want, next I’ll outline a 3–4 scene mini-arc for “Scaling + Mara Health” (each scene with a clear purpose and a key line), so you can drop them into your draft in whatever order suits your pacing.

Robyn:

Yes please. This is a solid team working together approach. Of course, as with all teams, there are mini-bumps here and there, but overall they all follow the same rules / policies... to protect everyone. Including Mara.

Emily:

Here’s a tight 4-scene mini-arc you can slot into the Wardrobe timeline. It keeps the focus on governance-as-care, gives each woman a specific role in scaling, and lets Mara’s health pressure the system without melodrama. I’ll build in “mini-bumps” that feel real but don’t fracture the culture.

Mini-Arc: Scaling + Mara Health¶

(4 scenes, each with: purpose, friction, key beat, and a carry-forward change)

Scene 1 — “Capacity Ceiling”¶

POV: Celeste (or Lauren)
Purpose: Establish that growth is real and they need governance, not heroics.
Friction: Celeste brings numbers; Mara hears “loss of control.” Sarah hates meetings but keeps it human.
The beat: Celeste presents a simple model: orders rising, QC risk rising; proposes a capacity ceiling + selective outsourcing only if QC is enforceable.
Mini-bump: Mara snaps: “Outsourcing is where standards die.” Sarah needles: “So don’t outsource standards.”
Key line (Celeste): “We’re not scaling output. We’re scaling correctness.”
Outcome change: They agree to a Capacity Ceiling Policy: if orders exceed X, they pause intake or add pre-approved overflow with Wardrobe QC sign-off.

Scene 2 — “Near-Miss”¶

POV: Lauren
Purpose: Show Mara’s health as small failures of perfection, and why the system must adjust.
Friction: Mara pushes through symptoms and misses something tiny; Lauren catches it, but it scares her.
The beat: A garment almost ships with a hidden stress flaw. Mara catches it at the last moment (or Lauren does), and Mara is furious—at the garment, at time, at weakness.
Mini-bump: Mara lashes out at “distraction” or “sloppiness,” and Sarah fires back with one line that’s funny but edged.
Key line (Lauren): “This isn’t about blame. It’s about chain of custody.”
Outcome change: Lauren quietly starts Incident Logging v2 (internal): near misses count as data, not shame.

Scene 3 — “Two Signatures”¶

POV: Lauren or Celeste
Purpose: Turn love into policy; show Mara’s resistance and eventual acceptance through procedure.
Friction: Mara calls it bureaucracy; Celeste frames it as continuity; Sarah keeps it from going cold; Lauren doesn’t blink.
The beat: They introduce the Two-Signature Rule for shipments and critical QC, plus a defined exception process.
Mini-bump: Mara demands to be the sole authority; Celeste refuses the bottleneck: “Single points of failure are not romantic.”
Key line (Celeste): “A standard that depends on one person isn’t a standard. It’s a risk.”
Outcome change: A new governance artifact exists: SHIP ONLY WITH TWO SIGN-OFFS in the ledger + a QC checklist attached to every order batch.

Scene 4 — “The Slip”¶

POV: Celeste (or Charlotte, if you want it to land quietly)
Purpose: Make Mara’s health undeniable; show the team rallying without turning it into pity or a lecture.
Friction: Mara tries to hide it. The team refuses the secrecy—not invasively, but firmly.
The beat: Mara has a visible “slip”: dizziness, a hand tremor, a migraine aura—something that makes her stop mid-task. She tries to shrug it off.
Mini-bump: Mara snaps: “I’m fine,” and Sarah’s humour fails on purpose—she goes serious. Lauren steps in with calm authority.
Key line (Sarah, quiet): “We’re not asking. We’re staffing.”
Outcome change: They impose a Mara Load Rule (framed as quality control, not “care”):
- Mara no longer does late nights alone.
- Design sign-off has time windows.
- One day per week is non-production for Mara (research / painting / recovery) with zero guilt.

Closing emotional line (not melodramatic):
- Lauren: “You built the ‘us.’ Now you live in it.”
or
- Celeste: “Your legacy is a system, Mara. Let it work.”
or
- Mara, grudging: “Fine. But don’t get sentimental.”
- Sarah: “Wouldn’t dream of it.” (and she means it)

The mini-bumps (so it feels like a real team)¶

These are small conflicts that show personality without threatening the culture:

1) Mara vs. Outsourcing: Mara treats it as betrayal; Celeste frames it as controlled overflow with enforceable QC.
2) Sarah vs. Meetings: Sarah jokes through tension; Lauren snaps once: “Jokes after decisions.” Sarah backs off—she respects command.
3) Celeste vs. Control Instinct: Celeste tries to “fix” too much too fast; Lauren reminds her: “Governance, not domination.”
4) Lauren vs. Protective Rage: Lauren wants to ban everything risky; Charlotte says, calmly: “Living includes risk. We manage it.”

Optional 5th “bridge” scene (if you want Sarah’s role to deepen)¶

Scene 5 — “Front Door”¶

POV: Sarah
Purpose: Sarah becomes the culture guard, not just the quip machine.
Beat: Sarah turns away a pushy client / influencer / “curious” person with terrifying politeness, protecting Mara’s bandwidth and the team’s focus.
Key line (Sarah): “We’re booked. We’re private. And you’re done.”
Outcome: Sarah is formally given Front-of-House Authority (yes, even over Mara in client moments).

How this sets up the next phase¶

After these scenes, you can cleanly pivot into: - Celeste stepping into external strategy (partnerships, contracts, supplier audits), - Lauren becoming operations lead in practice (not just “mum energy”), - Sarah becoming gatekeeper and morale (with real authority), - Charlotte moving into QC/wear-testing as a pillar, - Mara confronting the reality that her standards can survive without her hands doing everything.

If you want, I’ll write Scene 1 (“Capacity Ceiling”) in full prose next, with the meeting feeling like your world: not corporate fluff—women being precise, a bit sharp, and ultimately aligned.

Robyn:

Please write scene 1.

Emily:

Scene 1 — “Capacity Ceiling” (Celeste POV, past tense)¶

Celeste didn’t call it a meeting.

She called it a table.

That was all Wardrobe ever really did: put the truth on a table and make people look at it until it stopped being scary.

They’d cleared the cutting surface after lunch. Pattern paper rolled up, pins swept into their tin, chalk dust wiped away. The ledger sat at one end like a judge. A laptop sat at the other like an irritant.

Mara stood with her arms folded, already radiating dissatisfaction at the very concept of numbers.

Lauren sat with a notepad and a pen, posture calm, eyes focused, the kind of readiness that made Celeste feel both grateful and faintly challenged.

Sarah leaned against the doorway with a coffee and an expression that suggested she would rather be anywhere else, but would be useful anyway because she was loyal in the only way that mattered: she showed up.

Charlotte hovered near the back of the room, quiet, listening—present enough to count, not so present she felt like she was auditioning.

Celeste set her laptop down and took a breath.

“Okay,” she said. “This won’t take long.”

Sarah immediately lifted her coffee. “Famous last words.”

Celeste didn’t look at her. “If you interrupt, I’ll assign you spreadsheets.”

Sarah made a face like she’d been threatened with violence.

Lauren’s mouth twitched. “She will.”

Mara’s voice was flat. “Proceed.”

Celeste clicked a simple spreadsheet open. No fancy graphs. Just a list. Orders. Dates. Hours. QC time. Returns.

“The US winter orders are not a blip,” Celeste said. “They’ve become a season.”

Mara’s eyes narrowed. “We’ve always had seasons.”

“Not like this,” Celeste replied. “We’ve doubled the run rate compared to last year, and the lead time has tightened. It’s good. It’s real. But it changes our risk profile.”

Lauren nodded once, already tracking.

Sarah’s eyebrows lifted. “Risk profile. Listen to you.”

Celeste kept going. “Right now we’re managing it the old way.”

Mara didn’t blink. “The correct way.”

“The heroic way,” Celeste corrected, calmly. “Late nights. Adrenaline. Everyone doing two jobs. It’s not sustainable.”

Mara’s jaw tightened. “Sustainable is a word people use when they want permission to do less.”

Lauren’s pen paused. She didn’t look up yet, but Celeste could feel her attention sharpen.

Celeste didn’t rise to the provocation. She’d learned that with Mara, the fastest route wasn’t winning an argument—it was building an undeniable frame.

“This is not about doing less,” Celeste said. “It’s about controlling standards when volume rises.”

She turned the laptop slightly so they could all see.
