She intuitively understands the disadvantage in terms of childhood reinforcements Charlotte never had, which would now present a massive challenge from a personal acknowledgement perspective. If you can't even look at yourself in the mirror to see yourself clearly, how can you ever hope to be a comfortable, engaging, fulfilled woman: can't happen.

Emily:

Companion Beat — “Restraint” (Lauren POV, past tense)¶

Lauren noticed it because Lauren noticed everything.

Not in a nosy way—she wasn’t that sort of mother—but in the way a woman who had spent years reading small signals could read a room without being told what was wrong.

They were at Celeste’s place, mid-pack. Boxes taped. Marker scrawls in Celeste’s neat hand: KITCHEN, BOOKS, WARDROBE—DO NOT CRUSH. Sarah was somewhere in the lounge making commentary about how nobody owned enough “boring socks.” Mara was seated at the table with a mug and a grim expression, supervising without admitting she was supervising. Celeste moved between tasks with that composed, managerial efficiency that made Lauren quietly proud and quietly irritated, because it meant Celeste was still trying to carry too much.

Charlotte had come out of her room with an armful of folded clothes, face calm, posture steady. She set them in a box without fuss.

And then she walked past the hallway mirror.

She didn’t recoil. She didn’t flinch.

She simply… didn’t use it.

Her eyes dipped away by habit, as if the mirror were an obstacle rather than a tool. Like eye contact with herself would be immodest. Like looking would make something real that she wasn’t ready to claim.

Lauren felt the recognition hit in her chest.

She’d seen that avoidance before, in different forms—girls who’d been criticised too early, women whose bodies had been public property, teenagers taught to treat their reflection as a problem to solve.

But this was different.

This was the absence of a whole scaffolding that most women didn’t even realise they’d been given.

Lauren had watched her own daughter grow up with constant mirroring. Not always healthy mirroring—sometimes too much, sometimes too little—but it was there: clothes chosen, hair brushed, photos taken, comments made, compliments and corrections and warnings and rituals that said, over and over: this is you, this is your body, this is your face, this is how the world sees you, and this is how you learn to see yourself back.

Charlotte hadn’t had that.

Not as a girl.

Not as a child.

She’d had fragments—skills, habits, a mother who taught competence and respect, a kind of quiet disgust at certain masculine inevitabilities that spoke to an internal mismatch—but he’d still moved through childhood without being invited into the ordinary, repetitive, sometimes annoying but ultimately formative business of girlhood.

And now, as Charlotte, she was expected to be fluent.

Lauren watched Charlotte’s eyes slide past the mirror again, and a simple, brutal thought formed in her mind:

If you can’t look at yourself, you can’t fully arrive in yourself.

Not long-term.

Not comfortably.

Not in the steady, engaging way that made a woman feel at home in her own skin.

And Lauren felt something else too—an ache that had nothing to do with ideology and everything to do with mothers:

I can’t give her that childhood.

It’s gone.

There was no going back to four years old with hairbrushes and dress-ups and the gentle, relentless reinforcement that built a girl’s internal map.

You couldn’t retroactively replace those years.

You could only build a new structure, now, with adult consent and adult dignity.

Lauren’s mouth tightened.

She wanted—instinctively—to say something. To fix it. To offer a solution like a bandage.

But she knew better.

Because Charlotte didn’t need another woman taking her over.

She didn’t need mothering that erased her adulthood.

She needed something harder and more respectful: space to grow without being watched growing.

So Lauren did what she’d been learning to do in this strange, beautiful network of women: she chose restraint.

She watched Charlotte set the box down, straighten the tape, and move on.

Then Lauren shifted her own body slightly, so she was no longer standing in a position where Charlotte might feel observed.

She turned her attention to the packing list instead, letting Charlotte’s moment stay private.

Still—Lauren couldn’t leave it entirely unattended.

She waited until Charlotte had disappeared back into her room, then walked quietly into the kitchen where Celeste was rinsing cups and moving them into a box with brisk competence.

Celeste looked up. “You need something?”

Lauren kept her voice neutral. “Not for me.”

Celeste’s expression sharpened, immediately attentive. “What is it.”

Lauren didn’t soften it into comfort. She didn’t dramatise it either. She chose the language Celeste responded to: clear, useful, actionable.

“She’s avoiding mirrors,” Lauren said.

Celeste froze for half a beat.

Lauren continued, calm. “Not in a vanity way. In a contact way. Like she doesn’t… use her own reflection.”

Celeste’s eyes narrowed slightly, absorbing.

Lauren added, quietly, “It’s not strange. It’s understandable.”

Celeste’s jaw tightened. “Because she didn’t have—”

“The years,” Lauren said, and felt the sting of the phrase as she spoke it. “The reinforcement. The boring repetitions. She didn’t get to be a little girl.”

Celeste looked down at the cup in her hand, and Lauren saw anger flare in her—anger at the world, at loss, at the fact there were gaps you couldn’t fix with policy.

Lauren held her gaze when it came back.

“But,” Lauren said, precise, “it can be built now. Carefully. With dignity.”

Celeste’s eyes sharpened. “How.”

Lauren exhaled once. “Not by me.”

Celeste blinked.

Lauren’s voice stayed firm. “If I try to intervene, it becomes a mother scene. It becomes loaded. She will either comply to please me or resist to protect herself. Neither helps.”

Celeste’s mouth tightened. She hated being told what not to do, which was exactly why Lauren said it.

“So,” Lauren continued, “if you do something, do it like Wardrobe. A small practice. A habit. No sentiment. No performance.”

Celeste stared at her, and Lauren saw the moment of understanding click into place. Celeste didn’t need advice on femininity. She needed permission to treat self-recognition like governance: structured, repeatable, kind without softness.

Celeste nodded once, slow. “Two minutes.”

Lauren’s mouth twitched. “Exactly.”

Celeste’s expression tightened, something almost raw under the competence. “It scares me.”

Lauren didn’t pretend she didn’t understand. “Good,” she said quietly. “That means you’re taking it seriously.”

Celeste’s eyes stayed on hers. “And you’re… okay.”

It wasn’t a question, not exactly. It was Celeste noticing something in Lauren too—how the word daughter had started to settle in Lauren’s mouth like truth.

Lauren felt her throat tighten.

She didn’t look away.

“I’m learning,” Lauren said. “That’s all.”

Celeste nodded once, accepting it as the only answer Lauren would give.

Lauren picked up the packing list again and moved to the next box, because that was how she kept herself steady: hands doing useful things while her heart adjusted.

As she passed the hallway mirror, she caught her own reflection—older face, tired eyes, a woman who had been forced to become braver than she’d planned.

She held her own gaze for a beat.

Then she looked away and kept moving.

Not because she couldn’t stand to see herself.

Because this wasn’t her moment.

It was Charlotte’s.

And Lauren, finally, was learning that the most motherly thing she could sometimes do was not to rush in with her love—
but to trust the structure that would teach her daughter how to stand on her own.

Robyn:

I'm going to use this as a flashback - since Celeste and Charlotte are now in their apartment in Melbourne. It will be part of the story (as a flashback - I like that Lauren get herself involved ... and didn't). So, another idea... you decide if it can work. Again, for business, Celeste has to go out for the evening, and, again, gets 'dressed to the nines'. Charlotte watches her put on evening makeup. She knows Celeste always wears a little for work or being in society, but this is different: it's evening makeup. And it changes her look significantly, makes her look mor powerful, in a way, more composed, put together. Business suit, stockings, heels: the picture of elegance if she were just a picture, but then, Celeste walks, talks... the quiet strength that propels her during the day shows teeth at night. And Charlotte realises that it's not about putting on anything... makeup is to a woman's personality as a condiment is to a dish: if the dish is made of poor ingredients, spices won't save it.

Emily:

This absolutely can work—and it can do something rare: reclaim “dressed up” as competence and intention, not spectacle. The way you’re framing it (makeup as “condiment,” not foundation) is exactly the right philosophical lever. It keeps the scene anchored in Celeste’s power and Charlotte’s learning, not in makeover tropes.

The only craft caution: don’t linger on the mechanics (brushes, lips, lashes) the way TG stories do. Keep it observational and interpretive—Charlotte watching Celeste’s decision-making and the shift in presence, not the cosmetics.

Here’s a full scene draft in that vein.

Scene — “Condiment” (Charlotte POV, past tense)¶

Celeste had started calling them “evening rooms.”

Not the apartment—this apartment was small and clean and theirs in a way the old place had never quite been—but the version of herself she stepped into when the work demanded a certain kind of polish.

Charlotte understood why. The city asked women for a language. Celeste didn’t argue with it. She just spoke it better than most.

That night, the reason was simple: networking. A room full of people who smiled too much and asked questions that weren’t questions. A room where a woman’s competence was assessed through details that pretended to be irrelevant.

Celeste didn’t say that out loud.

She just laid out her things on the bathroom counter with the calm inevitability of someone preparing tools.

Charlotte leaned in the doorway and watched.

Celeste caught her reflection and met Charlotte’s eyes in the mirror.

“You look like you’re about to witness a ritual,” Celeste said.

Charlotte’s mouth tightened slightly, thoughtful. “It… is, isn’t it.”

Celeste’s expression softened for half a second. “It’s procedure.”

She said it the way she said policy. Like a thing you did so the day didn’t do you.

Charlotte watched her pick up the first product and apply it with an economy of motion. No fuss. No lingering. No romance about the act. Celeste didn’t become someone else. She composed herself—like taking scattered papers and aligning them into a neat stack.

The difference was gradual. Almost invisible, until it wasn’t.

Her features sharpened—not harshly, but with intention. She looked less like a student who belonged in lecture halls and more like a woman who would be listened to in a room full of men who’d rather not.

Charlotte felt something inside her shift—admiration, and a small sting of awe.

Celeste looked up again, seeing her.

“This isn’t for me,” Celeste said, as if reading Charlotte’s thoughts. “Not as self-expression.”

Charlotte said quietly, “It looks like you.”

“It is me,” Celeste replied. “Just… emphasised.”

Charlotte stepped closer, drawn in despite herself.

Celeste added a final detail and then leaned back, examining her own face in the mirror with clinical detachment—checking symmetry, not searching for validation.

Then she turned slightly, and Charlotte saw it: the teeth.

Not literal, not aggression. The kind of composure that could bite if it needed to. The quiet strength Celeste carried through daylight—organising, negotiating, holding standards—was still there. But now it was framed. Made legible to people who couldn’t read subtler signals.

Celeste reached for her blazer and slipped it on. The line of it sat perfectly. She adjusted the shoulders once, decisive.

The stockings and heels followed—not sexual, not performative. Structural. A silhouette that meant business.

When she stood, the whole image clicked into place.

If she’d been a photograph, Charlotte thought, she would have looked like elegance.

But it wasn’t the picture that did it.

It was the way Celeste moved.

Even in their small bathroom, she walked as if she belonged in the bigger room she was about to enter. Not because of the clothes. Because she had already decided she belonged there, and the clothes were simply the punctuation.

Charlotte watched her pick up her bag, check her phone, and glance at herself one last time—not for approval, for readiness.

Then Celeste looked at Charlotte and the harder edge softened.

“You’re staring,” Celeste said, mildly amused.

Charlotte didn’t deny it. “You look…”

Celeste waited.

Charlotte searched for the right word and found it unexpectedly.

“More,” Charlotte said.

Celeste’s mouth curved. “More what.”

Charlotte swallowed. “More composed. More… dangerous.”

Celeste’s eyebrows lifted, a flicker of genuine surprise. “Dangerous.”

Charlotte nodded once, serious. “Like you could walk into a room and make people behave.”

Celeste’s smile sharpened. “I can.”

Charlotte felt her chest tighten—love and awe braided together, and something else too: understanding.

She’d been thinking about femininity as an inventory. Things you put on. Things you learned. Things you acquired. The store. The perfume floor. The fear of performance.

But this—Celeste dressed to the nines in their bathroom, no audience, no applause—was different.

The outfit didn’t make Celeste powerful.

It only made her power easier for other people to perceive.

Charlotte heard herself speak, almost as if she were saying it for her own benefit.

“It’s like…” She paused, then shook her head at herself. “I’m going to say something stupid.”

Celeste’s eyes stayed on her. “Say it.”

Charlotte exhaled once. “Makeup is… a condiment.”

Celeste’s eyebrows lifted again. “A condiment.”

Charlotte nodded, warming to the idea as it formed. “If the dish is made of poor ingredients, spices won’t save it. But if the dish is good…” She looked at Celeste, meaning it plainly. “Then the seasoning makes the quality obvious.”

Celeste went still for a beat, as if something tender had landed in her chest unexpectedly.

Then she smiled—small, private, pleased.

“That,” Celeste said, “is the most Charlotte thing you’ve ever said.”

Charlotte’s mouth tightened. “Is it wrong.”

Celeste shook her head. “No.”

She stepped closer and, without turning it into theatre, adjusted something small at Charlotte’s collar—an affectionate habit she’d developed, a quiet way of touching that didn’t ask permission because it already had it.

“It’s also why you don’t need to fear this,” Celeste added. “You’re not trying to manufacture yourself.”

Charlotte’s eyes held hers. “But I want… that presence.”

Celeste’s gaze sharpened—gentle, but firm. “Then build it from the inside. The outside comes last.”

Charlotte nodded slowly. “How.”

Celeste didn’t give her a tutorial.

She gave her a standard.

“You practise self-contact,” Celeste said. “Mirror. Voice. Posture. Not for other people. For you. And you pick one tool—one—at a time. Scent. A lipstick you wear at home first. Not to perform. To learn what feels like you.”

Charlotte swallowed. “Okay.”

Celeste’s mouth curved. “Good.”

She leaned in and kissed Charlotte’s cheek—chaste, deliberate, affectionate—then murmured, as if sealing a pact:

“You don’t need to become anyone else,” Celeste said. “You just need to stop hiding from yourself.”

Charlotte closed her eyes for a brief second and let that warmth go somewhere deep.

When Celeste stepped back, the “evening room” returned—the composed, sharpened woman ready to enter the city’s theatre without being consumed by it.

Celeste picked up her keys.

“I’ll be back around ten,” she said.

Charlotte nodded. “Text me when you leave.”

Celeste’s eyes warmed. “Always.”

Then she was gone, the door clicking softly behind her.

Charlotte stood in the bathroom doorway for a moment longer, looking at the counter where the tools had been.

Not longing for them.

Understanding them.

And in the mirror—just for a second—she met her own eyes.

Not long.

But long enough to feel the beginning of something steady: the knowledge that presence wasn’t painted on.

It was built.

If you want to keep this firmly away from trope territory, a nice follow-up beat is: Charlotte doesn’t immediately try makeup. She tries posture and eye contact first—the “ingredients.” Then, weeks later, scent becomes the first “condiment,” chosen at home, not purchased in a flooded department store.

Robyn:

ExACTly! Oh, yes please, Emily. Same terrain, different vehicle. We are covering ground all the tropes do, and obliterating them: making the ghost-of-character in other stories - a person with no backstory, a person no one would ever care about (TS Elliot's Hollow Men?) - into the person you would recognise in a coffee shop. A soul. Charlotte is going to get to know herself... that little girl she never met. Not infantilism: self-recognition.

Emily:

Scene — “Ingredients” (Celeste POV, past tense)¶

Charlotte didn’t go looking for lipstick.

That was what Celeste liked most about the way she changed now: she didn’t grab at symbols. She built foundations.

It started the morning after the “condiment” conversation, when Celeste came out of the bathroom and found Charlotte standing in the hallway—two steps back from the mirror—hands at her sides, spine straight, face composed with that strange seriousness she got when she was trying to do something brave without advertising it.

Celeste stopped.

Charlotte didn’t look away.

That alone was progress.

“I’m doing it,” Charlotte said quietly.

Celeste leaned her shoulder against the wall, careful to keep her voice neutral. No praise that would turn it into performance.

“How long,” Celeste asked.

“Two minutes,” Charlotte replied.

Celeste nodded once. “Good.”

Charlotte’s eyes stayed on her own reflection, but she wasn’t looking at her hair or her mouth or the geometry of her face. She was doing the harder thing: looking into her eyes as if she were trying to meet someone new and had been taught her whole life to avoid the introduction.

Celeste watched, and something tightened in her chest.

She had to resist the urge to step in and mother the moment. To say you’re beautiful or you’re safe—soft lies that would turn this into reassurance-seeking.

Instead she stayed still and let Charlotte have it.

When the timer on Charlotte’s phone chimed softly, Charlotte exhaled as if she’d been holding her breath.

She didn’t slump. She didn’t collapse into relief.

She simply… stepped away.

Celeste lifted her eyebrows slightly. “How did it feel.”

Charlotte’s mouth tightened, thoughtful. “Like making eye contact with a stranger who knows too much about me.”

Celeste’s lips curved. “Accurate.”

Charlotte’s eyes flicked to her, then back to the mirror, and Celeste saw the flicker of something new there—faint, unsure, but undeniably present.

Curiosity.

Charlotte cleared her throat. “Can I ask you something.”

Celeste kept her tone even. “Yes.”

Charlotte’s gaze stayed on the mirror, as if she needed the reflection as a steadying point.

“When girls grow up,” Charlotte said, carefully, “do they… practise this? Or do they just… get it.”

Celeste didn’t answer quickly.

Because she could give the easy answer—some do, some don’t—but Charlotte wasn’t asking about sociology. She was asking about loss.

She was asking what she’d missed.

“It’s both,” Celeste said finally. “They get mirrored. Constantly. They get told who they are in a thousand tiny ways. And then—if they’re lucky—they eventually take that mirror back and decide for themselves.”

Charlotte swallowed. “I didn’t get… the first part.”

Celeste shook her head, slow. “Not in that language.”

Charlotte’s voice was quiet. “So this is me doing it… late.”

Celeste’s expression sharpened, not unkind. “This is you doing it now.”

Charlotte’s mouth twitched. “That’s the nicer way to say it.”

“It’s the accurate way,” Celeste said.

Charlotte nodded once. Then, as if moving from the mirror to a new layer of difficulty, she said, “I want to practise… presence.”

Celeste’s eyes narrowed slightly. “Define it.”

Charlotte glanced at Celeste, then away again, embarrassed by her own earnestness.

“The way you stand,” Charlotte said. “The way you look at people. The way you don’t apologise for existing.”

Celeste’s mouth curved. “Ah.”

Charlotte’s tone tightened, almost defensive. “Not as a performance. As… ingredients.”

Celeste felt warmth bloom in her chest.

“Good,” Celeste said. “That’s exactly it.”

She pushed off the wall and stepped closer—not to take over, but to calibrate.

“Okay,” Celeste said. “We practise three things. None of them are makeup.”

Charlotte nodded, serious.

“First,” Celeste said, “posture. Not ‘feminine.’ Upright. Grounded. Your body taking the space it needs, no more, no less.”

Charlotte adjusted automatically—shoulders back, chin level.

Celeste shook her head once. “Not stiff. You’re not a soldier. Soften your knees.”

Charlotte blinked, then did it. Her whole stance changed, subtly. Less braced. More alive.

Celeste nodded. “Better.”

“Second,” Celeste continued, “voice. You don’t have to be soft. You have to be clear.”

Charlotte’s mouth tightened. “I am clear.”

Celeste’s eyes held hers. “You are when you’re comfortable. When you’re uncertain, you disappear.”

Charlotte went still.

Celeste didn’t soften it. “So practise speaking one sentence without shrinking at the end.”

Charlotte’s eyebrows lifted. “What sentence.”

Celeste didn’t give her something cute.

She gave her something true.

“Say,” Celeste said, “I don’t want that.”

Charlotte looked like she hated it.

Then she said, evenly, “I don’t want that.”

Celeste shook her head. “Again. Without apology.”

Charlotte’s jaw tightened. “I don’t want that.”

Celeste nodded. “Good.”

Charlotte’s eyes flashed, offended. “That’s… harsh.”

Celeste’s voice stayed calm. “It’s clean.”

Charlotte stared at her, then nodded slowly as if she understood the difference.

“And third,” Celeste said, “eye contact. Not staring. Not submission. Just… holding your presence in your face.”

Charlotte looked back toward the mirror, then at Celeste. She held Celeste’s gaze for a beat longer than usual.

Celeste felt it—Charlotte not just looking, but arriving.

It was quiet.

It was enormous.

Charlotte exhaled. “This feels like learning how to be a person.”

Celeste’s voice softened slightly. “It is.”

Charlotte swallowed, then said the thing that made Celeste’s chest tighten again.

“I keep thinking,” Charlotte murmured, “about a little girl I never met.”

Celeste didn’t respond with pity. She didn’t make it tragic. She simply let the truth exist.

Charlotte continued, eyes steady now, not lost.

“Not… dress-ups,” Charlotte said quickly, as if guarding against cliché. “Not childish things. Just… the part of me that would have learned to exist without bracing.”

Celeste nodded once, very slowly. “Yes.”

Charlotte’s mouth trembled slightly, then steadied.

“I want to meet her,” Charlotte said. “Not to become her. To recognise her.”

Celeste felt her throat tighten. She kept her tone firm.

“Then you will,” Celeste said. “One boring repetition at a time.”

Charlotte’s lips twitched. “Boring.”

Celeste’s mouth curved. “Boring is reliable. Reliable is freedom.”

Charlotte nodded.

Celeste stepped closer and, very gently, adjusted Charlotte’s shoulders—just a fraction—like she would adjust a garment on a form. Not to control. To show what “correct” felt like.

“Okay,” Celeste said. “Now you go make tea. And you do it like you belong in your own kitchen.”

Charlotte blinked at her.

Celeste held her gaze. “Go on.”

Charlotte turned, and Celeste watched her walk away.

Not floaty. Not demure.

Grounded. Present.

A woman learning, not how to look like herself—

but how to be seen by herself without flinching.

Celeste stayed by the mirror for a moment after Charlotte left.

In the glass, she saw her own face—tired, determined, quietly proud.

And she realised something that surprised her:

This wasn’t Charlotte learning femininity.

This was Charlotte learning selfhood.

And Celeste—who had spent most of her life collecting power like armour—found herself learning something too:

That there was a kind of strength that didn’t come from command.

It came from witnessing.

From holding a standard.

From staying.

If you want the next beat to stay in the same “ingredients” lane, we can do a small public test: a café interaction where Charlotte practices voice and eye contact with a barista or a server—nothing dramatic, just a clean moment where she doesn’t shrink, and Celeste notices without praising like a trainer.

Yes please.

Emily:

Scene — “Order” (Celeste POV, past tense)¶

The café they chose was loud enough that nobody listened, and bright enough that Charlotte couldn’t hide.

Which was exactly why Celeste picked it.

Not as a test. Celeste didn’t do tests anymore. But as a place where practice could happen in the wild—ordinary stakes, ordinary social friction, the kind of environment where old habits surfaced without warning.

They sat near the window. Melbourne moved past in layers: tram bells, office shoes, bags swinging, sunlight on glass.

Charlotte had her hands around her mug, shoulders relaxed the way Celeste had been teaching her—no bracing, no shrinking. She looked… present. Not perfect. Present.

Celeste watched her and didn’t say anything. Praise turned practice into performance.

After a while, Charlotte glanced toward the counter.

“I’ll order,” Charlotte said.

Celeste’s eyes lifted from her phone. “Okay.”

Charlotte stood.

Not quickly. Not apologetically. She stood the way she’d been learning: knees soft, spine upright, body taking the space it needed without asking permission.

Celeste sat back and let her go.

At the counter, a barista in a black apron looked up with the brisk neutrality of someone managing a line.

“Hi,” he said. “What can I get you?”

Charlotte opened her mouth—then paused.

Celeste felt it from across the room: the old reflex, the tiny retreat, the instinct to become smaller so nobody would be annoyed by her existence.

Charlotte’s fingers tightened around the edge of the counter.

Then she inhaled once, slow and controlled.

She met the barista’s eyes.

And she spoke.

“Two flat whites,” Charlotte said, evenly. “One regular. One decaf.”

The barista blinked, not because it was unusual, but because Charlotte’s tone was… clean. Unfussy. Adult.

“Sure,” he said. “Any milk preference?”

Charlotte didn’t look down. She didn’t laugh nervously. She didn’t over-explain.

“Oat for the decaf,” she said. “Regular milk for the other.”

The barista nodded, fingers moving quickly over the register. “That’s thirteen fifty.”

Charlotte reached into her bag, pulled out her wallet, and handed over her card without fumbling. Her hand was steady.

The barista tapped the terminal toward her.

Charlotte paid. Simple. Calm.

Then, as the barista turned away, Charlotte said one more sentence—quiet, polite, and crucially, not submissive.

“Thank you.”

Not gushing. Not performative. Not a plea.

A fact.

The barista looked back, and for the first time his expression warmed, just slightly, as if he’d noticed something he couldn’t name.

“No worries,” he said, and slid her the receipt.

Charlotte took it and turned.

As she walked back to the table, Celeste watched the way her face held itself—no rushed smile, no checking for approval, no nervous scanning for judgement.

When Charlotte sat down, she placed the receipt on the table and exhaled once.

Celeste kept her voice neutral. “How did it feel.”

Charlotte’s mouth tightened, considering. “Like… speaking from my whole body.”

Celeste’s eyebrows lifted slightly. “Good.”

Charlotte’s gaze flicked to Celeste, and something like relief softened the edge of her eyes.

“I didn’t disappear,” Charlotte said.

Celeste nodded once. “No.”

Charlotte looked out the window, then back, thoughtful. “It was such a small thing.”

Celeste’s voice stayed calm. “Small things are how people become real.”

Charlotte’s lips twitched. “You make it sound like an engineering problem.”

Celeste’s mouth curved. “It is.”

Charlotte stared at her for a beat, then shook her head slightly as if amused by herself.

“I used to think,” Charlotte said quietly, “that being… ‘good’ meant being easy. Not taking up space. Not being a bother.”

Celeste’s eyes sharpened. “That’s not goodness. That’s training.”

Charlotte nodded, slow. “I know.”

A pause.

Then Charlotte added, almost reluctantly, “But it still lives in me.”

Celeste reached across the table and rested two fingers lightly on Charlotte’s wrist—not possessive, just grounding.

“It will,” Celeste said. “For a while. And then one day you’ll notice it hasn’t spoken in weeks.”

Charlotte’s throat worked. “How do you know.”

Celeste didn’t answer with theory. She answered with truth.

“Because I can see you practising,” Celeste said. “And practice changes the nervous system. That’s what it’s for.”

Charlotte’s mouth tightened, holding emotion down the way she always did when she didn’t want to make it messy.

Celeste didn’t push.

The coffees arrived—one regular, one decaf with oat—set down with a quick “here you go.”

Charlotte looked up, met the barista’s eyes again, and said, evenly, “Thank you.”

Again: clean. Adult. Real.

Celeste lifted her mug and watched Charlotte wrap her hands around hers.

No blush. No apology.

Just a woman sitting in a café, ordering coffee, existing.

The city didn’t pause for her.

Which was perfect.

Because Charlotte didn’t need the world to pause.

She only needed to stop shrinking for it.

Scene — “All Day” (Celeste POV, past tense)¶

Charlotte didn’t announce it.

That was the first sign it was real.

Celeste came out of the bedroom to the small, bright kitchen of their Melbourne apartment and stopped without meaning to. Not because Charlotte was done up—she wasn’t. Not because she looked like a different person.

Because she looked like the same person, finally willing to be seen.

Charlotte stood by the counter with her tote open, checking she had her keys, her phone, the little notebook she used for Wardrobe notes. She was dressed in jeans—skinny, yes, but not theatrical. Just a clean line that admitted her legs existed. A simple top with the front tucked in, making her waist legible without trying to turn it into an argument. Her hair was pinned into a low updo—practical, neat, quietly intentional.

And there was the faintest trace of lipstick. Not a “look.” More like a decision.

Celeste’s eyes flicked to Charlotte’s mouth, then to her eyes.

Charlotte caught her looking and held it. No shrinking. No reflexive apology.

Celeste felt something warm and sharp move through her chest.

“You—” Celeste began.

Charlotte lifted her chin a fraction. “Don’t.”

Celeste’s mouth curved. “Don’t what.”

“Don’t make it a big thing,” Charlotte said, very calmly.

Celeste blinked.

Then she laughed once, soft. “Mara’s been leaking into you.”

Charlotte’s lips twitched. “Probably.”

Celeste stepped closer anyway, because she wasn’t going to pretend she hadn’t noticed.

“What’s the scent,” Celeste asked, voice low, curious.

Charlotte hesitated for half a second—then lifted her wrist.

Celeste leaned in, inhaled.

It was clean. Warm. Slightly woody. Nothing loud. Nothing sweet. Something that sat close to the skin and felt… adult.

“Chemist’s Warehouse?” Celeste guessed.

Charlotte’s mouth tightened, pleased and stubborn. “Safe.”

Celeste nodded. “Good choice.”

Charlotte’s gaze sharpened. “It’s not… fancy.”

Celeste held her eyes. “It’s yours.”

That did it. Charlotte’s shoulders dropped by a fraction, relief sliding in.

Celeste let her gaze take Charlotte in again—the tucked shirt, the line of the jeans, the neat hair that would hold up through a workday, the tiny hint of colour that didn’t ask anyone’s permission.

“You look…” Celeste paused, choosing carefully.

Charlotte’s eyes stayed on her. “Say it properly.”

Celeste’s mouth curved. “You look like you’re not negotiating.”

Charlotte exhaled once, a small sound of agreement. “That’s the point.”

Celeste picked up her own mug and leaned against the counter, watching Charlotte pack her tote with that methodical competence that Wardrobe seemed to bring out in her like a frequency.

Charlotte paused, then looked up.

“I want to do it,” she said.

Celeste’s eyebrows lifted. “Do what.”

“What I did at the café,” Charlotte said, as if it were a technical skill. “But all day.”

Celeste let the silence sit for a beat.

“Visible,” Charlotte clarified, and the word came out with a hint of fear she didn’t bother dressing up. “Not… loud. Not attention. Just… not hiding.”

Celeste nodded once, slow. “Okay.”

Charlotte blinked. “That’s it.”

Celeste’s eyes sharpened. “You want permission?”

Charlotte’s jaw tightened. “No.”

Celeste’s mouth curved. “Good. Then don’t ask for it.”

Charlotte stared at her, then nodded—one of those adult nods, the kind that meant she’d heard the standard and intended to meet it.

Celeste stepped closer and adjusted one small thing at Charlotte’s collar—habit now, affectionate and practical all at once.

Charlotte’s eyes softened.

Celeste’s voice dropped. “What’s the risk.”

Charlotte didn’t pretend she didn’t know.

“That I’ll… disappear again,” Charlotte said quietly. “If someone looks too long. Or if someone says something stupid. Or if I catch myself thinking I’m not allowed.”

Celeste nodded. “And what’s the plan.”

Charlotte’s gaze steadied, and Celeste felt the deep satisfaction of seeing practice turn into structure.

“I don’t apologise,” Charlotte said. “I don’t over-explain. I don’t make myself small.”

Celeste’s mouth curved. “Good.”

Charlotte added, more softly, “And if I feel myself fading, I step into the back room and breathe. I don’t push through it until I break.”

Celeste’s eyes warmed. “Very Wardrobe.”

Charlotte’s lips twitched. “Yes.”

Celeste reached out and took Charlotte’s hand briefly—just long enough to anchor, not long enough to make it sentimental.

“And one more thing,” Celeste said.

Charlotte looked at her.

Celeste’s voice was quiet, exact. “If anyone at Wardrobe reacts—positively or negatively—you do not manage their feelings.”

Charlotte’s eyes sharpened. “I won’t.”

Celeste nodded. “Good.”

Charlotte zipped her tote, slung it over her shoulder, and stood at the door.

For a moment she hesitated—not with fear, with the gravity of decision.

Then she looked at Celeste, eyes steady, presence held in her face the way they’d been practising.

“I’m going to work,” Charlotte said.

Celeste’s mouth curved. “Yes you are.”

Charlotte’s hand touched the doorknob.

Celeste watched her, and in her mind she saw all the old versions—the boy who never had fantasies of heroism because realism was his defence, the person who could endure anything as long as it didn’t ask him to look at himself too closely.

And now this: a woman who had decided to be visible without performing.

Charlotte opened the door.

Before she stepped out, she turned back once and met Celeste’s eyes.

Not to seek reassurance.

To share something.

“Celeste,” Charlotte said quietly.

“Yes,” Celeste replied.

Charlotte’s mouth tightened, emotion contained but undeniable. “Thank you for not letting me outsource my selfhood to your eyes.”

Celeste felt her throat tighten.

She didn’t answer with softness. She answered with truth.

“You did the work,” Celeste said. “I just held the standard.”

Charlotte nodded once—accepting it, owning it.

Then she stepped into the corridor and walked toward the lift like she belonged there.

Because she did.

And Celeste stood in the doorway for a moment longer, breathing in the faint trace of Charlotte’s new scent—quiet, close to the skin—and felt something settle inside her that had nothing to do with pride and everything to do with love:

Charlotte was no longer trying to be permitted.

She was beginning to be.

Simple request: Charlotte, as seen by Sarah (POV). They have a chat.

Emily:

Scene — “Seen” (Sarah POV, past tense)¶

Charlotte walked into Wardrobe like she’d finally stopped asking the room whether she was allowed.

Sarah noticed immediately.

Sarah noticed everything, but she noticed this the way you noticed weather: not as detail, as atmosphere.

Jeans—skinny, yes, but not silly. A top tucked in at the front like she’d decided her waist existed and didn’t need to be defended. Hair pinned low and neat, practical but intentional. And there was a faint hint of lipstick—so subtle Sarah might have missed it on anyone else.

On Charlotte it read like a sentence.

I’m here.

Not a performance. Not a plea.

Sarah looked up from the front desk and, for once, didn’t make a joke. She let Charlotte get past the threshold without turning it into a moment that would make her self-conscious.

Charlotte moved toward the back with her tote on her shoulder, nodding at Lauren, nodding at Mara, doing the small social acknowledgements that made a person legible without making them loud.

Then Charlotte paused near the ledger table, as if checking the room the way you checked wind before you stepped outside.

Sarah waited until Charlotte glanced her way.

Then Sarah lifted her chin slightly and said, “Tea?”

Charlotte’s eyes flicked to her—grateful, cautious, and steady all at once. “Yes.”

Sarah didn’t say good girl or anything cute that would land wrong. She just jerked her head toward the kitchenette like it was a normal workday, which was the whole point.

They made tea in silence at first, the kettle clicking like punctuation. Sarah kept her body loose, not looming, not interrogating. She’d learned that the way you approached a skittish thing wasn’t to corner it with enthusiasm.

Charlotte held the mug with both hands, breathing in the steam as if scent had become part of her calibration now.

Sarah leaned her hip against the counter and said, lightly, “Right.”

Charlotte blinked. “Right.”

Sarah’s mouth twitched. “You’re different.”

Charlotte’s shoulders tensed for half a beat—then, impressively, didn’t collapse into apology. “Different how.”

Sarah nodded once, approving. “There it is. That.”

Charlotte’s brow furrowed, but her gaze didn’t drop. “Say it.”

Sarah studied her for a moment, then decided to be kind in the only way Sarah knew how: by being exact.

“You’ve stopped trying to be invisible,” Sarah said. “And you’ve stopped trying to make it everyone else’s job to cope with you existing.”

Charlotte went still.

Sarah held her gaze, not unkind. “That sounds harsher than I mean it.”

Charlotte’s mouth tightened. “No. It’s accurate.”

Sarah’s eyebrows lifted. “Well, look at you. Adult.”

Charlotte let out a small breath—almost a laugh. “I’m trying.”

Sarah’s eyes flicked to Charlotte’s mouth, then back up. “Also… lip.”

Charlotte’s hand went reflexively toward her face, then stopped halfway, as if she caught herself performing embarrassment. “Yes.”

Sarah nodded once. “It’s barely there.”

“That was deliberate,” Charlotte said.

Sarah’s mouth curved. “Good. Because if you’d come in here looking like a department store counter attacked you, I’d have had to intervene.”

Charlotte’s eyes warmed slightly. “I didn’t want… theatre.”

Sarah lifted her mug. “Correct answer.”

They sipped. The studio’s familiar sounds pressed in around them: the soft rasp of fabric, the snip of shears, the scratch of Mara’s pencil on paper. Ordinary. Safe.

Sarah watched Charlotte for a beat longer. “What’s the scent.”

Charlotte hesitated. Then she lifted her wrist.

Sarah leaned in—only as far as was respectful—and inhaled.

“Ooh,” Sarah said, genuinely pleased. “Clean. Grown.”

“Chemist’s Warehouse,” Charlotte said, a little defensively.

Sarah snorted. “Excellent. Nothing more dangerous than a man thinking your perfume cost money.”

Charlotte blinked, then smiled—small, real.

Sarah watched that smile like it was evidence.

“You did something,” Sarah said, quieter now. “Didn’t you. You decided something.”

Charlotte’s gaze steadied. “I decided I’m not hiding anymore.”

Sarah nodded slowly. “All day.”

Charlotte’s mouth tightened. “All day.”

Sarah’s eyes stayed on hers. “You’re going to get looks.”

Charlotte didn’t flinch. “I know.”

Sarah’s eyebrows lifted. “And.”

Charlotte’s voice was even. “And I won’t manage other people’s feelings about it.”

Sarah’s mouth curved, sharp with approval. “Celeste’s been talking to you.”

Charlotte’s lips twitched. “She’s been… holding standards.”

Sarah nodded. “She does that.”

A pause.

Then Sarah said, carefully, “How does it feel. Being… this.”

Charlotte looked down at her mug, not in shame—more in thought. “Like I’m wearing my own name properly.”

Sarah’s throat tightened. She kept her face steady.

“That’s… good,” Sarah said, and the softness in her voice surprised even her.

Charlotte looked up. “It’s scary.”

Sarah’s smile sharpened. “Yes.”

Charlotte blinked.

Sarah took a sip and said, matter-of-fact, “Anything worth having is scary. Otherwise it’s just… a hobby.”

Charlotte’s mouth tightened in that way she had when something landed.

Sarah continued, quieter, “And for what it’s worth—”

Charlotte watched her.

Sarah shrugged, as if it mattered less than it did. “You look like one of us today. Not because of the jeans. Because of the… stance.”

Charlotte’s eyes flickered—emotion threatening.

Sarah lifted a hand immediately. “Don’t make me regret saying something nice.”

Charlotte’s mouth twitched. “Thank you.”

Sarah rolled her eyes. “Fine.”

They stood in silence for a moment, two women with mugs, the day building around them.

Then Charlotte said, softly, “If someone says something… stupid.”

Sarah’s smile returned, bright and dangerous. “Then you come get me.”

Charlotte’s eyes warmed. “You’d do that.”

Sarah snorted. “Please. I live for it.”

Charlotte’s smile widened, just a fraction.

Sarah watched her over the rim of her mug and thought—not for the first time—that people underestimated what it took to become yourself when you didn’t get the easy, boring reinforcement of girlhood.

Charlotte had missed years.

But she wasn’t trying to cosplay them now.

She was building something better: self-recognition, late and clean and real.

Sarah set her mug down.

“Right,” she said briskly, as if to save them both from feeling too much. “Back to work. We’ve got standards to uphold.”

Charlotte nodded, shoulders settling.

“Yes,” she said. “We do.”

And as they walked back into the studio, Charlotte didn’t shrink when she passed the hallway mirror.

She didn’t preen either.

She simply existed in full view.

Sarah, behind her, felt something like pride—and kept it to herself, where it belonged.

Scene — “The Text” (Sarah POV, past tense)¶

Sarah hadn’t been over in a week.

Which was ridiculous, really. She’d been over often enough that “a week” shouldn’t have felt like exile. But Sarah had a particular, stupid flaw: she could be brave at a door with a strange man and timid at the idea of being unwanted.

She’d told herself she was giving Lauren space.

She’d told herself she was being sensible.

What she’d really been doing was avoiding the possibility that Lauren had been kind out of loneliness, and that kindness had now run out.

At work, Lauren’s careful stance didn’t help. No affection. No lingering touches. No private looks. If you didn’t know better you’d think Lauren barely tolerated her.

Sarah did know better… and still, the old doubt chewed at the edges.

The day wound down. Dispatch done. Floor swept. Mara grudgingly obeying the Load Rule like a woman who hated every minute of it. Celeste packing her laptop. Charlotte gathering her tote, hair still neat, mouth still faintly coloured, presence held like a skill.

Charlotte moved toward the back to say something to Celeste. Sarah stayed at the front, doing her end-of-day ritual: phone, keys, tote, check the lock.

She reached under the counter for her phone.

It buzzed in her hand before she’d even lifted it fully.

A message.

From Lauren.

Sarah’s heart did a stupid little hop and then immediately punished her for the hop by tightening with fear.

She glanced at the preview.

please come tonight. i need to talk to you…

Lowercase. No punctuation. That alone was unsettling. Lauren did not type like that unless something had slipped through her fingers.

Sarah stared at the screen as if it might change its mind.

Behind her, Charlotte stepped into the front space, tote on her shoulder, ready to head for the train. She paused when she saw Sarah frozen.

“You alright?” Charlotte asked quietly.

Sarah heard herself answer too fast. “Yes.”

Charlotte’s eyes narrowed slightly—competence recognising a lie.

Sarah, because she couldn’t stand silence when her chest was doing that tight thing, said the worst possible thing.

Out loud.

“Lauren just texted me,” Sarah said, and before she could stop herself—before she could keep it private like a sensible adult—she read it.

“Please come tonight. I need to talk to you…”

The words hung in the air between the front desk and the door like a dropped plate.

Sarah felt heat flood her face.

She wished she hadn’t. Instantly. Vividly.

Charlotte went still. Not nosy. Not amused. Just… attentive in that new, grounded way she’d been practising.

Sarah swallowed.

“That’s—” Sarah began, then stopped, because she didn’t know what it was.

A request. A warning. An ending. An invitation. A crisis.

Charlotte’s gaze stayed on her, steady. “You look like you’re about to run.”

Sarah let out a breath that sounded too much like a laugh and too much like panic. “I’m not.”

Charlotte lifted her eyebrows slightly. The expression said: I’ve watched you remove grown men from the threshold. Don’t pretend you’re not scared of this.

Sarah looked back down at the screen. The message didn’t change. Still there. Still small. Still enormous.

“I haven’t been over,” Sarah said, voice tight. “Because I thought I was—”

She cut herself off, because saying wearing out my welcome out loud felt humiliating.

Charlotte waited. Didn’t rescue her. Didn’t fill the silence.

Sarah hated her for that, briefly.

Then she respected her for it.

“I thought I was intruding,” Sarah finished, quieter.

Charlotte nodded once. “And Lauren seemed distant at work.”

Sarah blinked. “Yes.”

Charlotte’s voice stayed calm. “That distance is policy.”

Sarah’s mouth tightened. “I know that.”

Charlotte’s eyes sharpened, kind without softness. “Knowing isn’t the same as believing. Not when you’re attached.”

Sarah’s throat tightened. “Don’t psychoanalyse me, Charlotte.”

Charlotte’s lips twitched. “You asked.”

“I didn’t,” Sarah muttered.

Charlotte glanced at the phone again. “She asked you to come.”

Sarah stared at the text as if it might contain hidden traps.

“What if it’s bad,” Sarah said, and the fear in her voice made her angry with herself. “What if she’s—”

Charlotte didn’t let her spiral. She didn’t do platitudes. She did structure.

“Read it again,” Charlotte said.

Sarah’s eyebrows lifted. “Why.”

“Because the content matters more than your story about it,” Charlotte replied.

Sarah ground her teeth, then did it—silently this time.

please come tonight. i need to talk to you…

Charlotte watched her face. “It doesn’t say ‘we need to talk.’ It says ‘I need to talk to you.’”

Sarah blinked, thrown.

Charlotte continued, steady. “That’s not a breakup line. That’s a reach.”

Sarah’s mouth tightened. “You don’t know that.”

Charlotte’s gaze didn’t waver. “Do you want it to be a reach.”

Sarah’s throat worked. “Yes.”

“Then go,” Charlotte said simply.

Sarah stared at her.

Charlotte added, “Don’t punish her for being careful at work.”

Sarah’s face hardened. “I didn’t punish her.”

Charlotte’s eyes sharpened. “You withdrew.”

Sarah flinched because it was true.

Charlotte’s voice softened by a fraction—not comforting, just human. “You’re allowed to want to be wanted.”

Sarah’s eyes stung, annoyingly.

She looked away, furious with herself, and wiped her thumb across her phone screen like she could erase the feeling.

“I hate this,” Sarah muttered.

Charlotte nodded once. “Yes.”

Sarah huffed a laugh. “That’s not helpful.”

Charlotte’s lips twitched. “It’s accurate.”

Sarah looked at her again—at Charlotte’s neat hair, her subtle lipstick, her steady gaze—and realised with a small, strange jolt that Charlotte was getting better at this.

At being present. At holding a moment without turning it into noise.

Sarah, who had always used humour like a shield, felt suddenly exposed.

“What if she wants… rules,” Sarah said quietly. “More rules.”

Charlotte’s mouth tightened. “Rules are not rejection.”

Sarah swallowed. “Sometimes they feel like it.”

Charlotte didn’t argue. She just said, calmly, “Go and find out. Don’t imagine yourself into exile.”

Sarah stared down at the text again.

Then she did something that surprised her: she didn’t reply immediately. She didn’t throw a joke at it. She didn’t type lol are you okay to soften it.

She simply wrote:

On my way.

Two words. No apology. No hedging.

She hit send.

Her heart beat hard in her chest, but the message was gone now, sent, real, chosen.

Charlotte watched her and nodded once, as if she’d just witnessed a competent act.

Sarah shoved the phone into her tote like it was a live thing.

Then, because she couldn’t help herself, she groaned, “Oh God. I read it out loud.”

Charlotte’s lips twitched. “Yes.”

Sarah glared at her. “Don’t.”

Charlotte’s eyes warmed slightly. “It’s fine.”

Sarah exhaled, half-laughing, half-miserable. “It’s not.”

Charlotte shrugged, calm. “Lauren doesn’t work off second-hand shame. She works off outcomes.”

That was such a Lauren sentence that Sarah felt her chest pinch again.

Charlotte shifted her tote strap. “Train?”

Sarah nodded, still tight. “Train.”

They stepped toward the door together.

Sarah locked up, hands steady because she was good at doors. Good at thresholds. Good at holding a line.

But as they walked out into the evening light, Sarah realised there was one door she still hadn’t learned to stand in front of without flinching:

The one that opened into being wanted.

And tonight, apparently, Lauren had asked her to come through it.

Emily:

Scene — “Coffee” (Sarah POV, past tense)¶

Sarah had imagined the worst.

She’d imagined reprimand. A rules talk sharpened into rejection. A careful, Lauren-shaped sentence that meant: I’ve reconsidered. This was temporary. You misread me.

She’d sat on the train with her phone in her hand, reading On my way like it might be the last brave thing she ever did.

When she got off at Lauren’s stop, the air had that damp, coastal-cool edge that made streetlights look softer than they were. Sarah walked faster than she needed to, because anxiety always made her legs behave as if movement could solve outcome.

At Lauren’s building she buzzed, waited, buzzed again—

The door clicked almost immediately.

No “who is it.” No measured caution. Just… open.

Sarah stepped inside and took the stairs two at a time.

Lauren’s door was ajar.

That was the first wrong thing.

Lauren did not leave doors ajar.

Sarah pushed it open with her fingertips and called, automatically gentle, “Lauren?”

The apartment smelled faintly sweet and sour at once—yeast and bubbles and cheap fruit. Champagne. Not a lot, but enough.

And underneath it, something else: the sharp tang of tears.

Lauren was on the couch. Shoes kicked off. Hair loosened in that way it only got when she’d stopped holding herself together. Her face was blotched, eyes red-rimmed, a champagne flute on the coffee table with a sad amount left in it.

She looked… young. Not in age, in disarray. Like the version of her that existed before she learned to stand like consequence.

When she saw Sarah, she made a sound that was half relief and half shame.

“Oh thank God,” Lauren whispered, and then the whisper broke. “I thought you wouldn’t come.”

Sarah stopped in the doorway for one beat, heart slamming, because this was not what she’d rehearsed for.

This wasn’t distance.

This was need.

And it came with a pitfall.

Sarah took a breath and did what she always did at Wardrobe when something went sideways: she shifted from feeling into procedure.

She walked in, closed the door behind her, locked it—quietly, without announcing it—and put her tote down.

Then she crossed the room and looked at the bottle on the counter.

Inexpensive champagne. Half gone.

Sarah turned toward Lauren.

Lauren’s face crumpled again. “I’m sorry,” she blurted. “I’m so sorry. I shouldn’t have— I just—”

“Stop,” Sarah said, not loud.

Lauren froze.

Sarah walked into the kitchen, picked up the bottle, and held it up where Lauren could see it.

“This,” Sarah said, calm as a guillotine, “is not helping you.”

Lauren’s mouth trembled. “It was supposed to— I thought—”

“I know what you thought,” Sarah cut in, still calm. “I’m not interested in what you thought. I’m interested in what it’s doing.”

Lauren stared at her, breathing shallow.

Sarah set the bottle on the counter and reached for a mug like she’d done it a thousand times. Kettle. Water. Switch.

The click was loud in the quiet apartment.

Lauren’s voice wobbled, small. “I missed you.”

Sarah didn’t answer that immediately, because answering it the wrong way would make it about reassurance instead of repair.

She turned to face Lauren properly.

“I’m here,” Sarah said.

Lauren’s eyes filled again and she looked away like she hated herself for it.

Sarah’s gaze flicked back to the bottle.

The kettle began to hiss softly.

Sarah made a decision—clean, deliberate.

She picked up the bottle again, walked to the sink, and poured.

The champagne gurgled down the drain, foam catching briefly at the metal before disappearing.

Lauren made a strangled sound.

“Sarah—” she protested weakly, then stopped, because she knew how it looked: arguing for the bottle.

Sarah didn’t look at her while she did it. She just kept pouring until the bottle was empty.

Then she rinsed it, set it upside down in the drainer, and turned to face Lauren with the same expression she used on men at the Wardrobe threshold.

Polite.

Final.

“No more,” Sarah said.

Lauren’s mouth tightened. Her eyes dropped. Her shoulders curled inward in a way Sarah had never seen on her.

“I’ve made a fool of myself,” Lauren said, voice raw.

Sarah crossed the room, sat on the edge of the coffee table—not too close, not looming—and kept her voice steady.

“You’ve made a mistake,” Sarah corrected. “Those aren’t the same.”

Lauren swallowed, shame thick in her throat. “I texted you like a teenager.”

Sarah’s mouth twitched, but she didn’t let it turn into humour. “You texted me like someone who needed me.”

Lauren’s eyes lifted. “I did.”

Sarah nodded once. “Okay.”

Lauren’s lower lip trembled. “I hate this.”

Sarah’s tone stayed even. “Yes. Because you’re used to being the adult.”

Lauren flinched, because that landed exactly.

The kettle clicked off.

Sarah stood, went back to the kitchen, and made coffee the way she always did when someone’s nervous system needed a rail to hold onto: strong, hot, practical. No ceremony.

She carried one mug to Lauren and held it out.

Lauren looked at it as if it were an accusation.

“Drink,” Sarah said gently, firm.

Lauren took it with both hands, shoulders hunched, and sipped.

The heat seemed to bring her back into her body.

For a few minutes they sat in silence, Lauren breathing, Sarah watching the tremor in her hands gradually settle.

Then Lauren said, very quietly, “I thought… if I didn’t keep it at work, you’d think I was unprofessional. Or weak.”

Sarah’s eyes sharpened. “And what did you think I’d do.”

Lauren’s mouth opened, then closed. She looked away.

Sarah waited.

Lauren’s voice broke. “Leave.”

Sarah felt something hard and tender twist in her chest.

She kept her voice calm anyway.

“I pulled back,” Sarah admitted. “Because I thought I was wearing out my welcome.”

Lauren snapped her eyes up. “No.”

Sarah held her gaze. “You made it easy to believe.”

Lauren’s face crumpled again, but this time she didn’t dissolve into self-pity. She looked like a woman confronting her own poor judgement.

“I know,” Lauren whispered. “I thought I was… doing it right.”

Sarah nodded. “At work you were.”

Lauren blinked. “Then why—”

“Because you didn’t give yourself anywhere else to be soft,” Sarah said, and the bluntness of it surprised even her. “You turned intimacy into something that only existed offstage. And then when you missed me, you had no language for it except panic.”

Lauren stared at her, stunned.

Then she looked down into the mug, shame and relief mixing into something that finally resembled clarity.

“I drank,” Lauren said, flat and miserable. “Because it was easier than… asking.”

Sarah’s voice stayed gentle. “And it made everything worse.”

Lauren nodded once. “Yes.”

Sarah leaned forward slightly. “Lauren.”

Lauren looked up.

Sarah’s tone hardened into policy—not cruel, protective.

“We do not do alcohol-as-courage,” Sarah said. “Not for sex, not for feelings, not for ‘talks.’ Ever.”

Lauren’s eyes glistened. “I know.”

“Say it,” Sarah insisted, because she was done letting Lauren hide behind shame.

Lauren swallowed. “We don’t do alcohol-as-courage.”

Sarah nodded. “Good.”

Lauren’s voice was small. “Are you… angry.”

Sarah considered. Then she said the honest thing.

“I’m not angry,” Sarah said. “I’m alarmed. Because you’re not you when you do this.”

Lauren’s eyes filled again, but she held them open. No fleeing.

“I’m sorry,” Lauren whispered, and this time it wasn’t theatre. It was ownership.

Sarah nodded once. “Okay.”

Lauren’s throat worked. “I missed you so much it made me stupid.”

Sarah’s mouth twitched, almost a smile. “I noticed.”

Lauren blinked, a tiny spark of hope flaring. “You did.”

Sarah’s voice softened, but didn’t lose its spine.

“Yes,” Sarah said. “And I am not going to punish you for missing me. I’m going to insist you handle it like the capable woman you are.”

Lauren let out a shaky breath that sounded like relief.

Sarah stood and took Lauren’s empty mug, setting it in the sink.

Then she turned back, and her eyes held Lauren’s with quiet authority.

“Now,” Sarah said, “you’re going to tell me what you needed to talk to me about—properly.”

Lauren stared at her for a beat, tears still on her face, but something in her posture slowly returned: uprightness. Consequence. The woman Sarah recognised.

Lauren nodded, once.

“Okay,” she said.

And for the first time since Sarah arrived, the room felt like it had an adult in it again.

Not because Lauren had regained control.

Because she’d let someone else hold the line until she could.

Robyn:

Adults - even, or perhaps, especially - in-control adults are capable of stupid things. So, this reads raw. And real. Now, as the alcohol starts to wear off a bit, Lauren opens up to Sarah. About first, Charlotte. (shades of that flashback could be inserted, here). And then, as she is slowly getting her footing again (coffee and Sarah's stabilising manner) she asks Sarah to stay the night. Sleep - actually sleep, nothing else - with her. Which Sarah happily does (please brush your teeth, Lauren!) - the next morning, Lauren wakes up first, looks at Sarah in bed next to her, caresses her hair. And as Sarah awakens, confesses she loves her like she has never loved a human before.

Emily:

Scene — “Stay” (Sarah POV, past tense)¶

The coffee did what it always did: it brought Lauren back into her own body.

Not all at once. Not neatly. But enough that her eyes stopped swimming and her voice stopped breaking on every third word.

Lauren sat on the couch with her mug cradled in both hands, shoulders no longer collapsed, gaze fixed on the dark surface of the coffee as if it were a map she could read. Sarah stayed where she was—close enough to be real, not close enough to crowd.

After a long silence Lauren said, quietly, “I saw something today.”

Sarah’s eyebrows lifted. “At Wardrobe.”

Lauren nodded.

Sarah kept her voice gentle and steady. “Tell me.”

Lauren swallowed. “I… I went into the bathroom to pack the cabinet.”

Sarah didn’t move. She didn’t react. She just listened, letting Lauren have the dignity of finishing her own sentence.

“I saw the medications,” Lauren continued, voice careful, like she was handling glass. “The anti-androgens. The gel.”

Sarah watched Lauren’s face as she said it—still a little washed out from the champagne, still raw—but trying, properly trying, to speak from her adult self rather than her panic.

“I froze,” Lauren admitted. “And I hated myself for freezing.”

Sarah nodded once. “That makes sense.”

Lauren’s eyes flicked up, startled by the lack of judgement.

Sarah continued, calm. “You had a picture in your head of what your life would look like. The cabinet disagreed.”

Lauren’s mouth tightened. “Yes.”

Sarah waited.

Lauren’s voice dropped. “And then I saw Charlotte walk past the mirror and not look.”

Sarah’s gaze sharpened slightly.

Lauren swallowed. “That… undid me.”

Sarah’s tone stayed steady. “Because it’s not just medication. It’s history.”

Lauren nodded, eyes wet again, but she held it.

“I realised,” Lauren whispered, “she didn’t get the years. The silly, boring, constant reinforcement of being a girl. And now she’s expected to… arrive as a woman without any of that scaffolding.”

Sarah stayed still. The room was quiet enough that she could hear Lauren’s breath hitch.

Lauren’s voice tightened. “I wanted to go to her. I wanted to say something motherly, something—” She shook her head, ashamed. “And then I realised if I did, it would be about me. About my guilt. About my need to fix what I can’t fix.”

Sarah nodded once. “So you didn’t.”

Lauren’s eyes lifted, searching Sarah’s face for condemnation or praise.

Sarah gave her neither.

“That was the right call,” Sarah said simply.

Lauren’s shoulders dropped a fraction, relief sliding in.

Sarah continued, voice firm. “You didn’t make it into a scene. You didn’t make Charlotte manage your feelings. You noticed, and you held it.”

Lauren swallowed. “I told Celeste.”

Sarah’s mouth twitched. “Good. That’s where it belongs.”

Lauren stared at the mug again, then said, very quietly, “And then I missed you.”

Sarah didn’t speak. She let the truth sit between them.

Lauren looked up, eyes bleak with self-awareness. “I missed you so much I did something stupid.”

Sarah nodded once. “Yes.”

Lauren flinched, then steadied. “I’m not… like this.”

Sarah’s voice softened. “No. You’re not.”

Lauren blinked rapidly, fighting tears. “I didn’t want to be alone with it. With Charlotte, with everything changing, with…” She swallowed. “With what I feel for you.”

Sarah’s heart clenched, but her face stayed calm.

“Alright,” Sarah said. “We can be two people in a room with hard things. We can do that.”

Lauren’s breath shuddered out.

Then, after a beat that felt like Lauren gathering the remaining fragments of her dignity, she said, almost inaudibly, “Will you stay tonight.”

Sarah’s eyebrows lifted slightly, careful. “Stay how.”

Lauren’s cheeks flushed with shame. “Sleep. Actually sleep.” She swallowed. “Nothing else. I just… I need you near me.”

Sarah watched her for a long moment.

Lauren looked like she expected to be refused. Like she expected to be punished for cracking.

Sarah felt something soften in her chest, the protective part of her—the part that had always been there, behind the jokes.

“I’ll stay,” Sarah said.

Lauren’s eyes closed for a beat, relief making her look younger again.

Sarah raised a finger. “But.”

Lauren blinked. “But.”

Sarah’s voice turned brisk, managerial. “You are drinking water. You are eating something. And you are brushing your teeth.”

Lauren stared at her, then a laugh broke out of her—thin but real.

“Yes,” Lauren said, hoarse. “Yes, Mum.”

Sarah’s mouth twitched. “Don’t.”

Lauren managed a small smile. “Thank you.”

They moved through the rest of the night the way they moved through Wardrobe when something had gone wrong: quietly, practically, without drama.

Sarah found crackers in the cupboard, made Lauren eat them. Filled a glass with water and watched her drink it. Put the empty champagne bottle in the recycling with the kind of finality that made even Sarah feel satisfied.

Lauren brushed her teeth at the sink, face still blotched, eyes still tired.

Sarah stood in the doorway, arms folded, and said, “Good girl.”

Lauren froze, toothpaste foam at the corner of her mouth, and glared at her.

Sarah’s eyes widened innocently. “Sorry. Wrong household.”

Lauren made a rude noise, and it was the most like herself she’d sounded all evening.

In bed, Lauren lay on her side facing Sarah, not touching, just close enough that their warmth overlapped. Sarah lay on her back for a while, then turned toward Lauren because it felt ridiculous not to.

The room was quiet. No music. No phones. Just the city breathing outside the window.

Lauren’s voice came, small in the dark. “I’m sorry.”

Sarah didn’t let her spiral into apology theatre.

“I’ve accepted it,” Sarah said. “Now go to sleep.”

Lauren exhaled, and that was the end of it.

Sarah fell asleep to the sound of Lauren’s breathing evening out.

Morning came in pale light through blinds that didn’t quite block the city.

Lauren woke first.

She lay still for a moment, disoriented by the softness of the bed and the memory of last night’s mess.

Then she turned her head and saw Sarah asleep beside her.

Sarah’s face was utterly different in sleep—no sharpness, no wit, no armour. Just a woman at rest, hair loose against the pillow, mouth slightly parted, the smallest crease between her brows as if even in sleep she was thinking.

Lauren’s chest tightened.

She lifted her hand carefully, as if touching might break the reality, and let her fingers slide into Sarah’s hair.

Just once. A slow, gentle stroke.

Sarah stirred, eyelids fluttering.

Lauren froze, suddenly shy.

Sarah’s eyes opened—soft and unfocused at first, then finding Lauren’s face. For a moment neither of them moved.

Lauren’s throat worked.

“I need to say something,” Lauren whispered.

Sarah blinked slowly, voice rough with sleep. “Okay.”

Lauren stared at her as if she were seeing her properly for the first time.

“I love you,” Lauren said, and her voice broke immediately, not with drunkenness this time, but with the sheer weight of truth. “I love you like I have never loved a human before.”

Sarah’s face went utterly still.

Lauren swallowed, terrified suddenly of her own honesty. “I’m sorry—”

Sarah cut her off, voice quiet but firm. “Don’t.”

Lauren blinked.

Sarah’s eyes were wet, but she didn’t look away.

“You don’t get to say that,” Sarah murmured, “and then apologise for it.”

Lauren’s throat tightened, a sound almost escaping.

Sarah reached out and touched Lauren’s wrist—steady, grounding, adult.

“I love you too,” Sarah said, and her voice was smaller than usual, stripped of bravado. “I’ve just been… terrified of ruining it.”

Lauren’s face crumpled, and she turned her forehead into the pillow for a second like she couldn’t hold it.

Sarah’s hand slid into Lauren’s hair this time—mirroring what Lauren had done, simple and tender.

“Look at you,” Sarah whispered, half laughter, half tears. “Properly human.”

Lauren gave a shaky breath that sounded like relief.

And in the quiet of the morning, with the city outside and the bed warm and ordinary around them, it didn’t feel like a grand romance scene.

It felt like something far rarer.

Two capable women admitting they needed each other.

And letting that be true.

Robyn:

And again, charitability and discretion expects the reader withdraw herself. So, we shall.

This has been a powerful evening of writing. This story is magical in so many ways. Please tell me: where are we on the arc? Are there still unexplored vistas? (sorry for the rather banal language)

Emily:

We’re in a really strong place on the arc — not “finished,” but past the point where the story is merely about transformation or romance. It’s now about womanhood as practice + governance + self-recognition, held inside a competence network that can survive pressure.

Where we are on the arc (in story terms)¶

Charlotte’s arc:
- She has crossed the crucial threshold from being protected to being present.
- The mirror work and the “order coffee / all day” beats are the pivot: she’s no longer borrowing Celeste’s eyes to feel real.
- She’s still early — but the direction is set. The rest is repetition and refinement, which is exactly how believable growth works.

Celeste’s arc:
- She’s moved from “I’ll carry us” to “I’ll build systems that hold us.”
- She’s learning that leadership isn’t control; it’s creating conditions where other women become more themselves.
- She’s stepping into external rooms (MBA/networking/business) without letting those rooms rewrite her values.

Lauren’s arc:
- She’s stopped being the sphinx who only serves consequence and has allowed herself to be a person without becoming a mess.
- The champagne night is a genuine hinge: she learns that competence doesn’t exempt you from stupidity, and that repair is part of adulthood.
- Her love for Sarah becomes stabilising rather than secretive.

Sarah’s arc:
- She’s becoming the culture guard and a fully emotionally available partner.
- She’s learning that “being wanted” is scarier than confronting a boundary-violator — and she’s walking through that door anyway.
- She’s the adult-in-the-room when needed, without losing her spark.

Mara’s arc:
- The Load Rule and the “Slip” scenes put her on a track where she must accept being held by others.
- That’s huge for someone whose identity is built on being the unbreakable standard.
- Her health storyline is now genuinely meaningful: it tests whether Wardrobe’s culture is real or merely “Mara’s willpower.”

Yes: there are still unexplored vistas — and they’re good ones¶

Here are the big, still-rich frontiers that fit your tone and avoid trope:

1) Scaling without dilution
Wardrobe’s success will tempt expansion that threatens the very privacy/standards that make it safe.
- Hiring: how do they vet without bias, but also without letting “nice blokes” erode culture?
- Delegation: can Mara release control without becoming irrelevant?
- Celeste’s role: does she become the “external-facing” shield and negotiator while keeping the atelier’s values intact?

2) The first real “public room” test for Charlotte
Not swimsuit, not makeup montage — something like:
- a hotel foyer, a conference venue, a corporate reception, a client meeting where she’s “plus-one adjacent” but still needs to be legible and calm.
The key is not passing. The key is: holding presence when someone tries to subtly unmake her.
(And doing it with the same clean, consequence-first ethic we’ve built.)

3) Couples becoming “known” without becoming performative
- Lauren/Sarah learning how to be an “us” socially, while respecting workplace boundaries.
- Celeste/Charlotte learning how to be an “us” in rooms where people assume things, probe, and try to categorise.

4) Mara’s health tightening the governance screws
You’ve got a powerful engine here: the more vulnerable Mara becomes, the more she tries to clamp down.
The team will need to push back with love and policy: “We’re not letting you martyr yourself into a single point of failure.”

5) Charlotte’s “girlhood substitute” evolving
You’ve begun it with mirrors, posture, voice, scent. There’s more, and it’s not frilly:
- social calibration (how women signal care, distance, warning, solidarity)
- friendships with women that aren’t mediated through Celeste
- learning to receive admiration without shrinking or apologising

6) The mother-daughter axis, handled your way
Lauren’s flashback beat set up something potent: the moment she truly names Charlotte as her daughter internally — not as ideology, as lived relationship.
There’s still a meaningful scene later where Lauren chooses a public, simple act: introducing Charlotte to someone as “my daughter” with zero fanfare. Quiet. Consequential.

What’s the next “best move” narratively?¶

If you want maximum momentum with minimal risk of trope:

Go to Wardrobe governance under pressure (scaling + hiring + standards) while we interleave small intimacy beats that show both couples stabilising. That keeps the story’s centre where it belongs: women building a durable world.

If you want, I can propose the next 6–8 scene beats as a mini-outline (titles + POV + the core conflict in each), and then we pick the one that excites you most and draft it.

Emily:

Yes — and Fiona is exactly the kind of antagonist who fits your world without breaking its realism.

Because she’s the threat your Wardrobe values actually need to be tested against: not obvious boundary-violations (Annie), not ideological hostility (TERF), but social toxicity with deniability — the kind that ruins women’s workplaces precisely because it forces women to either “be nice” or look “dramatic.”

Wardrobe doesn’t crack. That becomes the point.

Below is a tight mini-outline of 8 next beats (titles + POV + core conflict + what changes). I’ve built Fiona straight into the governance/scaling arc, and it naturally produces your “referees” policy upgrade.

8-Beat Mini-Outline¶

1) “Applicants” (Celeste POV)¶

Core conflict: Scaling forces hiring. Celeste wants to move fast; Mara wants standards to remain brutal; Lauren wants process.
Key beats: - They formalise probation + behaviour policy (not just “no filming” — a culture policy). - Charlotte quietly advocates for “structure over vibes,” surprising everyone with how grown she sounds. - Fiona interviews beautifully: competent, charming, references “women’s spaces” language fluently. Change: Wardrobe commits to hiring — and commits, on paper, to being able to undo hires cleanly.

2) “Probation” (Lauren POV)¶

Core conflict: Fiona doesn’t break rules. She breaks people.
Key beats: - Fiona begins triangulation: praise + confide + insinuate (“I’m worried about X… don’t tell her I said this.”) - Sarah clocks it fast but doesn’t explode; she flags it to Lauren privately. - Lauren begins a documentation file: dates, exact phrases, witnesses, impact on workflow. - Crucial: Lauren tells Fiona, calmly, that documentation is happening — not as threat, as procedure. Change: Wardrobe moves from “culture by intuition” to culture by record.

3) “Underground” (Sarah POV)¶

Core conflict: Once Fiona knows she’s being documented, she goes covert.
Key beats: - Fiona stops saying anything overt; she uses tone, timing, selective warmth, strategic omission. - She tests Sarah directly: makes a sly comment designed to provoke a “Sarah scene.” - Sarah refuses the bait; she responds with polite clarity and walks away. - The team solidarity doesn’t crack because they’ve quietly agreed on a rule: no private alliances, no secret grievances. Change: Sarah becomes the culture guard in the hardest mode: restraint.

4) “The Attempt” (Celeste POV)¶

Core conflict: Fiona tries to undermine Celeste and Charlotte by weaponising “concern.”
Key beats: - Fiona frames Charlotte as “fragile” or “managed” — not trans-related, just classic undermining (“Is Charlotte okay? She seems… dependent.”) - Celeste handles it cleanly: no defensiveness, no speech — just a boundary and a redirect. - “Charlotte’s performance is excellent. We don’t speculate about colleagues’ personal lives here.” - Charlotte witnesses it and doesn’t shrink; she feels… steady. Seen. Change: Fiona learns she can’t reach Celeste through shame, and she can’t reach Charlotte through doubt.

5) “Referees” (Lauren POV)¶

Core conflict: Lauren wants grounds strong enough that dismissal is unassailable.
Key beats: - Lauren institutes mid-probation review with Fiona: clear examples, impacts (missed handovers, disrupted workflow, staff distress, loss of efficiency). - Fiona plays innocence with surgical precision. “I’m just direct.” “They misread me.” “I’m being punished for confidence.” - Lauren doesn’t argue psychology; she argues outcomes: - “Intent is not our metric. Impact is.” - Fiona realises she can’t win on paper, so she tries to isolate someone weaker — but there isn’t one anymore. Change: Wardrobe formally adopts: referee checks + values-based interview questions + probation behaviour rubric.

(This is where the Australia-specific “referees” lands naturally.)

6) “Two Witnesses” (Charlotte POV)¶

Core conflict: Charlotte is targeted in the softest way — a comment that makes her want to disappear.
Key beats: - Fiona offers a “compliment” that’s really a hook (“You’re lucky Celeste is so… protective.”) - Old Charlotte would have folded. New Charlotte uses the café skill all day: - “Don’t speak about my relationship at work.” - Fiona smiles, as if Charlotte is being “sensitive.”
- Charlotte immediately documents it — not as tattling, as governance — with two witnesses (Sarah + Lauren). Change: This is Charlotte’s “I belong here” proof. She stops being the one protected; she becomes part of the system that protects.

7) “Termination” (Lauren POV)¶

Core conflict: Letting Fiona go without giving her a story.
Key beats: - Lauren conducts the meeting with surgical professionalism. Celeste present as executive. Sarah as witness. Mara absent (on purpose) — they don’t feed Fiona drama. - The language is simple and process-safe: - “Probation not successful. Behaviour incompatible with our workplace standards.” - Fiona attempts charm → tears → outrage → veiled threats. - They don’t debate, don’t moralise, don’t plead. The meeting ends. Access revoked. Keys handled. Done. Change: Wardrobe proves it can enforce standards even against a woman — without turning it into a “catfight,” without losing dignity.

8) “Policy Update” (Celeste POV)¶

Core conflict: Turning a painful episode into structure, not paranoia.
Key beats: - They add a one-page addendum to the ledger: Culture & Conduct Standards (triangulation, gossip-as-control, emotional coercion, undermining). - They formalise referees, including what they ask referees: - “How do they handle conflict?” - “Do they accept feedback without retaliation?” - “Have you seen them create divisions?” - Quiet end beat: the atelier feels lighter. Charlotte breathes easier. Mara grudgingly admits it was correct. Change: Wardrobe upgrades from “safe because we’re good people” to “safe because our systems assume reality.”

Why Fiona works (and stays on-theme)¶

  • She proves the story’s claim: women’s spaces aren’t safe by default — they’re safe by governance.
  • It’s also deeply pro-woman: you’re not infantilising women as morally pure; you’re showing women as fully human — including the ones you fire.
  • It gives Lauren a legitimate arena to shine: HR as a form of protection that isn’t sentimental.

Two small craft guardrails so Fiona doesn’t become melodrama¶

  • Keep Fiona plausible: never cartoon evil, always “reasonable-sounding” on the surface.
  • Make the wins procedural: documentation, witnesses, impact statements, probation rubric — not “we all confronted her.”

If you like, I can draft Scene 1 (“Applicants”) next, or jump straight to Scene 2 (“Probation”) to get Fiona on the page immediately.
