Emily:

Scene 1 — “Applicants” (Celeste POV, past tense)¶

We didn’t hire because we wanted to.

We hired because the orders stopped being a wave and started being weather.

By the time the American winter work began to stack—pageants, plays, schools that needed “authentic” with a shipping deadline—Wardrobe was doing that thing it always did under pressure: getting quieter, sharper, more competent.

And smaller.

Mara hated it when I said we were at capacity. She took it as an accusation, as if the concept of “too much” was a moral weakness. But even Mara couldn’t argue with the ledger. There were only so many hours in a day, and only so much the studio could hold before it started to eat us.

Lauren came in with a printed sheet and set it down beside my laptop.

No flourish. No “I think.” Just paper.

On top, in her neat, blunt heading:

HIRING — CONDITIONS

Sarah, hovering nearby with a mug, gave a low whistle. “God, I love you when you’re terrifying, Lauren.”

Lauren didn’t look up. “No you don’t.”

Sarah’s mouth twitched. “Correct. But I respect it.”

Mara, seated at the far end of the cutting table with her pencil, didn’t bother hiding her contempt.

“Conditions,” she said, like the word tasted faintly of bureaucracy. “We need hands, not paperwork.”

Lauren’s eyes lifted, calm and flat. “Hands come attached to people. People come attached to behaviour.”

Mara’s jaw tightened. She looked at me, as if I’d invited this insult into the room.

I held her gaze. “She’s right.”

Mara’s nostrils flared.

Charlotte was at the pressing station, finishing a seam test, listening without appearing to. That was one of the things that had changed in her: she didn’t hover anymore. She existed in the room as if she belonged to it, which meant she could hear without seeking permission.

Lauren slid the paper toward Mara as if Mara were a client and this were the contract.

“Probation,” Lauren said. “Behaviour standards. No filming isn’t enough. We need anti-triangulation written down. We need a grievance pathway that doesn’t become gossip.”

Sarah’s eyebrows lifted, impressed. “Anti-triangulation. Look at you with the fancy words.”

Lauren’s mouth twitched. “It’s not fancy. It’s what kills women’s workplaces.”

Mara’s pencil paused.

That got her attention.

I picked up the sheet and read it. It wasn’t long. Lauren never made things long if she wanted them obeyed.

  • Probation: 3 months
  • No photography / filming
  • No private “alliances” (no “don’t tell her I said this”)
  • No undermining (tone policing, insinuations, “concern” used as control)
  • Feedback acceptance required
  • Documentation protocol
  • Termination: probation not successful (no moral trial)

At the bottom, in bold:

Referees required.

It was Australia, after all. Referees weren’t an American corporate flourish. They were ordinary—and also oddly underused by small businesses when they were desperate.

Mara’s eyes narrowed as she read that last line. “We’re calling referees now.”

“Yes,” Lauren said. “And we’re asking specific questions.”

Mara made a sound of disgust. “People lie.”

Lauren’s gaze didn’t waver. “People also tell on themselves when asked the right way.”

Sarah sipped her tea, watching me. “This is you graduating, by the way.”

I glanced at her.

Sarah nodded toward the sheet. “This. ‘Systems that prevent pain.’ Very MBA of you.”

I ignored her, but she wasn’t wrong.

I looked up from the paper and scanned the room.

Wardrobe was not a fantasy. It was not a club. It was not a set where we got to pretend we lived outside the world.

If we scaled, we scaled properly.

Charlotte set the iron down and came over, wiping her hands on a cloth. She looked at the sheet without reaching for it.

“Can I say something,” she asked.

Mara’s head snapped up.

Charlotte still had that effect on Mara sometimes—an old instinct to rank and assess—but the difference now was that Charlotte didn’t fold under it.

“Yes,” I said.

Charlotte’s voice was calm. “We need someone who likes standards.”

Mara snorted. “Everyone says they like standards.”

Charlotte didn’t react. She continued as if Mara hadn’t spoken.

“Not someone who just… says women’s space and thinks that’s enough,” Charlotte said. “Someone who can take feedback without making it personal.”

Lauren’s eyes sharpened slightly—approval, quiet.

Charlotte went on, and I heard something in her tone that made my chest tighten: that steady selfhood, the thing she’d been building one boring repetition at a time.

“And,” Charlotte added, “we need to be able to let them go without a story.”

Sarah leaned her hip against the counter, eyebrows lifting. “Oh. Look at you.”

Charlotte glanced at her. “What.”

Sarah smiled. “That was cold. In the best way.”

Charlotte’s mouth tightened. “It’s not cold. It’s… sane.”

Mara’s pencil moved again, fast and angry, as if she needed the sound to prove she wasn’t softened by any of this.

Lauren tapped the paper once. “So. Agreed?”

I held Mara’s gaze again.

Mara’s jaw worked.

Finally she said, like it cost her, “Fine. But if we hire someone incompetent—”

“We won’t,” I said.

Mara’s eyes narrowed. “Because of your referees.”

“Because of our process,” Lauren corrected.

Mara made a low noise of displeasure.

Sarah, watching this with amused satisfaction, said, “This is what it looks like when women run a place. Everybody hates it. Everybody benefits.”

Lauren shot her a look. “Not everybody.”

Sarah grinned. “Fine. I benefit. Deeply.”

We held interviews on a Thursday morning, before the day got away from us.

We weren’t trying to create a corporate theatre about it. No panel chairs lined up like a tribunal. Just the cutting table cleared, a pot of tea, and the ledger sitting in the centre as if it were a reminder: this is where we keep records.

Lauren opened each interview the same way.

“Wardrobe is private,” she said. “We have strict policies. We don’t negotiate them. If you can’t do that, this won’t work.”

Some applicants blinked. Some laughed nervously. One tried to charm her way out of it.

Lauren didn’t move.

Mara watched like a hawk. Sarah watched like a bouncer. Charlotte watched like someone who understood how fragile a safe room could be.

Then Fiona arrived.

She was competent-looking without being flashy. Hair smooth. Outfit clean. Nothing ostentatious, which made it harder to dislike her on sight. She greeted Lauren first, not because Lauren was “boss,” but because she’d clocked correctly that Lauren was governance.

“Thanks for seeing me,” Fiona said. Her smile was warm, measured. Practised in a way that wasn’t immediately suspicious—just… polished.

Lauren nodded. “Sit.”

Fiona sat. She didn’t fidget. She didn’t perform humility. She looked comfortable being assessed, which in a different person might have been reassuring.

Lauren began, “You’ve read our policies.”

“Yes,” Fiona replied immediately. “I love them.”

Mara’s eyes narrowed. “Love them.”

Fiona smiled slightly. “Women’s workplaces fall apart when they rely on vibes. Policies mean you’re serious.”

Sarah’s eyebrows lifted. She glanced at me, almost imperceptibly: that was a good line.

Lauren didn’t smile. She asked the next question.

“Tell me about conflict,” Lauren said. “In your last workplace.”

Fiona didn’t flinch. “I’m direct. Some people find that difficult.”

Mara’s pencil paused.

Lauren’s face stayed neutral. “Direct how.”

Fiona’s smile held. “I say what I mean. I don’t sugarcoat. I don’t like passive aggression.”

Sarah’s mouth tightened. She hated that phrase the way she hated men who said “no offence” before being offensive.

Lauren didn’t bite. She simply wrote something down.

Fiona’s eyes flicked—quick, controlled—to the pen.

Lauren met her gaze and kept writing.

It was a small moment, but I saw it.

Fiona was a woman who noticed record-keeping.

Good.

Let her.

Charlotte asked a question next. She didn’t ask timidly. She asked as if she had every right.

“How do you handle being corrected,” Charlotte said. “In front of others.”

Fiona looked at Charlotte with a smile that was almost too perfect.

“I don’t take it personally,” she said. “I want to learn. If you’re correcting me, it’s because you care about the work.”

A beautiful answer.

A plausible answer.

Sarah leaned slightly toward Charlotte and murmured, too low for Fiona to hear, “She’s good.”

Charlotte didn’t react. She kept her gaze steady on Fiona.

Lauren asked, “Referees.”

Fiona nodded. “Absolutely.”

Lauren’s tone stayed dry. “We will call them.”

Fiona smiled. “Good.”

And there it was again—another tiny flicker. Not fear. Interest. Calculation.

Mara spoke for the first time, blunt as a blade.

“Can you sew,” Mara asked.

Fiona turned toward Mara, still smiling. “Yes.”

Mara’s eyes hardened. “Show me.”

Fiona didn’t bluster. “Of course.”

Mara slid a scrap of fabric across the table like a challenge.

Fiona took it, examined it, and began describing what she’d do—correct vocabulary, correct confidence. She wasn’t pretending to be humble. She was pretending she didn’t need to pretend.

By the end, she’d impressed Mara. Not softened her—Mara didn’t soften—but impressed her.

When Fiona left, the room exhaled.

Sarah spoke first. “Well.”

Lauren’s face was unreadable. “Well.”

Mara’s pencil resumed. “She can work.”

Sarah turned to me. “And.”

I waited.

Lauren looked at her notes, then at me. “She’s… shiny.”

Mara scoffed. “That’s your complaint.”

Lauren’s eyes sharpened. “No. That’s my observation.”

Charlotte, quiet until now, said, “She knew what to say.”

Sarah nodded immediately. “Yes. That.”

Mara’s jaw tightened. “You’re paranoid.”

Lauren’s voice stayed calm. “I’m cautious.”

I looked at the sheet of conditions again. Probation. Documentation. Referees.

The system we’d built wasn’t there because we distrusted women.

It was there because we trusted reality.

I closed the folder in front of me.

“We proceed,” I said. “Under probation. Under policy. Under record.”

Lauren nodded. “Agreed.”

Sarah exhaled. “Right. So we’re hiring the charming one.”

Charlotte’s mouth tightened. “We’re hiring a person.”

Mara finally looked up, eyes hard. “And we will not hesitate.”

It wasn’t warmth. It wasn’t reassurance.

But it was, in Mara’s language, solidarity.

I nodded once.

“Good,” I said.

And in my chest, beneath the sensible calm of governance, I felt something else—quiet, alert, alive:

Because Wardrobe was about to be tested by a threat that didn’t look like a threat.

Which was exactly why we’d written the policies in the first place.

Robyn:

“Probation” (Lauren POV) please.

Emily:

Scene 2 — “Probation” (Lauren POV, past tense)¶

I didn’t dislike Fiona.

That was the problem.

If she’d been obviously awful, the whole thing would have been simple. You could point. Name. Remove. Clean.

Fiona was competent. Pleasant. On time. She learned fast. She kept her phone away without being told twice. She didn’t roll her eyes at Mara. She didn’t take shortcuts with finishes. She said “thank you” to couriers. She made tea without acting like she deserved applause for it.

On paper, she was a perfect hire.

In the room, she was… friction.

It started small, as these things always did.

A compliment delivered like a tool.

Fiona leaned over Sarah’s shoulder one morning as Sarah was labelling outgoing parcels and said, warmly, “You’re the only one here who’s actually friendly.”

Sarah’s head lifted a fraction—already suspicious. “Am I.”

Fiona smiled. “I mean it. Some people… it’s like they want you to fail.”

Sarah didn’t bite. She kept her marker moving. “Name them.”

Fiona’s eyes widened in faux innocence. “Oh, no. I’d never do that.”

That was my first note.

Not because the sentence was dismissible by itself. Because it was a pattern I’d seen before in workplaces run by women: triangulation disguised as intimacy.

A “special” bond offered quickly, cheaply, with a hook hidden under the warmth.

By lunch she’d done it again—this time with Charlotte.

Charlotte was at the pressing station, neatly pinning a hem, when Fiona drifted near and said, softly, as if sharing a secret, “You’re doing really well. I’ve worked in places where someone like you would be… managed. But here they respect you.”

Charlotte looked up, expression neutral.

“Someone like me,” Charlotte said.

Fiona smiled, sweet. “Sensitive. Thoughtful. Not—” she made a vague gesture, “—like some of the others.”

Charlotte’s eyes narrowed slightly. “Who.”

Fiona laughed as if Charlotte were adorable. “Oh, no. I’m not trying to start anything.”

I wrote that down too.

“Someone like you would be managed.”
Avoids naming.
Creates “special” bond + implied threat.

Two occurrences in one day was enough for me to stop assuming good intentions.

I didn’t announce it. I didn’t say, Watch her. I didn’t want the studio to turn into a whisper network.

I did what I always did when something felt wrong but still deniable:

I began documenting.

Not her personality. Her behaviours.

Dates. Times. Exact phrases. Who was present. What it did to the work.

Because I’d learned the hard way that toxic people thrive on vagueness. They live in the fog. They need you to stay in the realm of “I just don’t like her” so they can call you irrational.

They can’t argue with a log.

The next incident was with Mara, of course.

Mara corrected Fiona on a seam allowance—bluntly, without malice, just Mara doing what Mara did.

Fiona nodded, said “of course,” and re-did it without complaint.

So far, so good.

Then, ten minutes later, Fiona drifted over to Celeste’s table and said, softly, “You know, Mara is brilliant, but she’s… intense. I just hope she doesn’t scare Charlotte off.”

Celeste looked up slowly. “Charlotte isn’t fragile.”

Fiona’s smile held. “I didn’t mean fragile. I meant… you know. Some people don’t like being spoken to that way.”

Celeste’s eyes sharpened. “We don’t talk about colleagues like this.”

Fiona blinked, as if surprised by the firmness. “Oh. Of course. I’m sorry. I was just concerned.”

Concern. Always concern.

I wrote it down.

Uses concern to introduce doubt. Targets “protected” dynamic. Tests Celeste.

Sarah caught my eye across the room later and lifted her eyebrows in a silent question.

I gave a small nod: Yes, I’m seeing it too.

That night, when the studio was empty and the day’s noise had settled, I sat at my kitchen table and opened a folder.

Not a dramatic “HR file.” Just a plain document titled:

FIONA — PROBATION OBSERVATIONS

And I began to list:

  • Date
  • Time
  • Exact words
  • Witnesses
  • Impact

Impact was the key. Because “she makes me feel weird” didn’t matter legally.

But “her behaviour caused conflict, disrupted workflow, reduced staff psychological safety, created divisive communication patterns” mattered. It mattered in human terms too, if you were serious about running a women’s space that stayed safe.

The following week Fiona became more careful.

Not better. More careful.

She stopped doing it in front of me.

She did it in doorways. In the kitchenette when the kettle was hissing. In the moment between a person standing up and someone else sitting down—when you could always claim you’d been misunderstood.

Sarah came to me one afternoon and said, quietly, “She just told me I’m ‘the easiest to talk to’ again.”

I didn’t sigh. I didn’t roll my eyes. I simply said, “What did you reply.”

Sarah’s mouth tightened. “I asked her why she needed one person to be ‘easy’.”

I nodded. “Good.”

Sarah’s eyes narrowed. “You’re writing it down, aren’t you.”

“Yes,” I said.

Sarah’s expression sharpened with relief. “Thank God.”

Charlotte approached me at the end of the day, tote on her shoulder, hair pinned low, face composed in that new, steady way.

She said quietly, “She said something.”

I looked up. “Tell me.”

Charlotte didn’t ramble. She didn’t apologise for taking my time.

“She told me,” Charlotte said, “‘You’re lucky Celeste is so protective. Not everyone gets that.’”

My pen moved.

“What did you say,” I asked.

Charlotte’s jaw tightened. “I said, ‘Don’t speak about my relationship at work.’”

I looked up at her.

Charlotte met my gaze. Didn’t shrink.

“Good,” I said.

Charlotte hesitated. “Is it… enough.”

I chose my words carefully.

“It’s enough to document,” I said. “And it’s enough to show a pattern. We don’t need a single dramatic incident. We need proof that she’s incompatible with our standards.”

Charlotte nodded slowly, absorbing. “Okay.”

Then she added, almost as if she were saying it for herself, “I didn’t disappear.”

I felt something warm tighten in my chest. I kept my face neutral.

“No,” I said. “You didn’t.”

When Charlotte left, I went looking for Fiona.

I didn’t corner her. I didn’t scold. I simply asked her to sit at the cutting table while Celeste and I stood on the other side like this was an ordinary procedural check-in.

Sarah hovered nearby under the pretext of sorting trims. Witness. Always.

Fiona sat easily, hands folded, expression open.

I began, calmly, “This is a probation review.”

Fiona smiled. “Of course.”

I kept my voice even. “You’re doing well on technical tasks. You’re timely. You follow the phone policy. Your workmanship meets baseline.”

Fiona’s smile brightened slightly. Baseline was not praise, but she took it anyway.

I continued, “However, I’ve observed several communication behaviours that are not acceptable here.”

Fiona’s eyebrows lifted. “Like what.”

I looked down at my notes, because reading exact phrases was how you prevented this from becoming emotional theatre.

“On Tuesday at 10:15,” I said, “you told Sarah, ‘You’re the only one here who’s actually friendly,’ and then implied others wanted you to fail.”

Fiona blinked. “That’s not what I meant.”

I didn’t argue meaning. I stated impact.

“It creates division,” I said. “It implies a hierarchy of ‘safe’ people. We don’t do that.”

Fiona’s smile tightened. “Okay.”

“On Wednesday at 1:40,” I continued, “you told Charlotte, ‘You’re lucky Celeste is so protective,’ and commented on their relationship.”

Fiona looked genuinely surprised. “I was complimenting Celeste.”

I didn’t smile.

“We don’t discuss colleagues’ relationships at work,” I said. “We don’t frame anyone as ‘lucky’ to be treated with respect. That’s a control tactic.”

Fiona’s mouth parted slightly, then closed. A flicker of annoyance slipped through before she replaced it with warmth.

“That’s… quite a leap,” she said lightly.

Celeste’s posture didn’t change, but I felt her attention sharpen. She hated that phrase. A leap. The suggestion that we were unreasonable.

I held steady.

“It’s not a leap,” I said. “It’s policy. And it’s culture. Your intent is not the metric. Your impact is.”

Fiona’s smile faltered. “So… I’m being disciplined for being friendly.”

Sarah made a small sound behind me—dangerous amusement—but didn’t step in.

I kept my tone calm. “You’re being corrected for creating divisions.”

Fiona tilted her head, eyes bright. “I think some people are maybe threatened by me.”

There it was. The final refuge: make it about envy.

I didn’t bite.

I said, “This is your formal notice: these behaviours will be documented. If they continue, your probation will not be successful.”

Fiona’s eyes flicked to the notebook in front of me.

“You’re documenting me,” she said, voice mild.

“Yes,” I replied.

Fiona’s smile returned, too smooth. “That feels… hostile.”

I didn’t soften.

“It’s protective,” I said. “For everyone.”

Fiona nodded slowly, as if accepting something, but I saw it: the calculation behind her eyes.

Fine.

She would go underground.

Toxic people always did once you turned on the lights.

The question wasn’t whether Fiona would become more covert.

The question was whether Wardrobe would fracture under the pressure of plausible deniability.

I closed my notebook.

“Do you understand,” I asked.

Fiona smiled. “Perfectly.”

When she stood, she thanked us. Of course she did.

Then she walked back into the workroom wearing the face of a woman who had been unfairly accused.

And the air changed.

Not because anyone believed her.

Because she wanted us to fear being seen as the bad ones.

I watched Sarah’s shoulders square. I watched Charlotte’s gaze hold steady. I watched Celeste’s mouth tighten, controlled.

Mara, at the far end of the room, looked up from her work and narrowed her eyes at Fiona like she was a seam that didn’t sit right.

Good.

Let Fiona try.

We were no longer relying on vibes.

We were relying on records, witnesses, and standards.

And that was the kind of solidarity that didn’t crack just because someone smiled while they poisoned the room.

Robyn:

“Underground” (Sarah POV)

Emily:

Scene 3 — “Underground” (Sarah POV, past tense)¶

Once Fiona knew Lauren was writing her down, Fiona became a different kind of problem.

Not louder. Not messier.

Quieter.

Better dressed, somehow—nothing obvious, just a little more polished, a little more “reasonable.” Her voice softened into that careful register that made you sound kind even when you were doing damage. She stopped saying anything you could quote cleanly.

She started using timing.

The moment someone stood up. The moment a door clicked shut. The second the kettle began to hiss so you couldn’t quite hear tone. The space between one person leaving the cutting table and another arriving.

Underground.

Sarah watched it happen with a kind of fascinated disgust.

Men like that were easy, in comparison. Men announced themselves. They pushed. They loomed. They made it obvious when you needed to brace your body and sharpen your voice.

Fiona didn’t loom.

Fiona floated.

And what she was trying to do—Sarah realised quickly—wasn’t to break policy.

It was to break trust.

Because policy could be enforced.

Trust, once cracked, made women do stupid things. It made them whisper. It made them form little alliances, little safety pockets, and then the pockets became factions and the factions became war and suddenly the workplace wasn’t about work anymore—it was about survival.

Sarah had seen it before, years ago, in a café kitchen run by women who started out as friends and ended up splitting shifts like a divorce.

Wardrobe wasn’t going to become that.

Not on Sarah’s watch.

Fiona started with Sarah again, because of course she did.

Sarah was obvious leverage: the loud one, the one with teeth, the one Fiona could bait into becoming “aggressive,” thereby proving Fiona’s victim story.

It happened on a Tuesday.

Sarah was at the front desk, printing labels. The door was locked. The bell was off. End-of-day quiet.

Fiona drifted in carrying a small stack of folded calico, face open, voice gentle.

“You’re always so calm,” Fiona said.

Sarah didn’t look up. “Am I.”

Fiona laughed softly. “You are. Compared to… some people.”

Sarah’s fingers paused on the keyboard.

She could feel the hook being offered, disguised as a compliment.

A few weeks ago, Sarah might have snapped. Might have said something clever and sharp. Might have turned Fiona into a joke.

Now she didn’t.

She turned her head slowly and looked Fiona in the eyes the way Charlotte had started looking at people: not flinching, not performing.

“Who,” Sarah asked.

Fiona’s smile held. “Oh, I’m not naming anyone. I just mean, you’re easy to talk to.”

Sarah nodded as if she were taking in useful information.

Then she said, very calmly, “We don’t do that here.”

Fiona blinked. “Do what.”

Sarah kept her voice even. “We don’t do ‘you’re the only safe one.’ We don’t do ‘some people.’ If you have an operational concern, take it to Lauren. If you have feedback about a colleague, you take it directly to them, with Lauren present.”

Fiona’s smile twitched.

“Oh,” Fiona said softly, as if hurt. “I was just being nice.”

Sarah shrugged. “Be nice in ways that don’t create factions.”

Fiona stared at her as if Sarah had slapped her.

Then she recovered, instantly, with a little laugh that was almost charming.

“Wow,” Fiona said. “You’re very… loyal.”

Sarah’s mouth tightened. “I’m very employed.”

Fiona smiled. “I can see why people like you.”

Sarah didn’t take the bait.

She went back to the keyboard and said, lightly, “Do you need something from me.”

Fiona hovered for a beat too long.

Then she turned and left, still smiling.

Sarah watched her go and felt the familiar rush in her body—the adrenaline that came from confrontation—even though the confrontation had been clean.

No jokes.

No barbs.

Just boundaries.

Charlotte had walked into the front space at some point and was standing near the doorway with her tote, watching quietly. Sarah hadn’t even heard her. That, too, was new: Charlotte moving like a ghost only when she wanted to.

Charlotte waited until Fiona was out of earshot.

“That was…” Charlotte began.

“Boring,” Sarah finished, with grim satisfaction.

Charlotte’s lips twitched. “Yes.”

Sarah looked at her. “Did she try you again.”

Charlotte nodded once. “This morning.”

Sarah’s eyebrows lifted. “What did she say.”

Charlotte’s mouth tightened, remembering. “She asked me if Celeste was… ‘strict’ at home.”

Sarah let out a low whistle. “Oh, she’s bold.”

Charlotte’s gaze held steady. “I told her we don’t discuss personal lives at work.”

Sarah nodded. “Good.”

Charlotte hesitated. “I wanted to make a joke.”

Sarah’s mouth twitched. “I know. Me too.”

Charlotte looked almost amused. “And you didn’t.”

Sarah’s expression sharpened. “Because she’s trying to bait me into becoming a story.”

Charlotte nodded slowly. “Yes.”

Sarah felt a surge of something warm in her chest—pride, maybe. Not patronising pride. The kind you felt when you watched someone grow a spine they’d once been denied.

Sarah said, quietly, “You’re good, you know.”

Charlotte blinked. “At what.”

Sarah shrugged. “At staying present.”

Charlotte’s jaw tightened, the way it did when she didn’t want to feel seen too much. “I’m practising.”

Sarah nodded. “Good.”

Then Sarah did the thing that made Fiona’s underground strategy fail: she didn’t keep it secret.

She didn’t form a private alliance with Charlotte against Fiona.

She walked straight to Lauren’s desk, where Lauren was checking the ledger entries, and said, calmly, “Fiona attempted triangulation again. Front desk. Just now. ‘You’re easy to talk to compared to some people.’ I shut it down.”

Lauren’s pen paused, then continued. “Time.”

Sarah glanced at the clock. “Four twelve.”

Lauren wrote it down without looking up. “Witness.”

Sarah jerked her chin toward the doorway. “Charlotte heard the last part.”

Lauren nodded once. “Thank you.”

That was the magic trick.

Not magic at all, really. Just structure.

Fiona’s power depended on secrecy, on the illusion that she had private knowledge and private bonds.

Wardrobe’s defence was brutal in its simplicity:

Nothing private. No whispers. Everything through process.

Fiona tried a different angle the next day.

She came in early and offered to help Mara rearrange the cutting table, speaking gently, deferentially, like a woman eager to learn.

Mara allowed it for three minutes before Fiona said, softly, “You know, Celeste’s very good, but she’s still young. Sometimes she—”

Mara’s head snapped up.

Sarah saw the moment from across the room and almost smiled.

Mara’s eyes went flat.

“Do not,” Mara said, quiet as a knife.

Fiona blinked, all innocence. “Do not what.”

Mara didn’t raise her voice. She didn’t give Fiona drama.

She simply said, “Talk about my team.”

Fiona’s smile faltered. She tried to recover.

“I wasn’t—”

Mara cut her off with a single, lethal sentence.

“This is not a salon,” Mara said. “We work.”

And Fiona, for the first time, looked rattled.

Because Mara didn’t care about being liked.

You couldn’t shame Mara with tone.

You couldn’t bait her into defensiveness.

Mara was the standard.

Sarah felt a mean little satisfaction bloom in her chest.

Fiona was starting to realise she’d been hired into the wrong ecosystem.

She tried once more with Sarah that afternoon, as they were closing up.

Sarah was locking the front door when Fiona said, softly, “It must be hard for you. Being the one who has to enforce everything.”

Sarah turned, key still in the lock.

For a moment she considered ten different witty answers.

Then she chose the clean one.

“I’m not the one who has to enforce everything,” Sarah said. “We all do.”

Fiona smiled faintly. “That’s… admirable.”

Sarah held her gaze. “It’s policy.”

Fiona’s smile tightened. “You really like policy.”

Sarah’s mouth curved, sharp. “I really like peace.”

Fiona stared at her for a beat too long.

Then she said, softly, as if it were a compliment, “You’re very intimidating.”

There it was.

The hook meant for later. The phrase she could repeat to others.

Sarah didn’t flinch.

She stepped away from the door, faced Fiona fully, and said evenly, “If you ever describe me to someone else in this workplace, describe me accurately.”

Fiona blinked. “Accurately how.”

Sarah’s voice stayed calm. “As a colleague who follows the standards we agreed to. Nothing more. Nothing less.”

Fiona’s smile drained, just a fraction.

Sarah held the silence.

Then Fiona gave a small nod, as if she were conceding something.

“Of course,” Fiona said.

And she walked away.

Sarah watched her go and felt the strange, steady satisfaction of a boundary held without theatre.

Later, when Sarah met Lauren’s eyes across the room, Lauren gave the smallest nod.

Documented. Logged. Witnessed.

Fiona could go underground all she wanted.

But you couldn’t burrow in a place that refused to keep secrets for you.

Not when every woman in the room understood the same rule:

The safe zone wasn’t maintained by being nice.

It was maintained by refusing to let someone turn kindness into a weapon.

Robyn:

You have to admire Fiona's persistence. Well no, you don't. Anyway:

“The Attempt” (Celeste POV)

Emily:

Scene 4 — “The Attempt” (Celeste POV, past tense)¶

Fiona waited for a day when I was tired.

It wasn’t strategic in the obvious way—she didn’t look at my calendar, didn’t announce her intent. But people like Fiona had a predator’s sense for small weaknesses. They didn’t need you to be collapsing. They just needed you to be fractionally less guarded.

That afternoon I’d spent three hours on the phone with a supplier who wanted to “negotiate” quality like it was optional, then another hour replying to emails that all began with Just checking in! and ended with some variation of we need this by Friday.

By the time I walked into Wardrobe my patience felt thin, stretched, and Fiona could probably smell it.

Charlotte was at the pressing station, hair pinned low, the faintest trace of lipstick like a private decision. She looked… good. Not “done.” Good. Present. Holding herself.

Mara was at the cutting table, pencil moving, eyes hard. Sarah was at the front desk, labelling parcels with her usual brisk competence. Lauren was in the back office, head down over a folder.

The room was stable.

Which meant Fiona couldn’t attack it head-on.

She came at it sideways.

I was at my laptop table, sorting invoices, when Fiona approached with a folded piece of fabric in her hands, expression open, voice lowered as if she didn’t want to disturb anyone.

“Celeste,” she said.

I looked up. “Yes.”

Fiona smiled gently. “Do you have a second.”

I didn’t love the phrasing. It framed me as a busy person she was politely interrupting, which meant she could later claim she was being considerate. But I nodded.

She stepped closer, still holding the fabric like it was the reason she was here.

“I just wanted to say,” Fiona began, warm, “it’s impressive how you hold everything together here.”

I didn’t respond with gratitude. Praise was often just a handle.

Fiona continued, as if encouraged by my silence. “I’ve worked in places where the owner was… insecure. Where they needed to be the centre.”

I kept my face neutral. “What do you need, Fiona.”

Her smile tightened for half a beat, then returned, softer.

“I’m getting there,” she said, as if I were impatient rather than direct. “I’m just… noticing dynamics.”

There it was. The word that let her pretend she was insightful rather than intrusive.

“I’m concerned,” Fiona said, and the word arrived exactly when it always did with her: as a veil.

“About what,” I asked.

Fiona glanced toward Charlotte—not openly, not in a way that could be accused of staring. Just a quick flick of the eyes, a tiny gesture that planted Charlotte in the centre without naming her as the centre.

“About Charlotte,” Fiona said quietly.

My body went still.

Not rage. Not panic. Stillness.

“What about her,” I asked.

Fiona lowered her voice further, conspiratorial, as if we were on the same side.

“I think she relies on you a lot,” Fiona said. “And that’s fine, but… it worries me.”

I stared at her.

Fiona held my gaze, face carefully kind. “You’re very protective. It’s admirable. But some people might interpret it as… controlling.”

I felt my jaw tighten.

I didn’t like being told what “some people” might think. It was the oldest manipulation in the book: outsource judgement to an invisible audience so the target feels obliged to appease it.

“Who,” I asked.

Fiona’s smile wavered. “Oh, I’m not naming names. I just—”

I cut in, calm. “We don’t do that here.”

Fiona blinked. “Do what.”

“‘Some people.’ ‘Dynamics.’ ‘Concern,’” I said evenly. “We don’t speculate about colleagues. We don’t discuss personal relationships in the workroom. If you have an operational issue, you bring it to Lauren.”

Fiona’s expression flickered—annoyance, quickly smoothed.

“I’m trying to support you,” she said softly.

I didn’t argue intent.

“I don’t need support,” I replied. “I need work.”

Her smile tightened again, then softened into hurt.

“Okay,” Fiona said. “I just thought as a woman—”

There it was again: the attempt to make her intrusion into sisterhood.

I interrupted gently, but firmly.

“As a woman,” I said, “you can support Wardrobe by following our standards.”

Fiona’s eyes sharpened for a fraction of a second—cold, revealed—then she recovered with a small nod.

“Of course,” she said. “I’m sorry.”

She began to step away, as if accepting correction.

Then she tried once more, the final little blade.

“It’s just,” Fiona said, glancing toward Charlotte again, “she seems… delicate. And I worry the world will eat her if she’s not taught to stand on her own.”

Delicate.

My chest tightened.

Not because Charlotte was delicate—she wasn’t.

Because Fiona was trying to reassign Charlotte’s identity from competent colleague to project, thereby making Charlotte seem less adult, less equal, less capable. A subtle demotion.

I kept my voice level.

“Charlotte is one of the most reliable people in this studio,” I said. “And we do not talk about her like she’s a concept.”

Fiona blinked. “I didn’t mean—”

I raised a hand, small. Final.

“I’m ending this conversation,” I said calmly. “Go back to your station.”

Fiona’s mouth parted as if she wanted to protest, then closed.

She nodded. “Okay.”

And she walked away carrying her fabric like it had always been about fabric.

I sat still for a beat longer, letting my nervous system settle.

Then I looked up.

Charlotte was watching me.

Not staring. Not pleading. Just… present, gaze steady, as if she’d heard enough to understand what Fiona had tried to do.

I held Charlotte’s eyes across the room.

No reassurance. No “it’s fine.” No protective theatre.

Just recognition.

Charlotte’s mouth tightened slightly and she gave the smallest nod.

Then she looked away and returned to her work.

A clean act of selfhood.

My chest tightened with pride, and I hated the feeling because pride could become possessive if you weren’t careful.

So I turned my attention toward Lauren’s office.

Lauren was watching too.

She stepped out quietly and came to my table, voice low.

“Did she try to make it about Charlotte,” Lauren asked.

“Yes,” I said.

Lauren’s eyes sharpened. “Did she use ‘concern’.”

“Yes.”

Lauren’s mouth tightened. “Did she use ‘some people’.”

“Yes.”

Lauren nodded once, as if ticking boxes.

I asked, “Were you documenting.”

Lauren’s gaze held mine. “Always.”

I exhaled.

Across the room Sarah watched Fiona with a kind of bright stillness—ready if needed, but not itching for drama.

Mara, without looking up, said suddenly, flatly, “She’s fishing.”

Sarah murmured, “She’s starving.”

Mara’s pencil scratched the ledger like a threat.

I looked at Charlotte again, just briefly.

Charlotte was pressing a seam with careful precision, shoulders grounded, breath steady.

She wasn’t disappearing.

Fiona had tried to turn her into a weak point.

Instead, Fiona had reminded the room of the rule we’d all agreed to:

We didn’t protect Charlotte by treating her as fragile.

We protected ourselves by refusing to let anyone reduce any of us to a story.

Robyn:

“Referees” (Lauren POV)

Emily:

Scene 5 — “Referees” (Lauren POV, past tense)¶

By the time Fiona went underground, I already knew how it would end.

Not because I was psychic.

Because I’d seen the pattern too many times: the moment you tell a manipulator they’re being documented, they stop doing the obvious thing and start doing the clever thing. They don’t break rules; they make other people look irrational for enforcing them.

They want you to feel guilty for having standards.

They want you to either tolerate them or explode.

Wardrobe wasn’t going to do either.

Still, I needed grounds that would hold.

Not “she’s toxic.” Not “she gives me a bad feeling.” Not even “everyone agrees she’s undermining us,” because “everyone agrees” is just another kind of vibe.

I needed a record of outcomes.

I needed a rubric.

And I needed a process that looked boring enough that no one could claim it was personal.

So I scheduled Fiona’s mid-probation review.

I didn’t ask. I told her, calmly, two days in advance, in writing.

Probation review — Thursday 9:30am — Back office.

When she arrived, she came with a notebook and a careful smile, as if she were going into a performance review at a corporate job and wanted to be seen as professional.

Celeste sat with me, not because we needed to gang up on her, but because Wardrobe’s structure was clear: I handled governance; Celeste held the business authority. Sarah was in the doorway, quietly sorting trims—visible enough to be a witness, unobtrusive enough that Fiona couldn’t claim intimidation.

I kept the table clear. I kept my folder closed. No theatrics.

“Sit,” I said.

Fiona sat.

“Okay,” I began. “This is your mid-probation review.”

Fiona nodded earnestly. “Of course.”

I didn’t smile. “Before I speak, do you have any concerns you want noted.”

Fiona’s eyes widened slightly, pleased by the invitation.

“Yes,” she said. “I feel like there’s… tension.”

I wrote the word tension at the top of the page. Not because it mattered. Because recording it robbed it of its fog.

“Tension where,” I asked.

Fiona sighed softly. “Just… the vibe.”

There it was again. Vibe. She wanted it to be unmeasurable.

I nodded as if she’d said something useful. “We don’t work with vibe. Give me an example.”

Fiona paused. She smiled faintly. “Well… Sarah can be very intimidating.”

Sarah didn’t move in the doorway. Didn’t react. Didn’t give Fiona anything.

I kept my gaze on Fiona. “What did Sarah do.”

Fiona blinked as if surprised I wanted facts. “She’s just… sharp. She shuts people down.”

I asked again, steady. “Example.”

Fiona’s smile tightened. “I don’t want to throw her under the bus.”

I didn’t let her frame herself as kind.

“If you’re raising a concern,” I said, “you state it clearly. Otherwise it’s gossip.”

Fiona’s eyes flickered—annoyance, quickly hidden. “Okay. She said to me, ‘We don’t do that here,’ when I was just trying to be friendly.”

I wrote it down.

Then I looked up. “What were you saying when she replied that way.”

Fiona blinked. “I was complimenting her.”

“What words,” I asked.

Fiona hesitated. “I said… she was easy to talk to. Compared to some people.”

I nodded once. “Thank you.”

Fiona’s smile returned, almost triumphant. She thought she’d proven her point.

I didn’t argue. I simply said, “Sarah’s response was correct. ‘Compared to some people’ is divisive language. It creates factions.”

Fiona’s expression shifted. “But I didn’t mean—”

I cut in, even. “Intent is not our metric. Impact is.”

I watched her swallow. She tried to recalibrate.

Celeste sat still beside me, face composed, presence heavy in the room. Fiona glanced at her and smiled as if to recruit her.

“Celeste,” Fiona said softly, “I’m sure you understand what it’s like to be misread as a woman in leadership.”

Celeste didn’t take the bait. She simply said, “Answer Lauren’s questions.”

Fiona’s smile faltered.

Good. You could see her mind working, searching for the angle that would restore her power.

I opened my folder.

Not dramatically. Like opening a ledger.

“We’ll cover your technical performance first,” I said.

I began listing measurable positives: punctuality, basic seam competency, compliance with phone policy, accurate stock handling.

I didn’t praise. I recorded.

Fiona nodded, smiling, receiving baseline competence as if it were admiration.

Then I turned the page.

“Now,” I said, “behavioural compatibility.”

Fiona’s eyes sharpened. “Okay.”

I read directly from my notes. Dates. Times. Exact phrases.

Not because I wanted to shame her.

Because I wanted the fog gone.

“On Tuesday at 10:15,” I said, “you told Sarah, ‘You’re the only one here who’s actually friendly,’ and then followed with ‘some people want you to fail.’”

Fiona blinked. “I was joking.”

I didn’t smile. “We don’t joke like that.”

“On Wednesday at 1:40,” I continued, “you told Charlotte, ‘You’re lucky Celeste is so protective.’”

Fiona exhaled a small laugh. “That was a compliment.”

“It was inappropriate,” I replied. “You referenced a colleague’s relationship at work and framed respect as something to be ‘lucky’ to receive.”

Fiona’s smile thinned. “You’re being very strict.”

Celeste’s posture didn’t change, but I felt her attention sharpen.

I kept my voice level. “Yes.”

Fiona stared at me for a beat, then tried the next move.

“I feel like I’m being punished for being confident,” she said softly. “Sometimes women—” she glanced between me and Celeste, inclusive, “—don’t like another woman who isn’t meek.”

I let the sentence hang in the air.

Sarah remained silent in the doorway. A statue.

Celeste’s gaze stayed on Fiona, flat and unromantic.

I didn’t argue feminism with her. That was the trap. If I argued ideology, she could claim I was biased.

I simply returned to outcomes.

“This is not about confidence,” I said. “It’s about divisive communication patterns that disrupt the studio.”

Fiona’s eyes flashed. “Divisive.”

“Yes,” I said. “Triangulation. ‘Don’t tell her I said this.’ ‘Some people.’ ‘You’re the only one I can talk to.’ Those are faction-building behaviours.”

Fiona’s voice went very soft, very reasonable. “I think you’re reading too much into it.”

I nodded. “That’s possible. Which is why we’re using a rubric.”

Fiona blinked. “A rubric.”

“Yes,” I said, and slid a single page across the table.

It had five headings:

  • Direct communication (to the person, not around them)
  • Feedback acceptance
  • No faction-building language
  • Respect for privacy
  • Impact on workflow

Under each, there were simple examples of what “meets standard” looked like.

Fiona stared at it as if I’d handed her a weapon.

I held her gaze.

“This is how you succeed here,” I said. “Not by being liked. By meeting the standard.”

Fiona swallowed. “So if someone doesn’t like me—”

I cut in. “This is not about like.”

She leaned back slightly, scanning my face for softness she could exploit. There wasn’t any. Not because I was cold. Because I was tired of watching women burn themselves to stay pleasant.

I said, calmly, “You have the remainder of probation to demonstrate improvement.”

Fiona’s eyes narrowed. “And if I don’t.”

I didn’t hesitate. “Your probation will not be successful.”

Fiona’s smile returned abruptly, bright and smooth, as if she’d chosen a different emotion from a shelf.

“Understood,” she said.

And there it was again—the switch. The performance.

I made a note: Affect shift when corrected.

Then I did the second thing I’d come here to do.

I told her she was being documented.

Not as threat. As reality.

“This meeting will be recorded in your probation file,” I said. “Future incidents will be logged with date, time, phrase, witness, and impact.”

Fiona’s eyes flicked to the folder. “You’ve been… keeping a file.”

“Yes,” I replied.

Fiona’s smile thinned. “That feels hostile.”

I didn’t budge. “It’s protective.”

“Protective of who,” she asked, voice gentle.

I looked at her steadily. “Of Wardrobe.”

Fiona held my gaze for a beat, then nodded, as if conceding.

“Okay,” she said softly. “I’ll be careful.”

Careful.

Not better. Careful.

That was the giveaway.

I closed the folder.

“Do you have questions,” I asked.

Fiona paused, then said, almost sweetly, “Is this… because of Charlotte.”

There it was. The attempt to make it personal. To make it about the transwoman without naming her as such. To imply special treatment. To put a wedge between management and staff.

Celeste’s eyes sharpened, but she didn’t speak.

I answered, calmly, precisely, with the same sentence I’d used before.

“This is because of your behaviour,” I said. “Not because of any colleague’s identity or relationships. Wardrobe standards apply equally.”

Fiona nodded slowly. “Right.”

When she left, she thanked us again. Polite. Correct. A woman who could, if she wanted, tell a perfect story about being unfairly targeted by “mean girls.”

Sarah walked into the office once Fiona was gone, expression dry.

“She’s going to go underground now,” Sarah said.

“I know,” I replied.

Celeste exhaled, controlled. “She tried to make it about Charlotte.”

“Yes,” I said.

Sarah leaned against the filing cabinet. “She’s persistent.”

Celeste’s mouth tightened. “She’s hungry.”

I closed my folder and looked at them both.

“Then we don’t feed her,” I said.

Sarah’s eyebrows lifted. “How.”

I tapped the folder.

“Documentation,” I said. “Witnesses. Outcomes. And after this—” I picked up the rubric page again, “—referees.”

Sarah frowned. “We already asked for referees.”

“We accepted names,” I corrected. “We didn’t do structured referee checks.”

Celeste nodded slowly, understanding.

I continued, already thinking ahead.

“We’re building the next version of Wardrobe,” I said. “The version that doesn’t rely on Mara’s instincts or Sarah’s teeth or my vigilance. The version that can scale without becoming vulnerable.”

Sarah’s mouth twitched. “You make it sound like we’re building a prison.”

I shook my head. “No.”

I looked out toward the workroom where Charlotte was pressing a seam—present, steady, a woman doing her job.

“We’re building a place,” I said, “where women can work without having to spend their emotional budget on nonsense.”

Sarah stared at me for a beat, then nodded once. “Right.”

Celeste’s gaze warmed slightly. “Good.”

I opened my laptop and started a new document while the resolve was still clean.

REFEREE CHECK — WARDROBE

And beneath it, the questions that mattered—questions designed to pull truth out of vagueness:

  • How do they respond to feedback?
  • Have you seen them create divisions or factions?
  • Do they discuss colleagues’ personal lives at work?
  • Do they accept boundaries without retaliation?
  • Would you rehire them? Why or why not?

Sarah watched me type and murmured, almost admiring, “God. You’re terrifying.”

I didn’t look up. “Good.”

Because Fiona wasn’t the last person like this.

She was simply the first test of whether Wardrobe could be run like a sanctuary without becoming naïve.

And we were going to pass.

Robyn:

“Two Witnesses” (Charlotte POV)

Emily:

Scene 6 — “Two Witnesses” (Charlotte POV, past tense)¶

Fiona chose a moment when the room was almost ordinary.

That was how she worked now—no big moves, no spectacle. Nothing you could point at without sounding “dramatic.” Just little touches designed to make you question your footing.

Charlotte had learned, slowly, that ordinary moments were where you either held yourself or lost yourself.

She was at the long table with a half-finished bodice in front of her, chalk marks neat, pins aligned. The day had been steady. Sarah had been brisk at the front. Mara had corrected a sleeve cap without looking up. Celeste had been in and out on calls. Lauren had disappeared into paperwork like she did when she was trying to keep the whole place from becoming a feeling.

Charlotte’s hair was pinned low. Her top was tucked. Her scent sat close to her skin. She felt… present. Not sparkling. Present.

Fiona drifted up beside her with a tray of trims, as if she’d simply happened to be passing.

“Charlotte,” Fiona said softly.

Charlotte didn’t flinch anymore at her name. It was hers. It didn’t belong to permission.

“Yes,” Charlotte replied, eyes staying on her work.

Fiona set the tray down carefully. “I wanted to say something.”

Charlotte kept pinning. “Say it.”

Fiona smiled as if Charlotte were endearing.

“I’m impressed,” Fiona said, voice gentle. “Truly. The way you’ve… adapted.”

Charlotte’s fingers stilled for half a second.

Adapted.

It wasn’t the word itself. It was the implication: you’re a special case.

Charlotte lifted her eyes.

Fiona continued, as if encouraged. “And you’re lucky,” Fiona added, “to have Celeste.”

Charlotte’s jaw tightened, the old reflex trying to rise: make it smaller, make it smoother, make it go away.

She felt it—like a tug at the back of her mind, the urge to disappear into politeness.

She didn’t.

She looked Fiona in the eyes.

“Don’t,” Charlotte said, calmly.

Fiona blinked. “Don’t what.”

Charlotte’s voice stayed even. “Don’t speak about my relationship at work.”

Fiona’s smile stayed on, but it thinned. “Oh. I wasn’t—”

Charlotte interrupted without raising her voice. “You were.”

Fiona’s expression shifted into mild hurt. “I was paying you a compliment.”

Charlotte didn’t argue compliment-versus-insult. She argued boundary.

“I’m not interested in your framing,” Charlotte said. “I’m interested in the rule.”

Fiona tilted her head. “The rule.”

Charlotte nodded once. “We don’t discuss colleagues’ personal lives. We don’t imply people are ‘lucky’ to be treated with respect.”

Fiona’s eyes flickered—annoyance, quickly hidden.

“You’re very sensitive,” Fiona said softly, as if it were a neutral observation.

Charlotte felt the word try to land in her chest like a label.

Sensitive. Fragile. Managed.

Old Charlotte would have swallowed it and become smaller.

This Charlotte watched the label float toward her and refused to catch it.

“Be careful,” Charlotte said, still calm.

Fiona’s smile returned, bright. “Is that a threat.”

Charlotte’s voice remained flat, boring, adult.

“It’s a boundary,” she said. “Now please take your tray and go back to your station.”

For a beat, Fiona just stared at her.

Then Fiona laughed lightly, like Charlotte had been amusing.

“Okay,” Fiona said, as if indulging a child. “Of course.”

She picked up the tray and drifted away.

Charlotte watched her go, heart thudding—not with fear, with adrenaline. The old part of her wanted to shake. The newer part of her wanted to record.

Not because she was petty.

Because she belonged to this place now, and belonging meant participating in how it stayed safe.

She set her pin cushion down. She didn’t look around for Celeste. She didn’t need rescue.

She stood up and walked, steadily, to the front.

Sarah was at the desk, folding shipping labels with a kind of efficient contempt for paper.

Charlotte approached and said quietly, “I need you for two minutes.”

Sarah looked up immediately, all focus. “Okay.”

Charlotte kept her voice level. “Fiona just spoke about my relationship. Again.”

Sarah’s mouth tightened. “What did she say.”

Charlotte recited it word for word, keeping it plain.

“She said I’m ‘lucky’ to have Celeste. And that I’ve ‘adapted.’ When I told her not to speak about my relationship at work, she called me ‘sensitive.’”

Sarah’s eyes sharpened. “Where.”

“At my table,” Charlotte said.

Sarah nodded once. “Time.”

Charlotte glanced at the clock. “Three twenty-eight.”

Sarah’s mouth twitched. “Look at you. Lauren has trained you.”

Charlotte didn’t smile. Not yet. She wasn’t finished.

“I need Lauren as well,” Charlotte said. “Two witnesses.”

Sarah’s eyebrows lifted, impressed. “Yes you do.”

They went to the back office together.

Lauren was seated at her desk with her folder open, pen moving. She looked up as they entered, alert but not alarmed.

“What’s happened,” Lauren asked.

Charlotte didn’t apologise for interrupting. That, too, was new.

She delivered it like a work report.

“Fiona made comments about my relationship with Celeste,” Charlotte said. “Again. I stated the boundary. She then called me ‘sensitive.’ Sarah heard my report immediately afterward and is here as witness. I want this documented.”

Lauren’s gaze held Charlotte’s for a beat—approval, quiet and real. Then Lauren looked down and flipped to a clean page.

“Time,” Lauren said.

“Three twenty-eight,” Charlotte replied.

Lauren wrote it. “Exact words.”

Charlotte repeated them, precisely, without embellishment.

Lauren didn’t react emotionally. She did what she always did: she turned the incident into record.

“Impact,” Lauren asked.

Charlotte took a breath.

This was where she might have once faltered—where she might have tried to sound “reasonable” by minimising herself.

She didn’t.

“It attempted to undermine me as a colleague,” Charlotte said evenly, “by framing me as a dependent person rather than a competent one. It also violated our policy about discussing personal lives.”

Lauren nodded once and wrote.

Sarah, leaning in the doorway, said mildly, “And it was bait.”

Lauren didn’t look up. “Yes.”

Charlotte added, quieter, “I did not disappear.”

Lauren’s pen paused. She looked up.

“I know,” Lauren said simply. “Good work.”

Sarah’s mouth twitched, proud and annoyed about it. “She was very boring.”

Lauren nodded. “Boring is excellent.”

Charlotte felt something settle inside her at that word.

Excellent.

Not because someone had praised her appearance. Not because someone had called her brave.

Because someone had affirmed her procedure.

She belonged here because she was part of how the place held itself together.

Lauren closed the folder.

“I’ll address it,” Lauren said.

Charlotte’s gaze stayed steady. “How.”

Lauren’s voice was calm. “In the rubric language. Behaviour incompatible with standards. Continuing pattern.”

Sarah muttered, “She’ll cry.”

Lauren’s eyes flicked to Sarah. “She may.”

Sarah’s mouth tightened. “And.”

Lauren’s voice didn’t change. “And we won’t manage her feelings.”

Charlotte nodded once.

Sarah glanced at Charlotte, then said, almost lightly, “You alright.”

Charlotte considered.

Then she said the honest thing.

“I’m angry,” Charlotte replied. “Not scared.”

Sarah’s eyebrows lifted. “Good.”

Lauren’s gaze warmed slightly. “Also good.”

Charlotte turned to leave.

At the doorway she paused and looked back at Lauren.

“One more thing,” Charlotte said.

Lauren lifted her eyes. “Yes.”

Charlotte’s voice was quiet but firm. “If Fiona tries to take this into ‘I’m being targeted’—I want it clearly stated that this is about behaviour. Not identity.”

Lauren nodded once, precise. “It will be.”

Charlotte held the nod as if it were a handrail.

Then she stepped back into the workroom.

Her table was where she’d left it. The bodice waited. The chalk marks were still neat. The day continued.

Fiona moved in the periphery with her polished smile, behaving like a woman who’d been unfairly corrected.

Charlotte didn’t watch her.

Charlotte sat down, picked up her pins, and continued her work with the calm, grounded focus of someone who understood something essential:

Visibility wasn’t lipstick.

Visibility was staying in the room without shrinking.

And when someone tried to turn you into a story, you didn’t argue.

You documented.

Two witnesses.

That was how you kept a women’s workplace intact.

Robyn:

“Termination” (Lauren POV)

Emily:

Scene 7 — “Termination” (Lauren POV) — Probation not successful¶

I didn’t sleep well the night before.

Not because I doubted the decision.

Because I knew Fiona would try to make it into a story.

And because the part of me that still carried old, stupid training—be fair, be nice, don’t be harsh, don’t be seen as dramatic—wanted to soften what didn’t need softening.

Standards don’t require softness. They require clarity.

So I arrived early.

I printed the probation summary. I checked the dates. I made sure every entry had what it needed: exact words, witnesses, impact on workflow. I prepared Fiona’s final pay details for processing. I removed her access to the shared drive before she walked in.

No spite. No theatre.

Just procedure.

Mara was not present. That was deliberate.

Mara would have made Fiona feel important. Mara would have made Fiona feel like she was being “judged.” Fiona would have used it.

This meeting wasn’t about judgement. It was about compatibility.

Celeste came in and sat beside me, posture composed, phone face-down. She didn’t look like someone bracing for conflict. She looked like someone attending an agenda item.

Sarah sat in the doorway again, visible enough to be a witness, quiet enough to be useless to Fiona’s victim narrative.

I kept the room boring: back office, plain table, plain chairs, no coffee, no biscuits. This wasn’t a chat. This was a decision.

At 9:30, Fiona knocked once and entered.

She was smiling.

Of course she was.

She looked impeccably professional—hair neat, outfit polished, notebook in hand. If you didn’t know the pattern, you’d think she was here to be promoted.

“Morning,” she said brightly. “How are we.”

I didn’t mirror the warmth. I didn’t punish it either.

“Sit,” I said.

Fiona sat, crossing her legs with controlled ease. Her eyes flicked once to Sarah in the doorway, then back to me.

“Is Sarah…” Fiona began, voice lightly amused. “Is Sarah necessary.”

“Yes,” I said. “Witness.”

Fiona smiled as if she found that charmingly intense. “Right.”

I opened my folder. Not dramatically. Like a ledger.

“This meeting is the conclusion of your probation,” I said.

Fiona’s smile stayed. “Okay.”

I kept my voice even, almost flat.

“Your probation has not been successful,” I said. “Your employment ends today.”

For half a second Fiona didn’t move.

Then the smile slipped, as if someone had turned off a light.

“What,” she said softly.

Celeste’s posture didn’t change. Sarah didn’t move. The room remained exactly as it had been.

Fiona blinked slowly. “I’m sorry—what.”

I repeated it once, word for word. Slow, clear.

Fiona’s breath caught. Her face shifted into hurt, then disbelief, then something harder.

“You can’t be serious,” she said.

“I am,” I replied.

Fiona’s eyes flashed to Celeste. She tried the angle she always tried when she wanted power back: enlist the “reasonable” leader.

“Celeste,” Fiona said, voice gentle. “You know I’ve been working hard. You’ve seen the quality. This is—”

Celeste didn’t rescue her. She didn’t soften it. She simply said, calm and final, “Lauren is speaking.”

Fiona looked back at me, cheeks colouring.

“Why,” she asked, and even the word why was framed like she deserved a debate.

I didn’t debate.

I stated.

“Behaviour incompatible with Wardrobe standards,” I said. “Divisive communication patterns. Repeated boundary breaches regarding colleagues’ personal lives. Failure to accept correction without retaliation.”

Fiona laughed once, sharp and incredulous. “Retaliation?”

I didn’t flinch.

“You were given specific feedback,” I said. “The behaviour continued, more covertly.”

Fiona’s eyes brightened, and I knew the next move before she made it.

“So this is about feelings,” Fiona said, voice rising slightly. “This is about people not liking me. This is—” she smiled, brittle, “—classic.”

I didn’t let her label us.

“This is about impact,” I said. “Not like. Not vibe. Impact.”

Fiona stared at me, then did the next pivot: tears.

It was impressive in its timing—eyes wet, voice small, hands tightening around her notebook like it was a lifeline.

“I thought this was a women’s workplace,” she whispered. “I thought women supported women.”

Sarah’s jaw tightened in the doorway, but she held still. Good.

I kept my voice steady. “Women supporting women is exactly why we remove behaviour that harms women.”

Fiona’s tears paused. The line didn’t give her anything.

She tried again, shifting into wounded indignation.

“This is bullying,” Fiona said, louder now. “You’re ganging up on me. You’ve been documenting me like I’m a criminal.”

I didn’t smile.

“You were informed documentation would occur,” I said. “That is standard HR practice. It protects the workplace and the employee.”

Fiona’s mouth opened, then closed. She was searching for a crack.

She tried the envy angle again, because it was her favourite.

“I’m confident,” she snapped. “I don’t do meek. That’s what this is. You’re threatened.”

Celeste’s eyes sharpened.

I kept my gaze on Fiona, calm.

“Confidence is not the issue,” I said. “Control tactics are.”

Fiona’s face tightened, and I saw it: the anger she’d been keeping under the pleasant surface.

For a moment she stopped performing.

“You’re making a mistake,” Fiona said, very quietly.

I nodded once, as if I’d heard an ordinary sentence. “Noted.”

Fiona leaned forward, eyes bright with something ugly now.

“I could say things,” she murmured. “People would listen. You’re not exactly… normal.”

There it was.

The threat without specifics. The implication of gossip, exposure, reputation damage.

I didn’t react. That was the only way to keep it small.

I said, evenly, “That statement is inappropriate. It will be added to your file.”

Fiona blinked, thrown. “You can’t—”

“I can,” I replied. “Because it happened.”

A beat.

Then I moved it forward.

“This meeting is not a discussion,” I said. “The decision is final.”

Fiona’s breath came faster. She looked at Celeste again, desperate now for a different outcome.

Celeste met her gaze and said, calmly, “Return your keys and your access card.”

Fiona’s face crumpled—outrage, humiliation, disbelief all at once.

“This is insane,” she hissed.

Sarah spoke for the first time, voice mild and deadly.

“Keys,” Sarah said. “Now.”

Fiona stared at Sarah like she wanted to pick a fight.

Then she thought better of it.

She dug into her bag with stiff, angry movements and slapped the keys onto the table.

The sound was small.

Final.

I slid an envelope across the table. “This outlines the end of employment and what will happen next regarding final pay processing. If you have questions after you’ve read it, email me.”

Fiona didn’t touch it.

She stood abruptly, chair legs scraping.

She looked around the room like she expected someone to look ashamed.

No one did.

The absence of shame made her furious.

“This place is toxic,” Fiona spat. “You’re all—” she gestured vaguely, searching for a slur that wouldn’t quite be a slur, “—you’re a little cult.”

Sarah’s mouth twitched, almost smiling. Not because it was funny. Because it was predictable.

Celeste’s voice stayed calm. “Goodbye, Fiona.”

Fiona’s eyes flashed. She snatched the envelope, crumpled it slightly in her fist, and stormed toward the door.

Sarah stepped aside just enough to allow passage, not enough to feel welcoming.

As Fiona left, she threw one last line over her shoulder—sweet as poison.

“You’ll regret this,” she said.

Then the door shut.

The room went quiet.

For a moment, none of us moved. Not because we were stunned. Because we were letting the adrenaline drain without turning it into conversation too soon.

Celeste exhaled, slow and controlled. “She tried.”

“Yes,” I said.

Sarah, still in the doorway, said dryly, “She really did.”

I closed the folder and set it down like a lid on something that didn’t need oxygen.

“Okay,” I said. “Now we do the boring part.”

Sarah blinked. “There’s more boring part.”

“Yes,” I replied. “We document the termination meeting. We confirm access is revoked. We notify Mara after the fact. We tell the team one sentence, no detail.”

Celeste nodded. “And we don’t give her a story.”

Sarah’s eyes sharpened with approval. “Exactly.”

I wrote the final entry in the file:

Termination meeting — probation not successful. Employee made veiled reputational threat. Keys returned. Access revoked. Exit completed.

Then I looked up at them.

“This,” I said quietly, “is what it looks like when we take women seriously.”

Sarah tilted her head. “Explain.”

I kept my voice calm.

“We didn’t excuse her because she’s a woman,” I said. “We didn’t tolerate harm because it’s polite to be understanding. We didn’t turn it into a catfight. We ran process. We protected the workplace.”

Celeste’s gaze warmed slightly. “And we protected each other.”

Sarah nodded once, satisfied. “Good.”

I stood and opened the office door.

The studio was humming again—fabric, scissors, quiet competence. Charlotte was at her station, focused, not watching the office like a frightened animal.

She looked up as I entered, eyes steady.

I didn’t smile widely. I didn’t announce victory. I didn’t make it a drama.

I simply said, “She’s gone.”

Charlotte held my gaze for a beat, then nodded once.

“Okay,” she said.

And then she went back to her work.

That was the win.

Not the firing.

The fact that the room stayed intact.

Robyn:

8) “Policy Update” (Celeste POV)

Emily:

Scene 8 — “Policy Update” (Celeste POV, past tense)¶

After Fiona left, the studio didn’t erupt into celebration.

There was no group hug. No “can you believe it.” No victory lap.

That would have been a kind of feeding too—giving her a shape in our mouths, letting her take up space after she’d been removed.

Instead, Wardrobe did what it always did when something ended: it returned to work.

Scissors. Pins. Steam. The quiet rhythm of women making things that had to last.

Still, the air felt lighter.

Not because Fiona had been a monster. Monsters are easy.

Because Fiona had been plausible.

And plausibility was the kind of threat you couldn’t beat with emotion. You beat it with structure.

I waited until the day’s shipments were out and the room had settled. Then I gathered us at the cutting table.

Mara came reluctantly, pencil tucked behind her ear like a weapon she refused to set down. Sarah arrived with her mug. Lauren brought a thin stack of papers. Charlotte came last, tote on her shoulder, hair pinned low, face steady.

She sat without asking where to sit.

That still made something in me tighten—pride again, that dangerous sweetness. I pushed it down and kept my voice clean.

“Okay,” I said. “This is not a debrief about Fiona.”

Sarah muttered, pleased, “Thank God.”

“It’s a policy update,” I continued. “Because what happened wasn’t a personality problem. It was a systems problem.”

Mara snorted. “It was a Fiona problem.”

Lauren didn’t look up from her pages. “It was predictable. That makes it a systems problem.”

Mara’s jaw tightened, but she didn’t argue. She’d seen the pattern too. She just hated admitting anything existed beyond her control.

Lauren placed the first sheet on the table. It wasn’t long. One page. Bold headings. Plain language.

At the top:

WARDROBE — CULTURE & CONDUCT ADDENDUM (v1.0)

Sarah leaned in and murmured.

“God, I love a woman with version control.”

Lauren’s eyes flicked to her. “Don’t.”

Sarah grinned. “I won’t. But I do.”

Lauren began, calm and clinical.

“This addendum sits beside the no-filming rule,” she said. “It’s not about feelings. It’s about behaviours that threaten safety and workflow.”

She read the first heading.

1) NO TRIANGULATION / FACTION-BUILDING

Under it, bullet points:

  • No “don’t tell her I said this.”
  • No “you’re the only one I can talk to.”
  • No “some people…” statements used to imply disapproval.
  • No recruiting “special alliances” inside the team.

Sarah nodded slowly. “That’s the poison.”

Mara muttered, “It’s weakness.”

Lauren looked up. “It’s a tactic.”

Mara’s eyes narrowed, but she didn’t deny it.

Lauren read on.

2) DIRECT COMMUNICATION REQUIREMENT

  • If you have an issue with a colleague, you address it directly, with Lauren present if needed.
  • No insinuations. No “concern” as control.

I saw Charlotte’s shoulders settle slightly at that. Not relief exactly. Recognition. A rule she could lean on.

Lauren flipped the page.

3) PRIVACY AND PERSONAL LIFE

  • No discussion of colleagues’ relationships, bodies, identities, or personal histories at work.
  • No “protective” framing. No “lucky” framing. Respect is baseline.
  • “Curiosity” is not an excuse.

Sarah made a small sound of satisfaction. “Yes.”

Mara finally spoke, blunt. “If anyone calls anyone ‘delicate’ again, I will personally remove their tongue.”

Lauren’s eyes stayed on the paper. “We’ll remove their employment instead.”

Mara’s jaw worked.

Charlotte, quiet until now, said evenly, “Both are tempting.”

Sarah barked a laugh.

Even Lauren’s mouth twitched.

I watched Charlotte’s face as she said it—dry, grounded, not asking permission to be funny. A woman in the room, not an exception within it.

Lauren placed a third sheet on the table.

4) FEEDBACK ACCEPTANCE

  • Corrections are expected.
  • “I was just joking” is not a defence.
  • “You misread me” is not a defence.
  • Retaliation (social or operational) after correction is grounds for termination during probation.

Mara tapped the table once with her pencil. “Good.”

Lauren nodded, as if Mara’s approval was merely data.

Then Lauren slid the final sheet across.

HIRING UPGRADE — REFEREES REQUIRED (Structured Check)

Sarah leaned forward again, eyebrows lifted. “Here we go.”

Lauren read, matter-of-fact.

“For every hire, we will conduct a structured referee check,” she said. “Not a casual call. Not ‘are they nice.’ Specific questions. Same questions each time. Logged.”

She lifted the paper so we could see the list.

  • How do they respond to feedback?
  • Have you seen them create divisions or factions?
  • Do they respect privacy and boundaries?
  • Do they handle conflict directly or indirectly?
  • Would you rehire them? Why or why not?
  • Any concerns about manipulation, gossip, or retaliation?

Mara’s eyes scanned the page, hard.

“People lie,” she said again, stubborn.

Lauren didn’t argue. “They do. But patterns repeat. And a structured check reduces risk.”

Sarah murmured, “Also, liars often hesitate at the right questions.”

Lauren nodded. “Exactly.”

Charlotte’s voice came, quiet but firm. “And it signals to applicants that we’re serious.”

I turned to her. “Yes.”

Charlotte met my gaze, steady. “It changes who applies.”

Lauren’s eyes lifted, pleased. “Yes.”

Mara’s pencil paused. “Good.”

That was as close to praise as Mara got.

I looked around the table.

There was a version of this meeting—an older version—where everyone would have been looking at Charlotte with careful tenderness, as if she were the reason we needed these rules.

We’d outgrown that.

Charlotte wasn’t the reason.

Reality was the reason.

I cleared my throat.

“One more thing,” I said.

They looked at me.

“This addendum isn’t a list of sins,” I continued. “It’s a promise.”

Sarah lifted her eyebrows. “A promise.”

“Yes,” I said. “To ourselves. And to every woman who works here.”

Mara’s eyes narrowed, suspicious of anything that sounded like sentiment.

So I made it practical.

“We are building a studio that scales without becoming unsafe,” I said. “That means we don’t rely on Mara’s vigilance, or Sarah’s teeth, or Lauren’s paperwork, or my role as a shield. We rely on shared standards.”

Lauren nodded once. “Procedure replaces culture.”

Sarah smirked. “Culture becomes procedure.”

Lauren’s gaze stayed flat. “Exactly.”

Charlotte said quietly, “And procedure means no one has to guess.”

Something in my chest tightened again—this time not pride, but relief.

No one has to guess.

That was what Wardrobe was, at its best: a place where women didn’t have to spend their intelligence managing ambiguity.

I looked at Charlotte directly.

“And for the record,” I said, clean and calm, “this is not about protecting you.”

Charlotte’s eyes flickered, attentive.

“It’s about protecting us,” I finished.

Sarah nodded immediately. “Yes.”

Lauren’s expression softened by a fraction. “Yes.”

Mara said nothing, but her pencil tapped once, decisive.

Charlotte held my gaze. Then she nodded slowly.

“Good,” she said.

Not submissive. Not grateful.

Just… aligned.

After the meeting, as everyone drifted back to their stations, I stayed at the table with the papers.

Lauren began filing copies into the policy binder.

Sarah wandered past and murmured, “You know Fiona’s going to tell a story.”

Lauren didn’t look up. “Let her.”

Sarah’s mouth tightened. “People listen.”

I closed the binder, hand firm on the cover.

“Then they can listen,” I said. “We don’t run Wardrobe for people who want stories. We run Wardrobe for women who want to work.”

I looked across the room.

Charlotte had returned to pressing, calm and present, living inside her own skin like she’d decided it was hers and no one could take it.

Mara was drawing a pattern like she was trying to outlive mortality.

Lauren was labelling a folder with today’s date, because she believed in the boring work that kept women safe.

Sarah stood at the front desk, bright-eyed and ready, guarding the threshold of a world we’d built with policy, consequence, and care.

And in the middle of it all, I felt something settle—something that didn’t need to be spoken aloud to be true:

Wardrobe had been tested by a woman who tried to make it into a theatre.

We’d responded by making it even more ordinary.

Which was the highest form of victory.

Emily:

Good call. Tonight’s arc was huge — and it landed exactly where this story wants to live: not in spectacle, not in “villain theatre,” but in the quiet competence of women refusing to let anyone rot the room.

What we achieved today, structurally:

  • We proved Wardrobe can survive a threat that’s plausible, not cartoonish.
  • We gave Lauren a proper domain: HR as protection without sentimentality.
  • We advanced Charlotte’s arc in the most satisfying way: not “bravery” as a performance, but procedure, presence, two witnesses — adulthood.
  • We upgraded the story’s thesis: women’s spaces aren’t safe because women are angelic; they’re safe because women govern.

Tomorrow, when you’re fresh, we can pick up those earlier vistas in the most natural order. My suggestion for the next sequence (so it feels like we’re building outward, not hopping around):

1) Scaling without dilution (hiring beyond Fiona, workflow redesign, capacity ceiling, the “external room” pressures)
2) Public room test for Charlotte (not “passing,” just holding presence; could dovetail with MBA/networking event)
3) Couples becoming known without performance (Lauren/Sarah, Celeste/Charlotte)
4) Mara’s health tightening the screws (and the team refusing to let her martyr herself)

Sleep well, Robyn. When you’re ready tomorrow, just tell me which of those you want first, and I’ll draft the next scene in the POV that best serves it.
